THE RED PAGE.
From a new book of verses called ‘ Realms of
Unknown Kings,’ by (Miss) Laurence Alma
Tadema (daughter of the painter ?)■ —
LITTLE GIRLS.
Nobody knows but you and I,
Just you and I and not one more.
They all imagine I’m a child,
And look at me with a grown-up eye,
Just as they did before ;
They none of them see that I never smiled
Or spoke as now I smile and speak :
Yet my very walk must show it!
And none but you and 1 may know it,
Not for a whole long week.
None ; yet do you, too, I wonder,
Peel that the birds know ? The grass?
All the flowers? the blue
Above? and the bough we walk under?
Look well at the sweet silent tilings as we pass.
That butterfly, he knew —
And the goldfinch there, perched up so tall
On the thistle . . . To them we may show it;
Yet nobody else in the world may know it
—Save God, for God knows all.
Here are the words of one of the songs the girls
have just finished singing—
See the Robbers passing by,
Passing by, passing by-
See the Robbers passing by l
My fair La-Ay !
What’s the robbers done to you,
Done to you, done to you ?
What's the Robbers done to you f
My fair La dy !
Stole my watch and stole my chain,
Stole my chain, stole my chain.
Stole my watch and stole my chain,
My fair La-dy !
Off to prison you must go,
You. must go, you must go,
Off to prison you must go l
My fair La-dy l
This is the fashion in which justice is meted out
in some of the child-rhymes. The Lady is haled
off to prison—acted in pantomime, as all the other
incidents are—for having been robbed of her own
watch and chain ! The mention of the latter orna-
ments might suggest that the rhyme had its origin
during the heyday of highwaymen. But the refer-
ence to highwaymen in the plural casts doubt on
this theory. Highwaymen rarely exercised their
profession in bands. Claude Duval worked singly;
so did Dick Turpin, except on rare occasions
when he had an extra large contract on hand.
Then his friend Tom King appeared on
the scene to render valuable assistance.
I am, for my own part, inclined to place the date
when the rhyme was produced as far back as the
period of the Robber Barons. The line
Stoic my watch and stole my chain
might easily be a modernised version of
Stole my rings and chatelaine.
* * * *
But this is a question not to be lightly settled
in a paragraph. Before singing the song given
below, the girls form a large ring, with spaces left
between each girl and her neighbor. In the
centre of the ring stands a girl by herself. This
girl passes out and in through the openings while
the following lines are being chanted :
In and out the window,
In and out the window,
In and out the window—
As you have done before.
The girl who takes the star-part stands in the
ring facing some other girl she has chosen as
partner. The song goes on—
Stand and face your loved one,
Stand and face your loved one,
Stand and face your loved one,
As you have done before.
Then comes —
Follow her to London,
Follow her to London,
Follow her to London,
As you have done before.
The heroine of the little lyric comedy then
marches off to London —situated a few yards
down the street —with her Loved One grasping
her by the skirts and all the rest of the players
following in single file. This small dramatic ditty,
so clearly picturing an elopement in the old style,
may not date back further than a couple of cen-
turies or so. “ Poor Alice weeping for her Sweet-
heart ” and tlie following are possibly of palaeo-
lithic antiquity:—
Here stands a lovely creature I
Who she is I do not know.
Will she answer, for her Beauty —
Will she ansv:er Yes, or No ?
No, she won’t have gold or silver,
No, she won’t have house or land,
21 To, she won’t have ships on the ocean—
All she wants is a nice young man.
Exactly. Talk about the Eternal Feminine !
The Everlasting He occupies the thoughts and
pervades the dreams of the Female of his kind
even in the nursery.
• * * *
And now for the last rhyme and the one I like
best of all—
Here come two Dukes a-riding,
A-ree, a-row, a-riding,
Here come two Dukes a-riding,
To greet your daughter Jane.
My daughter Jane is yet too young,
She has not learnt her mother tongue.
Be she young or be she old,
We her beauty must behold.
Go back, go back, you saucy Jack,
And clean your spurs more brightly.
My spurs are bright and richly wrought.
And in this town they can’t be bought,
And in this town they shan't be s old,
Neither for silver nor for gold.
Go through the kitchen and through the hall,
And choose the fairest one of all.
The fairest one that I can see
Is pretty Nelly. Come to me!
For ‘Nelly,’ read Mary, Annie, or any other
dissyllabic name. And Nelly, or Mary, or Annie
comes, of course, and rides away with the dukes
• —why there should be two dukes to one damsel
may seem strange, but that is how the song goes.
It is, evidently, as old as the days of chivalry,
when the gold was always red, and the ladies
always gay, and the love-affairs of anybody under
the rank of a knight were not thought worth
singing about.
* * * *
Anthropo-philoloKists (horrible coinage but
handy) of the Max Muller school would find in
the foregoing simple, natural ballad-poetry of
childhood, profound and complex meanings too
deep even for clear-obscure exposition—except in
German, t’ut, certainly, they would make of the
lovely creature of the second rhyme the Sanscrit
Aphrodite—t he Latin Venus Genetrix ; Nature,
in fact. And in the heroine of the third rhyme
quoted t hey would see as clearly as the spectacles
on their noses a personification of Ushas the
Vedie dawn-maiden, leading her flock of rosy
clouds over the mountains in the hush of the early
Aryan morning. The Robbers and the Lady, and
the Two Dukes and Daughter Jane, and her
Mother, would also be described by them as solar
myths of the most transparent character. But it
is some years since those antiquarian rag-and-bone
men—those grubbers in neolithic kitchen-middens
—the new school of archaeologists, crushed the
anthropo-philologists and their theories and buried
them beneath a huge heap of prehistoric skulls,
and bones, and flint weapons, and relics of broken
victuals, consumed and digested tens of thousands
of years before Adam ate the apple which the
Woman gave him for the confusion of all mankind,
* * » *
Shall we ever have an Australian child-litera-
ture of song and story that will supersede the old
nursery favorites with their Northern color and
Northern ideality ? Not for five hundred years, I
think. Not until the blackfellow, and the kanga-
roo, and the emu, and the Tasmanian devil, and
the squatter have become, as dim and legendary
and lost in the Grey Vague of Time as the Scan-
dinavian Trolds and the Celtic Fairies are at the
present day. A story that will please a man may
be written in a year or two, a story that will de-
light a woman may be composed in a month or
six weeks, but a story that a child will take to its
heart without question requires a thousand years
to grow to maturity. And, once grown, it out-
lasts dynasties, and Empires, and even languages.
- _ .—-
Edgar Jephson writes in English ‘ To-
Morrow ' —
The intrusive bicycle, substituting in either
case healthy action for unnatural stimulants, has
damaged the sale of patent pills and the literary
drug; has provoked a simultaneous movement
among the manufacturers of either, to make the
retailers sell their goods at net prices ; and lias set
before us more clearly than ever the similarity of
the two trades. This is, indeed, the age of patent
medicines ; and when Keats spoke of the world as
a place ‘ where but to think is to be full of sorrow,’
he was unwittingly pointing out to many worthy
and laborious persons a royal road to fortune and
renown. For, of late years, action, the old and
proper sedative of the malady of thought, has
been growing impossible to greater and greater
numbers of people: you cannot call quill-
driving or counier - jumping action: and the
literary drug is more and more taking its place.
This drug, which is rarely a drug in the market
and still more rarely literature, is of qualities and
prices to suit the different intellects and purses of
those who take it : the office-boy buys it in a
half-penny packet, labelled ‘Comic Cuts,’ the
head of the firm in a four-and-sixpenny packet,
labelled ‘The Christian.’ But its effect is always
the same : it fills the void in the urban mind per-
petually craving to be occupied. For the strain of
city life seems to have robbed the Townsman of
the power of doing nothing with a quiet content;
he can no longer sit still and let his mind wander
idly from thought to thought; unoccupied it fills
with distressful thoughts only, and aches dully.
There is a mental irritation on him that rnußt be
allayed, and he allays it with a literary drug.
* * * •
As with all other drugs, the passion for it grows
with indulgence; it becomes the necessity of life.
Observe the clerks and intelligent artisans in the
carriage of a suburban train, and you will see such
of them as are not in a dull, happy stupor over a
half-penny paper, feverish, with wandering eyes
and fidgetty fingers ; protract the journey for an
hour, and they will grow haggard under the
strain of doing nothing. They should have
interest to fill their thoughts not unpleasantly, at
least they must have their dreams; but their
weakened minds have lost the grip on them, they
are aching for the literary drug. Is it August,
and you are journeying to the sea, observe the
desperate haste of the flurried trippers to arm
themselves against reflection with the weekly
rag-bags ; observe the relief in their faces when
their clammy, urban hands have secure in their
grasp the blue-covered trash. They have probably
been looking forward for weeks to soothing their
street-grimed eyes with the country sights, their
traffic-stunned ears with its sounds; and the
woods and meadows slip by the carriage-windows
unobserved, while they read, with a thirsty satis-
faction, that the flounder lays seven million eggs
in a year; someone cries out at the first sight of
the sea, their indifferent eyes pass swiftly over the
wonder to fall glutted on the wooden antics of the
saw-dust dolls of the ‘ Guinea Story,’
• * * *
[Cf. Calverley’s neatest anti-climax—
Girls’ Song-Games.
Writes Victor Daley :—
As I sit smoking by an open window of a cot-
tage in a quiet suburban street I hear the voices
of a group of girl-children on the other side of the
way singing the child-songs that were sung by
their mothers and grandmothers—and by possibly
a far-stretching line of ancestresses who had also
their days of youth, but have long since passed
into the Herebefore. The songs are for the most
part as direct in statement as the old ballads in
the ‘Percy Reliques.’ They have, too, the cheer-
ful inconsequence in the matter of refrain, and
the delight in repetition, which are such conspicu-
ous features of some of the old ballads that have
come down to us. These qualities are to be found
in the primitive poetry of all races. Savages of
the present day will sit and listen complacently
by the . hour to chants containing but one
idea, stated over and over again. But think
of our music-halls, and the dreadful ditties
without any idea at all sung in them nightly
to delighted audiences ! The blackest and
most benighted African seems to be, in regard
to his taste in song-material, far superior to the
average Britannic citizen.
» * * *
Children of civilised or so-called civilised
parents are simply small savages writhing in the
bonds of petticoats or knickerbockers. Therefore
they are satisfied with their own traditional
literature ; in fact, when they are by themselves,
and not under the compelling eyes of parents or
teachers, will have nothing to do with any other.
The most charming of songs and stories have been
written for children during the present century —
(in previous centuries children were regarded as
little nuisances, and were sent to bed early out of
the way of their elders)—but they have been given
a cold reception by the small public for whom
they were written. It is, indeed, questionable
if many of Hans Andersen’s stories have been
taken to the hearts of the children, even in Den-
mark and Germany. But ‘ Jack and the Bean-
stalk,’ and ‘Jack the Giant-Killer,’ and the
‘ Three Bears,’ and ‘Little Red Riding Hood,’
and ‘Cinderella,’ will probably outlast the life of
the tremendous British Empire, and be heard
with unabated interest by the children living in an
age when the plays of imperial Shakespeare will
be known to only the Bookworms and Dryasdusts
of the period.
* * * *
The vitality of these child-stories seems almost
miraculous. It would be quite so if it were not
for the fact that the human raoe—in spite of
homocultnre, and scientific training, and no end
of other nauseous specifics for turning men into
machines—is continually beginning at the be-
ginning and passing through several years of
healthy, happy, irresponsible barbarism before it
takes up the yoke of Civilisation. If the authors
of ‘Jack and the Beanstalk,’ and ‘Little Red
Riding Hood,’ and ‘Cinderella’—the latter
known in other lands and languages as Avislang
and Rhodope and by many other ni/mei —
had only arranged with their publishers
(and the heirs and executors of the same
to the thousandth generation) for a royalty
on the sale of their works, to be paid to their de-
scendants in scecula sceculorum, those descendants
would about own the earth by this time. But
even the names of these wonderful authors are as
lost to us as the name borne by Hercules when he
sojourned amongst women, or the -secret name of
PEACE: A STUDY.
He stood, a worn-out city clerk—
Who’d toil’d, and seen no holiday,
For forty years from dawn to dark—■
Alone beside Caermarthen Bay.
He felt the salt spray on his lips;
Heard children’s yoices on the sands;
Up the sun’s path he saw the ships
Sail on and on to other lands ;
And laugh’d aloud. Each sight and sound
To him was joy too deep for tears;
He sat him on the beach, and hound
A blue bandana round his ears
And thought how, posted near his door,
His own green door on Camden Hill,
Two bands at least, most likely more,
Were mingling at their own sweet will
Yerdi with Vanoe. And at the thought
He laugh'd again, and softly drew
That • Morning Herald ’ that he’d bought
Forth from his breast, and read it through.]
These are they whose mental craving is dulled
by the coarsest and cheapest forms of the drug ;
but the spread of enlightenment, the University
Extension Lectures, and even more the custom of
employing more servants in the households of
well-to-do tradesmen, have formed a largesuburban
class, chiefly women, of greater leisure; and for
them the drug must not only be wrapped in
dearer packets, but it must be of more stimulating
properties. Bare incident, threadbare jokes, and
bald, unessential information do not fill comfort-
ably their mental void ; tl ey n quire their novi Is
to deal with ‘Burning Questions,’ the ‘Complex
Problems of our Modernity,’ and other fine-sound-
ing drivel. Some puritanical twist of mind, in-
herited probably from dissenting ancestors, makes
them prefer that their fiction should inspire into
them the feeling that they have been spending
profitably their time in absorbing great truths;
and. they, arise from a perusal of Miss Corelli’s
atomic theory ‘cultured.’
♦ * * *
For all the dearness of the packet, cheapness is
the striking quality of its contents : cheap thought,
cheap fancy, cheap character, cheap pathos, cheap
humor, all cheaply presented. Literature, the. fine
handling of life, does not appeal to these readers;it
displeases them rather ; they are flying from life.
Insight into human nature* shocks them; the
truth pains them ; they are 1 lind to beauty. But
after all husks are quite filling.
*•* - - *
It is the fashion to feel, or to affect, a fine anger
against the manufacturers of the literary drug. It
may be righteous ; but it is rather a waste of a
good passion. Their pretensions are irritating,
doubtless; but after all not more irritating than a
near barrel-organ. When the blaze of the boomster,
swelling the volume of undying trumpeters, is
sonorous over the land, the turning a deaf ear to
it is more sensible than wailirlg of Literature dis-
graced by self-advertisement. Literature is not
touched. When, as you row up the Thames, the
black Beeeham hoard, flanked by the appro-
priately bilious Carter board, starts up in a
u> adow and importunes the eye, you do not wail
at the disgrace to medicine, you look at the
meadows on the other side. Miss Corelli and
Caine are as necessary to the vulgar as Beeeham
and Carter —the former fill, the latter empty—
surely they may push their wares in the fashion
that draws best the money from the pockets of
their customers. Rather admire their advertising
skill, their sympathetic knowledge of the great
heart of the vulgar, of the claptrap that appeals
to it; cease to wonder, and, above all, cease to
be angry that Miss Corelli has sold her thousands,
Mr. Caine his ten thousands.
Amen to all that.

H.C.C.: “ You ask ‘ Which of Austin Dobson’s
is the best English rondeau? ’ I like this one, un-
earthed in 1887 from ‘ Harper’s ’ of June, 1885 :
TO A JUNE ROSE.
O royal Rose ! the Reman dress’d
His feast with thee: thy petals press’d
Augustan brows ; thine odor fine,
Mixed with the three-tiim'S-mingled wine,
Lent the long Thracian draught its zest.
What, marvel then, if host a.nd guest,
By Love, by Song, by Thee caress’d,
Half-trembled on the half-divine,
O royal Rose 1
And yet—and yet—l love thee best
In our old gardens of the West,
Whether about my thatch thou twine
Or Hers, that brown-eyed maid of mine,
Who lulls thee on her lawny breast,
O royal Rose! ”
[ln the collected edition line 7 appears as
By Song, by Joy, by Thee caress’d.
Probably our author reflected that the Roman
didn’t let Love in till after the feast—he ‘never
mixed.’ Or possibly he reflected that the intro-
duction of Love in the middle made Love at the
close an anti-climax.]

“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak
that I could not leave my bed. ~At this stage I was pro-
nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines
of different kinds, but nothing did me any good until,
reading in the Age of your ‘ Bronchitis Cure,’ I got a
bottle, and after taking two doses I was much improved.
After taking two bottles the cough and night sweats left
me, my appetite returned, and I was able to get up ; and
am now in good health, for which I am truly grateful, and
shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Yours
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill, Victoria.”
“ After suffering from Asthma until I was 17 years old,

I have much pleasure in recommending it.—ALEX. J.
ANDERSON, Geelong, Victoria. ”

night was black ; the burglar was black ; the took
aoparently, was black also. The black intrude
ran after the widow into the blackness outsid-
and then disappeared. And yet lots of peop
positively identify lots of other people under ci
uumstances exactly like this.
LOVE’S MOLOCH.
How long shall we hear the sobbing?
How long shall our hearts beat slow
To the wail of a ceaseless sorrow
That follows us to and fro,
Who watch from our safe rock-ramparts
The wreck of the ringless brides
On the flow of the crimson sunsets,
On the ebb of the white moon-tides?
They walk in the weeping darkness,
They hold on a wasted arm
What Love cannot guard from hunger
Or Passion itself keep warm ;
They walk in the weeping darkness,
They clasp to their breasts of shame
The pitiful white-faced burdens
The world will refuse to name.
They bend to the wee wan faces,
And scald with their burning tears
The tiny lips that will curse them
When knowlec’gi has come with years ;
They weep by their rocking cradles
Whose labor should prove so sweet,
And the wealth of their white girl-garland?
Lies crushed at the gray world’s feet.
They creep to our curtained windows,
They stand at our doors, thrice-barred ;
And their feet are torn and bleeding
Who found that their path was hard.
Their hands that we held may touch us,
Their lips that we loved may plead,
But never an ear will hearken,
And never a heart will heed.
They shrink from the glaring sunlight,
But down where the lamps are lit
They stretch a hand to their sorrow,
And drink to the deeps of it;
There are sins that the night will pardon,
And smiles for the roses red,
Oh ! Woe to the maiden-mothers !
And Woe to the bonds unsaid 1
How long shall we lead the victims ?
How long in a crimson flood
Shall the gates of our great Gomorrah
Be washed in our sisters’ blood ?
How long shall they heap the altars ?
How long shall their cry be heard
Ere the fire and the brimstone teach us
That the anger of God be stirred ?
WILL H. OGILVIE.
A SONG OF LOVE.
Why is it, sweet, that ever hearts will sadden
Just in the rapture of the purest bliss ?
Why is it that thy music cannot gladden
The hot remembrance of a stolen kiss ?
That when my arm thy throbbing form embrao-*-.
And all is happiness and rest supine,
A vision as of death and weeping faces
Comes right between thy laughing eyes ana
mine?
A glimpse of winter through the golden maze
Of summer days.
I listen to the song that you are singing,
And all around the world is gay and fair,
Yet, as the notes caressingly are ringing
In joyous freedom through the mellow air,
It seems to me as if some sombre moral,
A sound of sobbing faintly underlay
The triumph and the glory of the choral
That tells exultingly of summer’s sway.
As if some chord (so soon, my God, so soon)
Were out of tune !
A gloomy shadow on the sunlit hours;
In warm, confiding hearts a sudden chill ;
A drop of poison in the brightest flowers ;
A dark presentiment of coming ill.
Ah, sing no more! Though deep the lov
treasure,
And sweet thy music—at some eerie call
There rises, even from the midst of pleasure.
A nameless something, bitterer than gall,
And pours its sadness into, line for line,
That song of thine.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 26, 1898.
A Word for Australia.
Those good Englishmen, and Irishmen, and
Scotchmen who are arranging our Federal
destiny have so little imagination—so little
imagination. And the two or three Australians
who leaven the lump are as bad, for they belong
mostly to the old generation which drew strength
and impetus from England, which nourishes Aus-
tralian ideals (if it nourishes them), despite its
mental habits and its training. While there is
yet time, The Bulletin speaks one word for
the New Generation, for the Young Men, for
the great and growing, though as yet inarticu-
late, class for whom this our country is Home,
the only Home they know, the Home which
they love ardently, and in whose future they
trust. For, no matter what superficial critics
think and say, our Young Men are not wholly
wrapped up in sport, are far from wholly un-
concerned as to the business of nation-making
now proceeding under their eyes. But they
still have to find themselves, and their Voice.
• * * *
With Heaven knows what groanings and
contortions the Federal delegates have at last
resolved to practically leave the control of the
continent’s rivers to the continent’s govern-
ment. And throughout all the clamor which
preceded the decision, though many men rose
to advocate the rights of New South Wales, or
of Victoria, or of some other province, there
was not found one man to rise and say : “ Damn
New South Wales and the rest ! I Represent
Australia ! ” That obvious utterance was
beyond the capacity of the most capable delegate
of them all. And the Conference’s attitude to
this question is its attitude to all. The settle-
ment of financial differences is postponed for the
present, but only because, after all the un-
imaginative heads had striven for months, they
found themselves unable to split the odd half-
penny. They dearly desired to split the odd
halfpenny. So throughout. The debates have
been full of arguments that so much was due
to this province and the other, and quite empty
nof reference as to what was due to the Commo-
wealth of all the provinces. The claim of
Australia was first and last and sole reason for
the meeting of the Convention, and all efforts
are devoted to upholding the claims of this
or that fragment of Australia.
* * * *
The time has come for more enlightened
efforts to a grander end. Only knaves or fools
would wish to perpetuate division in the very
hour of union. It is true that, in a political
sense, some of the Convention delegates are
foolish, some are knavish. They are so used
to look at the province beyond the border as
an enemy that it is impossible for them to
change the point of view, no matter what lofty
ideal tempts or urges. Or they believe that
their own personal interests are bound up with
the perpetuation of provincialism, and fear that
confederation will diminish their political im-
portance or their political profits. And pro-
vincial hostility has been so long inflamed by
Parliaments and by the press that a section of
the people has been infected with the poli-
ticians’ jealousy, and sees through the poli-
ticians’ squinting eyes. But what intelligent
young native of any province is there who does
not regard himself, first and foremost, as a
native of Australia, does not reckon all Aus-
tralians as brethren, is not careless what hap-
pens so long as Australian interests are served
—knowing that the welfare of all the provinces
must involve the welfare of each, and that indi-
vidual losses will not be a drop in the bucket of
the general gain ? Our artificial boundaries,
and restrictions on intercourse, and obstacles to
community, will have to meet destruction
sooner or later, and the longer they remain the
greater will be the difficulty and cost of
removal. Federation is a return to nature and
the natural order. Who does not see that the
single vital element in the Federal scheme is
the need for its speedy consummation, on any
terms or on none ?
* * * *
The Tories, the fogies, the men of the Old
Generation, call Federation a leap in the dark.
It is a leap out of the dark—cannot but be
towards the light. Why, then, carry the pro-
vincial prejudices forward to the account of the
new era ? The writer was born in one Austra-
lian province; has lived in two others ; has
travelled in all six ; and he honestly declares
that lie is only technically aware that boun-
daries exist—he has no preference—the justice
that he would give one he would give all—to
him they are all Australia. And he multiplies
himself by the New Generation, and by the
Newer Generation, and by the Generation yet
Unborn, and confidently affirms that Australia
can trust Australia, that Australians can trust
themselves. And if, without any condition
or reservation whatever, the map of Australia
were to-morrow colored one uniform tint, to-
day’s green and to-day’s blue and to-day’s
yellow would gain equally, and the trifling loss
due to substitution of a natural for an unnatural
state of things would, in proportion to the gain,
be indistinguishable.
• * * *
The financial and commercial and national
profits of Federation have been often insisted
on, but the fact that Federation means a lessen-
ing of the very gravest national danger has
been less stressed. Nobody knows just what
would happen to this country in the event of
European war involving Britain ; but the risk
is (the word is measured) terrible. Whether
we separate from Britain or remain allied with
her, that risk remains ; and our present situa-
tion is nothing but an invitation to attack.
With a handful of fifth-rate vessels to defend
thousands of mile of coast-line; with a meagre
and inefficient military force, divided under
six separate commands ; without arsenals,
weapons, ammunition, or the means of making
them ; with broken-gauge railways to impede
mobilisation; with wealthy coastal cities prac-
tically unfortified, and with agricultural and
mineral land which is a rich bait to a colonising
invader; our position is on the very edge of a
precipice. Federation would not put us out of
risk, but it would help. And the risk is so
tremendous that anyone who has taken the
least trouble to comprehend it cannot but rage
at the petty-minded political “leaders” who
squabble over the details of provincial property
when at any moment we may be called on to
fight for our national existence. In time of
peace this language may seem strong; how
weak it would seem in war-time ! The man
perhaps the most competent to judge it who
has ever been in Australia, soldier-statesman
Henry Norman, has endorsed every word.
The longer The Bulletin lives the keener
grows its sense of Australia’s danger under
existing conditions. But the excellent English-
men and Irishmen and Scotchmen who are
arranging our Federal destiny are concerned
merely with the things right under their noses
—the odd halfpenny and the odd bucket of
water. Alas ! they have so little imagination.
Land Auctions.
The announcement of N.S.W. Government
that it will hold a tremendous series of auction
sales on the 29th and 30th March is an example
of how an animal disrespectfully mentioned in
Scripture returns to a certain substance that is
too unpleasant to mention. There are to be
auction sales on these two days at 62 separate
towns. Over 1000 lots are to be offered, all in
the Eastern and Central Divisions, the average
size of the allotments being about 110 acres.
The total area to be thrown on the market is
111,648 acres, at an average upset price of 255.
an acre, and it is expected that the sale will
pan out for something like £200,000. There
are no conditions as regards residence or im-
provement ; the only stipulation is that the
purchase money is all to be paid inside three
months—in other words, it is all to arrive in
the Treasury by June 30, the last day of the
financial year, just in time to help reduce the
Reid deficit, or to help at making a Reid sur-
plus. Most of the land will probably, there-
fore, be grabbed by large landholders, banks,
financial companies, and private speculators
in other words, by tbe class which has ready
money. The small agriculturist, as a rule,
hasn’t much ready money, and can’t pay up
inside three months.
In the old days—the good days of drunken
inflation and boom and riot which so many
people insist on regarding as the normal condi-
tion of the country (hence, when the country
got into a more natural condition, they in-
sisted on calling it “depression”)—land-sales
and borrowing were the sources of N.S.W.’s
alleged prosperity. Work was plentiful then
because the Government found a billet for
almost every able-bodied man who wanted one
at making roads, railways, and bridges, at
building up and digging out; and paid his
wages with borrowed money, or with money
obtained by throwing the public estate whole-
sale at whoeyer would pay for it. Wages were
high, because the Government took so many
men off the general labor-market that those
who remained were in strong demand. Taxes
were low, for the land-sale money paid half,
and sometimes more than half, the expenses of
government. Until the collapse of the first
great Foreign-trade regime at the end of 1892,
and the advent of the three Governments of
which Dibbs was the leading figure the
Stuart-Dibbs Ministry of 1883-85, the Dibbs
Ministry of 1885, and the Jennings - Dibbs
Ministry of 1886—the sale of public lands raged
furiously, and there were good times in the
Treasury, and a great and glorious burst all the
way from Eden to Milparinka.
* * * •
The following is a record of 15 years’ Govern-
ment land-jobbing in this particular line :
Sold by auction.
Acres.
Parkes ... ... 1881 ... 851,752
„ 1882 ... 858,735
Stuart-Dibbs ... 1883 ... 61,683
» ... 1884 ... 41,544
~ ... 1885 ... 2,648
Jennings-Dibbs ... 1886 ... 60,353
Parkes ... ... 1887 ... 107,374
lBBB ... 105,485
» ... ... 1889 ... 22,589
» ... ... 1890 ... 68,683
.. 1891 ... 47,105
Dibbs ... ... 1892 ... 82,981
>. ... ... 1893 ... 96,219
.. ... ... 1894 ... 44,794
Reid ... ... 1895 ... 21,886
„ ... ... 1896 ... 13,959
From these figures it would appear that the
present N.S.W. Government has, so far, the
best record in this particular direction. But,
having sacrificed a great sum in customs revenue
to appease its friends, the Calico Jimmies of
Sydney, and not being able to raise enough
direct taxation to fill the gap, and finding it
impossible to charge quite everything to loans,
and having used up the expedient of leaving
part of this year’s bills over to next year on a
Cash Basis, and the other expedient of collect-
ing two years’ income-tax in one year, and find-
ing the annexation of the Education Fund in-
sufficient to cover the yawning void, it has to
get back, after all, to the old, easy, corrupt re-
source of land-jobbing on a big scale, as
practised in the old, gay, crooked days of
Parkes, and Garrett, and Ezekiel Baker,
and John Davies—he of the famous Three
Hats.
* * * •
The huge auction of next month is to be a
bigger sacrifice of the public estate in two days
than has ever taken place in a whole year since
1883, when Stuart and Dibbs made their
famous effort at reform. The great objection
to the auction system is that it is purely and
simply an expedient for obtaining money.
It doesn’t necessarily imply settlement or im-
provement, for there are no conditions attached
except the condition that the money shall be
paid up very suddenly so as to prevent the
Foreign-trade regime being discredited by
another deficit. It doesn’t profess to do any-
thing in the way of restraining monopoly or
encouraging production—it is nothing at all
save a desperate device for raising cash. This
reversion on a large scale to the worst kind of
land-jobbery is not what was expected of a
Ministry which made such large professions on
the subject of land reform ; certainly it was
not expected that that Ministry would go in
for the most wholesale sacrifice of the public
estate that has been attempted in the last 15
years. This one sale will more than equal (in
two days) all the auction sales done by Stuart
and Dibbs in three years. And, in addition to
this, the Government is endeavoring to raise
another .£40,000 or more by the sale of certain
city lands—so apparently a quarter-million
is wanted by next June, and wanted darned
bad.
* « * *
Barring the three Ministries of 1883-86, in
which Dibbs was the strong man and chief
financier, no N.S.W. Government for about a
quarter of a century has made any great effort
to swear off the evil habit of living on the easy
throwing away of the public estate. There
were variations, certainly, in the modes of
throwing it away, and some modes were worse
than others, but the results didn’t differ widely.
In 1881 and 1882, when the land policy was at
its worst, Parkes realised about £2,800,000 per
year by putting the soil up the spout. In 1883
Stuart and Dibbs sacrificed £1,200,000 of
revenue by the almost entire stoppage of
auctions, and during the Dibbs, Stuart, and
Jennings regime the public estate was so care-
fully conserved that the revenue derived from
it averaged only £1,600,000 per annum—the
lowest figure recorded in the last 23 years. But
the attempt to impose direct taxation in order to
make up the loss failed through the bitter opposi-
tion of the old Parkes Foreign-trade party. The
ad valorem duties which were put on instead
were insuflScient, and they brought down on
the Government the bitter rage of the Sydney
shoddy press. The want of revenue laid the
first foundations of the “ Dibbs deficit,” about
which so much has since been heard. Hardly
anybody recollects now that the more or less
Dibbs Ministries of 1883-86 sacrificed nearly
£5,000,000 of revenue in trying to put down
the miserably-extravagant land-auction sys-
tem ; that fact has been so carefully concealed
by the daily papers of Sydney that the only
thing remembered about these Ministries is
certain small unpaid liabilities they left behind
them. Then Parkes came back to power, and
proceeded to save the country and straighten
the finances by raising £700,000 a year
more land - revenue, and throwing away
the public estate at the rate of £2,300 000
per annum. The New South Wales land-
revenue, of late years, has averaged
from £1,900,000 to £2,000,000 annually. But
the worst feature of the system—the great
auction dodge—seemed to have almost entirely
died out ; after the violent shutting-down
effected by Stuart and Dibbs, in 1883, no one
—not even Parkes— dared to start it again on
the old scale, and the Reid Ministry, though it
raised about the usual £1,900,000 or £2,000 000
a year off the land, kept this particular
evil at a minimum. But now, appar-
ently the dire necessities of the imported
Shoddy regime call for a resumption of the old
gaudy policy, with its smellful reminiscences of
the bad old rum-and-curse days that are de-
parted.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tasmania’s Maximum Wage.
The minimum-wage question has never yet
struck Tasmania, but the maximum wage
in that lovely Land of Dreams is fixed by the
Chief Justice. As thus : The C.J., as chair-
man of the bush-fires Relief Committee and
Lord High Apportioner of the publicly-sub-
scribed funds, lately ukased that four shillings
a day is to be the maximum wage for those
burnt-out settlers and others who may be em-
ployed re-building fences and houses in the fire-
devastated districts. But as the Government
employes in some of those districts—road-
repairers, bridge men, &c.—were being paid at
the rate of six shillings a day, some dissatis-
faction naturally ensued. Quote :
.Representations were, however, made to the Minister
of Lands and Works upon the point, and Mr. Pillinger
at once gave instructions to have a proper rate resorted
to by the officers of the department under his charge.
That the Van Dobson, whose own minimum-
wage is fixed by Act of Parliament at about
£4 a day, should consider four shillings a day
a “ proper ” maximum for Bill Smith, is pro-
bably what Bill Smith expected and deserved ;
but that Jim and Joe, and all the other
six-bob-a-day chaps in blucher boots would
be sweated a third of their munificent
salaries in the sacred cause of Charity, not
even a burnt-out Bill Smith would have con-
sidered probable. Yet if Bill had brains as
well as, or in lieu of, bluchers, he would note
that a third of the C.J.’s screw is £SOO, and
that £lO is all that appears in' the published
list as the measure of his sacrifice. But the
beauties of private charity are lost on the aver-
age person who sweats and wears bluchers, and
it isn’t much use preaching at him yet. For the
present, we can find food for thought in noting
how the Shadows of the Old Regime cling to
Tasmania under the Van Dobson ditto.
Confusion.
The further the Federal Convention struggles
along the path to brotherhood, the more foolish
appears the policy of leaving the public debts
under State control. The policy of leaving the
railways under separate State control is still
more foolish so far as concerns the future
prosperity of the interior, but with regard to
the financial muddle under immediate con-
sideration the federation of the debts is the
first essential. The situation boils down into
these four propositions :
(1) The Federal Government will have the sole right to
collect customs and excise revenue. Therefore, the pro-
vinces hand over a source of income which yields at
present about £7,000,000 a year. They will also be
relieved of expenditure to the extent of about £1,600,000
a year.
(2) If the debts, or the bulk of them, were handed over,
the provinces would give up £7,000,000 a year of revenue
and be relieved of about the same expenditure. The
Federal Government would be responsible for raising the
needful revenue to meet the interest on the debt. Having
the sole right to levy customs and excise it could raise
the cash that way, or, if it preferred, it could do it by
direct taxation. The fiscal issue would be left absolutely
open.
(3) Under the proposed system the Federal Government
may collect so much revenue that it will be able to return
the States enough out of the surplus to pay the interest
on their debts. On the other hand it may not. In the
latter case some of the States dun no where they are.
(4) The Foreign-trade party in N.S.W. says distinctly
The Beginning of the End.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are Envoys, China, knocking at thy gate!
While the Western world that lies beyond thy wall
Took the puissance of thy mysteried power on
trust,
Stood the Mongol’s ancient realm immune from
thrall,
Though the dynasties around it bit the dust ;
But the veil that hid thy palsied throne from sight
In the neighbouring lands showed threadbare
to the sun—
Then the chimera of centuries took flight,
And the race to parcel China was begun.
Hence the presence of the Envoy at the gate.
When the conqueror of old had marked a prey,
t He was wont to wheel his legions into line—
‘ Behold ! ” he said, “ let me but grasp the bay,
The golden spoils of victory be thine ! ”
And the quarry stood to watch the war-cloud
break,
Prepared to meet and wrestle with the foe;
The ravisher fought boldly for the stake,
The victim knew the issue of the blow.
But the witless days of iron buried lie,
And wilier brains direct the “ game of kings ”;
No herald’s trump proclaims invasion nigh,
The overture is played on muted strings ;
A righteous-visaged Presence, labelled “ Trade,”
Moves out from where the loaded Maxims stand,
With treasure-chest to proffer friendly aid
To tap the golden founts that stud the land.
There are unfamiliar voices at the gate.
China, the Smooth-faced Presence fronts thee now,
And scans the Flowery Land with amorous
glance;
He wears a dual crown upon his brow,
Lion and Bear divide his cognizance.
Haply some respite on the suit may wait,
The while the game of Royal Brag is played,
But both the gamesters hold the trumps of fate,
And stand to win upon a hand of Trade ;
And their Envoys now are posted at the gate.
Strip the lustre off your shrines and ye are wise—
Forthe Smooth-faced Oneall other idols mocks—
Hide the gems—he hath a key whose art defies
The cunning of your children and their locks;
Eat, drink, and merry be while yet you may.
The to-morrow is but short that ye can boast,
For the Envoys at the gate have come to stay,
And the convoys of their masters line the coast.
Hark / the Envoys wax impatient at the gate.
Brisbane.
P.J.D.


that it will do its best to persuade the Federal Govern-
ment to practically abolish customs duties, on the ground
that N.S.W. can live on its land revenue and its borrow-
Jng-s, and it doesn’t need to care whether the other States
live or not.
This makes a highly interesting complication,
and Ihe Bulletin infers from it that the
Commonwealth ought to make matters clear by
taking over all or a large portion of the public
debts. At the same time, the matter
isn’t worth quite all the row that is being
made about it. If the Foreign - trade
party captures the Federal Government,
and it won’t raise a big customs revenue,
and the separate provinces have no longer the
power to levy customs-duties, and they have
their iuterest to pay, and have lost the usual
means of paying it, the only thing left to do is
for the States to send in to the Federal Trea-
surer a general intimation that they are going
broke. Then the central Government would
take over the debts, for the sake of maintain-
ing its blessed credit in London, and the matter
would end quite simply and naturally. If the
central Government didn’t make its arrange-
ments quickly enough, and some of the pro-
vinces actually broke before things were
fixed up, it would give Australian credit
in London a shock which it has wanted
very badly for a long time. Apparently,
Australia wfill keep on borrowing in London
until Bull gets such a shock that he won’t lend
any more, and the very worst kind of a shock
is urgently needed. Therefore, The Bulletin’s
theory is : (1) That the Federal Government
ought to take over the debts, or the bulk of
them, now, for the sake of the possible saving
in interest; (2) at the same time, if it doesn’t
do this now it will almost certainly do it later
on ; (3) if it doesn’t do it later on the results
should be a highly interesting study, and this
paper wouldn’t miss seeing it for a good deal.
Anyhow, the matter is certainly not important
enough to be a fair excuse for blocking Federa-
tion.
\ A Tape-Worm Specialist.
LiAs't week the N.S.W. Full Court confirmed a
sentence of six months passed by Judge Docker
on an alleged tape-worm specialist. “Madame ”
Kingston, the specialist in question, be-
longed to the tribe of practitioners who go
into trances, or discover the state of the
patient’s inside by communication with the
spirits, or get information by palmistry, or who
communicate with the ghost of a deceased
grandmother. She fastened on to Mrs. Stewart,
and told her that she had 503 worms in
her body, three of them being large and
aggressive tape - worms. She sold large
quantities of medicine to Mrs. Stewart,
and professed to extract worms out of her
wholesale by various operations. Unluckily,
the patient got away with a bottleful of the
alleged worms, which proved to be white of
egg, and the result was six months’ light labor.
So utterly quack-ridden is N.S.W. that a law-
yer actually appealed on the ground that
getting money by false pretences in this manner
was not a false pretence “ within the meaning
of the Act,” and tried to demonstrate that the
proceeding was perfectly legitimate. What
most people will wonder at is that the prisoner
(she was tried by Judge Docker— a gentleman
who, when in anything like good condition,
dashes tremendous sentences about in the most
lavish fashion) didn’t get three years at the
very least. Many a poor wretch has got three
/ears for raising =63 15s. by false pretences
without aggravating his crime by telling the
victim that her inside was full of reptiles, and
keeping her in a grievous state of alarm, and
performing operations on her, and filling her
with horrid medicines, and possibly endanger-
ing her life by preventing her resorting to
proper medical advice, and finally insulting her
by professing to extract bottles-full of worms
from her. Judging by the sentence it would
appear that N.S. W. law regards all these un-
pleasant proceedings as rather a mitigation of
the offence than otherwise.
The Joneses and Smiths of Politics.
The decision of N.S.W. Labor Electoral League
to go in bald-headed for the abolition of the
Leg. Council is vastly better than leaving the
Council as it is, or than reforming it on the 25-
year nomination basis—still it has serious draw-
backs. It would leave the Assembly in sole
possession, and the curse of the Assembly is
that it doesn’t even pretend to represent N.S.
Wales. It is made up of 125 men who repre-
sent a spot apiece, and each man is charged
with looking after the separate interests of his
separate spot. But any matter which doesn’t
affect one single spot but N.S.W. in general
is nobody’s business, consequently there isn’t
one strong Federationist in the whole Assembly.
The only great and eloquent Federationist
N.S.W. possesses was thrown out of the popular
Chamber because he was of no special use to
Gum-tree Flat or Old Dry Plain, and he had to
become a nominee councillor to prevent' him
being thrown out of politics altogether. N.S.W.
badly wants a House that will represent all
N.S.W.—the whole of it, voting by manhood
suffrage as one constituency—not merely Old
Dry Creek or Hog Vale individually. Then
its members would have to get in on their own
merits, because no man could keep a grocery-
shop all over N.S.W., or provide culverts for
all N.S.W., or kiss all the infants in N.S.W.
In short, it wants a House that it can put its
great men into, when it has any. At present
it has no place to put them. But as such a
House would necessarily overshadow the purely
parochial Assembly, and reduce the member
for Old Dry Plain to insignificance, there is
very little chance of it being created. The
average M.L.A. is a poor creature who wants
either to have an Upper House which is so
dull and Tory and unrepresentative and intel-
lectually barren that it will make him look
almost a brilliant statesman by comparison, or
else to have none at all, so that there will be
no room for invidious comparisons. The three
Joneses, and the two Smiths, and Whiddon,
and Wilks, and ex-preacher Smailes are at
home in a small, mean, parochial political
Bvstem, and there would be no place for them
in anything better. Ami no one expects the
three Joneses and the two Smiths to yearn for
the elevation of the Legislature into an affair
With any national soul in it.
More About Thrift.
The Weekly Press (Christchurch, M.L.) thus
re-states the case for Thrift, and the Tory plea
against the Seddon Government’s Old Age
Pension scheme :
All property is the result of labor and thrift. Taxes
come from labor or savings. If the aged poor are paid a
pension out of the taxes, those who save keep them. If
there are two workmen who get equal wages, and one
puts by a sixpence a week from the time he is 21 till he is
65 he will have a pension that will keep him. The other
workman, instead of putting past his sixpence a week,
spent it, it may be in drink, or tobacco, or amusements—
at 65 he has no pension. If the Government steps in and
says to this penniless man “ here is a pension for you
equal to what your fellow-workman has got by his
own saving,” is that right ? Surely if there is one
of the two to be rewarded the man who has been inde-
pendent, self-regarding and thrifty should be rewarded.
But no, some people say that the man who refused to
put anything by for a rainy day should receive a reward,
and the other get not even thanks for his self-denial.
That is not all ; the taxes come from labor and saving,
and the man who gets no pension has to pay part of
his small fund his saving to help to keep his mate
because his mate w'ould not save.
Almost every case has another side to it, and
The Bulletin hereby presents the other side
to the above. All property is not the result of
labor and thrift. The most valuable part of
every nation’s property is the land and the
natural water-supply, and neither labor nor
thrift made these. Taxes in a great measure
come from vice instead of labor and savings ; in
Maoriland, the great bulk of them are derived
from the consumption of drink and tobacco and
imported luxuries ; through tote - gambling,
through fines for disorderly conduct and the
like—in fact, out of about £2,800,000 revenue
raised by taxes of all kinds, just about half-
a-million are paid by the thrifty. And, in-
stead of the thrifty man paying away part of
his small fund in his old age “ to help to keep
his mate because his mate would not save,” it is
the other way about; the spendthrift is paying
all his life, by his contributions to the drink
and tobacco and cigar and tote revenue, and
the revenue derived from duties on innumerable
luxuries, to support the man who does save.
The really saving man—the one who doesn’t
drink or smoke or gamble or get fined or buy
luxuries to eat or drink or wear—contributes
hardly anything to the revenue ; the thriftless
man pays practically everything for him. And,
when the latter grows old, and gets the pen-
sion that he has earned by his thriftlessness
dozens of times over, it isn’t the thrifty man
who pays for it; it is paid for almost wholly by
the new generation of thriftless men who are
again keeping the revenue going. The thrifty
man, in this connection, is a lying, grasping
fraud all round. In any case, what does the
Tory propose to do with the old penniless
spendthrift? If he shouldn’t be maintained
at the public cost per medium of a very small
State pension, it is equally immoral to main-
tain him at the public cost in a hospital
or refuge or a benevolent asylum, or by pro-
miscuous charity. If as a pensioner he is a
burden on the Thrifty, he is also a burden as
a pauper. Therefore, there are only two logical
courses to be followed. One is that the ancient
who has no money should starve to death in
some place where the sight of his bones can’t
be an annoyance to the Thrifty (the thrifty
have no more right to be annoyed by the spec-
tacle of the bones of the thriftless than to be
annoyed by having to pay to keep the latter
class from becoming bones). The other idea is
that the thriftless should be executed when
they get past work, and then boiled down and
the products sold to pay execution expenses
(for the thrifty man to have to contribute out
of his little fund towards the expense of de-
capitating the thriftless would be nothing short
of an outrage). Which of these two courses
has the Christchurch Tory organ the courage to
advocate ?
The Orange-and-Green processions case in Mel-
bourne brought to light a shriekingly humorous
Wesleyan preacher. The reverend was on the
Orange side of the case, and started his evidence
glibly enough :
George Tregear, Wesleyan minister, gave evidence
with regard to the service performed in the Presbyterian
Church, Brunswick, on 18th July. He had seen the
emblems produced in court the previous day, and they
were emblematical of the great civil victories won by the
King—the lawful King.
Then barrister Smyth stopped his streak of
eloquence by asking the name of the king, and
there was a pause which nearly blew the roof
off the premises. The witness had either
forgotten or had never known the name of the
blessed monarch he was gushing over, and had
to be informed that his hero was William 111.
When he was informed, he remembered sud-
denly, but probably if he had been told that his
hero was named Samuel XII. he would have
remembered just the same. Then an Irish person
named Nolan loomed up darkly through the fog
of controversy on the other side of the case. He
objected to the waving of the Orange banner
as a premeditated insult intended to rub in the
recollection of the “ batin’ ” his countrymen
“ ressaved.” Being asked for dates or names or
other particulars about the said “ batin’,” he
couldn’t supply any. So, if these two witnesses
w r ere fair specimens of their respective parties,
neither side really knew what it was proceshing
and waving banners and filling the air with cheers
and road-metal about. They howled because
their fathers howled before them, and their
fathers howled because their grandfathers
howled, and what their grandfathers were
howling about had been quite forgotten.
Ed. Bulletin, — Re the cheap nigger. A mass
meeting of Kanakas at Geraldton (Q.), not long
ago, decided to form a Union, and resolved that
no “ boy ” is to sign under £36 a year. Which
goes to prove that the Kanaka is not the
danger to Australia. His supply is limited,
anyway, and he is rapidly dying out; and,
from an economical view-point, this latest move
ought to about abolish Ids usefulness altogether.
£36 a year is nearly 14s. a week, and as the
farmer can procure any amount of white labor
for 155., and the tucker is the same ; also, as
the white hand has to pay his own hospital exs.
when “that tired feeling” comes on, and re-
ceives no pay during absence from work, and
gets the sack in the slack season, or when it
rains too much, or too little ; also, as the white
hand does about three times the amount of
work his black brother condescends to perform,
and doesn’t run in a mob with cane-knives after
his employer, and for many other weighty
reasons too numerous to mention, he, the white
hand aforesaid, turns out to be the true “cheap
labor,” after all. — Von Kotze.

Horace Tozer, in a blazing oration at Gynvpie,
gibed at the Q.N. Bank Commission —the one
which dug out the awful iniquities of the rotten
and bankrupt institution- —because it was paid
for its services. Even Tozer ought to have
dropped down dead of shame before he fired off
that remark. He is the political partner of
Nelson and Barlow who declared that they
had made an exhaustive examination of the in-
solvent wreck, and solemnly staked their honor
on its soundness. He was, till the other day,
the political partner of MTlwraith, who got
away with such enormous sums of the
Q.N.B. depositors’ money. And he is
the political partner of Nelson who is
reported as having asked why M'llwraith
shouldn’t have taken that historic overdraft, if
he could get one—in other words, why he
shouldn’t have made away with other people’s
money, if he was allowed to do it. A man who
can live in such an atmosphere has no right to
complain of any commission taking payment
openly and honestly for its services. In fact,
he would have no right to complain if the com-
mission had bolted with all that remained of
the bank.
T.D. : “There is no convincing average people
that the great war (when it happens) will be
fought on different lines from those of 100 years
back— Nelson and his half-naked tars are still
quoted to show that Britain rules the waves.
But the present navy is altogether untried.
Not one effective vessel has ever been tested—
except by running her aground—whilst 90 out
of every hundred pasty-faced navy-boys have
never faced fire, and those who have were
engaged in nigger-chasing sorties. As for the
man in command, Tryon was the only senior
Admiral of late years with known stamina, and
Tryon’s dead. Beresford, Rawson and the
other dare-devils are all mere rear-admirals and
captains, whose only show is to play second
fiddle to some epauletted old lady.”
The law seems to delight in making a bigger
ass of itself every day. At Flemington (Melb.)
police court on Feb. 8, Wm. Considine, for
assa,ult, battery, and attempted smash of
his wife, was bound over to keep the
peace for twelve months, in sureties of
£lOO altogether. Then defendant: “I have
no money to enter into a bond.” Bench :
“ Well, you will be imprisoned till you get it;
but not more than 12 months.” (Just as if he
could earn it in gaol.) Defendant: “That
means I shall have to go to gaol for a year.”
Exactly ! He will have to go to gaol for a year
—not for assaulting his wife, but because he
hasn’t got the use of a few pounds. Assault
isn’t half so awful a crime as poverty.

Editor Bulletin, —Re working of Factories
Act, it may be news to you that one of the
biggest manufactories in Sydney works on a
sort of sub-contract system. It’s this way.
The foreman, or boss of each department,
instead of receiving a fixed wage, has the option
of sending in a price for the work, and thus
becomes a middleman, and as he has conse-
quently to cut things as fine as possible the
wages of the men under him (paid by him and
not by the firm) have to suffer accordingly.
One man in this position has averaged from
£2O to £3O per week for years ! yet is too
miserable to keep a temporary hand on five
minutes longer than he can help ! When there
happens to be a rush of work, and a few men
are put on for a couple of hours, if this boss
can save a single quarter of an hour’s work it’s
—“ That’ll do for to-day !” as he puts his hand
in his pocket and chucks the money (about
9d. per hour for heavy toil) on the ground,
like a bone to a dog. After all,
though, the men don’t deserve better treat-
ment —the temporary hands for submitting to
it, and the permanent hands for crawling.
Some of these latter, after working all week
—Saturday afternoons too, and overtime in-
cluded, will go creeping round to the works on
Sunday trying to put in a few hours’ work for
odd jobs, thus doing the poor devils of casual
hands out of a few shillings ! Finally, it is
significant that the department in these works
where the men have the shortest hours and the
highest pay is not worked on this infernal sub-
contract system.— Worker.
The recent stoppage of European cables, through
interruption of telegraph lines between Port
Darwin and Adelaide and also between Mel-
bourne and Sydney, should do much to urge the
construction of a line from Burketown (Q.) to
the station at Port Darwin. The two present
routes are the longest possible, and a message
destined to Sydney or Brisbane has either to
travel right through the continent to Adelaide,
and then along many hundred miles of coast-
line, or else to be transmitted by the still more
tedious alternative line from Roebuck Bay
(W.A.) to Perth, and so to Adelaide. Only the
fact that Port Darwin is in S.A. territory, and
the S.A. Govt, wants the pickings, could ever
block the extension from Burketown, which
would avert most of the inconvenience now
experienced.
The parochial party in N.S.W. politics was
very hard up for an advocate when the splashy
A. G. Taylor was induced to whoop up the
provincial aspect of the case in the columns of
the Star. The following slab shows Taylor
at his best:—
The Federal Constitution is tied to the following absurd
trinity of action Each colony must govern itself; each
colony must, in the Parliament of the Australian Com-
monwealth, govern its neighbors ; and each colony must,
by the same medium, be governed by its neighbors— all at
the same time. Is not the collapse of these conflicting
powers and interests likely to cause such a complicated,
contradictory and quixotic rule as never before prevailed
anywhere? Could it possibly last?
Adolphus G. Taylor evidently thought he had
discovered something quite new when he dug
out this fact, and yet if he had suddenly dis-
covered the existence of the ’bus-horse it would
have been about as wildly novel a find. The
United States is run on exactly that plan ; each
State governs itself (locally), and by its repre-
sentatives in Congress it helps (federally) to
govern the other States and they govern it.
Switzerland, Germany, Mexico, and other
States run on the same basis. So does
N.S.W. ; Sydney, Newcastle, and other places
govern themselves by their municipal councils,
and they also, through the local Parliament,
govern each other and are governed by each other.
The other provinces are in exactly the same
situation, only more so because they have more
complete local government systems. They
have all been in the same situation for many
years. Every country in Europe is in the same
situation, and some of them have been there
for nearly eight centuries. The spectacle of A.
G. Taylor, advancing this find as his own
perfectly new Federal horror, is the worst
example on record of the lean and slippered
ancestor receiving instruction about the egg.

vict-Gov. Phillip is buried, rushes open-
mouthed at N.S. W. for a monument to decorate

Phillip’s bones and stood them on end
in the chancel and inquired severely
whether they weren’t disgusted with N.S.W.’s
neglect. Also, the rev. Fish doesn’t explain
how he ascertained that posterity wishes to be
reminded that the hangy vicegerent “ ever
stepped foot in Australia,” or show that
posterity cares one Scriptural rap about the
matter. Under these vague circumstances The
Bulletin surmises that the grievance begins
and ends with the rev. Fish’s yearning to have
the spending of somebody else’s money in
glorifying his sacred institoosh’n, and it de-
mands in a voice of thunder that the rev. Fish
should put his head in his font and ask the
sexton to pour water on it.
Clement Scott’s aspersions on theatrical mora-
lity, followed by the reproving perfeshunal
cry of “ Judas ! ” may be said to “ give away”
that greasy notice-writer in his critic capacity.
Scott writes as one intimately acquainted with
all sorts and conditions of mummers, and they
naturally protest against the bite of the serpent
they have taken to their bosom. “ What
business had Clement Scott, the critic, to
write about the private life of the mummers
he hobnobbed with ? ” asks the perfeshun.
What business had he with ’em at all, outside
of his own professional work ? the public would
like to know. Some years ago the London
Daily News man (Mowbray Morris?) ad-
mitted regretfully that his theatrical friend-
ships disqualified him from really impartial
criticism. The ideal critic, as he said, would
be an experienced playgoer, possessing a good
artistic perception, dramatic instinct, and a
good memory, who could frustrate all the
attempts of actors, actresses and managers to
push their “friendship” upon him. Mummer
friendliness and “respect” for notice-writers
is just the same thing as the fondness of any
young human animal for the uncle who carries
lollies in his pocket.
A gigantic job is evidently being worked some-
where in the decision of the Vic. defence
department to re-arm the local military forces
with the Martini-Eufield rifle. The present
arm is the Martini-Henry, a weapon good
enough in its way, but completely unfit to cope
with the latest implements of warfare. It was
therefore considered about time to substitute
an up-to-date rifle, and after solemn delibera-
tion the authorities had the brazen front to
recommend the almost equally obsolete Martini-
Enfield ! The reasons that apply to the rejec-
tion of the Martini-Henri also apply with
nearly equal force to the Martini-Eufield. The
latter arm is an improvement on the old weapon,
but a very slight improvement, and at the best
it would be just as useless against the long-
range, quick-firing guns with which other nations
are armed as the arquebus or shanghai. The
Canadian people, whom it was attempted to
bunco with the same old weapon, called in a
board of experts, who unanimously declared
against the Martini-Eufield ; and yet the Im-
perialist gang of swashbucklers in Victoria is
heard to-day shouting the praises of the
weapon, and raising objections to the Lee-Met-
ford. As a matter of fact, there is not the
slightest reason why the rejection of the Mar-
tini-Enfield should mean the acceptance of the
Lee-Metford. The Ashton magazine rifle,
locally owned and locally financed, has been
proved, on the word of those same swash-
bucklers, to be superior in every respect—
accuracy, speed, and durability—to either of
the other weapons.

Miss Sutherland, directress of the Vic. Neg-
lected Children’s Society, says that few decent
girls take advantage of the Women’s Hospital
for maternity purposes, and that nine-tenths
of the patients belong to the lowest class of
women. This she ascribes to the “ committee ”
system, which is the rule at the institution
named. Every applicant for admission in her
time of trouble has to come before a committee
of “ society ” women and be subjected to a
series of questions regarding her “fall” and
the details thereof. Only the most innocent
and the most shameless women brave this
ordeal, the majority preferring, when the
gentle abortionist fails, to seek some wretched
suburban hovel, and, with her last few shil-
lings, hire some drunken harridan to “see her
through.”

giving unemployed clerks a chance, isealling in pen-
sioners for temporary work at =£Bo a year in addi-
tion to pensions payable . This is characteristic of
Turner.
