The new nautical drama of “ Nancy and
Her Sailor ” was presented at Sydney Lyceum on
Saturday, and a refreshing whiff of sea-breeze
blew from the painted-calico sea into a densely-
crowded house. The breeze came in handy, too,
for Saturday night at the Lyceum isn’t an over-
healthy function nowadays. The 2s. d.c. grows
steadily more crowded and less select, and, on
Saturday, it chews peanuts something awful, and
breathes a powerful breath of fish and cheese at
the universe in general. Also, it stands up in its
excitement just when Diver is throwing the
butchered girl into the English Channel, and, on
Saturday, it stood up more than usual, and blotted
out a good deal of “ Nancy ” from the writer’s
view. There is a large wreck in “ Nancy ” : Diver
blows out the beacon on a stormy night so that
the steamer with his boss on board may be lost,
and thus prevent the old man coming ashore to
find out the shortage in the cash. A rather realistic
steamer labors ashore, and Diver, in a moment of
repentance, hauls a lone survivor on the beach.
Then finding it is the boss, he bangs his head
against the rocks and throws him in again, after
taking all his money.
* * • *
Theieafter Diver leads a bloodstained ex-
istence, which is just visible through a driving
mist of pea-nuts. He leads Maud Williamson
astray, which is nothing unusual for that lady,
who must be one vast blue bruise through fainting
in many dramas when the same bad man declines
to make her an honest woman. (He was just
refusing to make her an honest woman on Satur-
day when the writer’s attention was distracted by
a friendly person offering him a pea-nut.) He also
slaughters her beside a rustic bridge, in the pre-
sence of Lilian Wheeler, who is walking in her
sleep, dressed in white, and carrying her bedroom
candle, so that it won’t combust the mosquito-
curtains in her absence. Likewise, he accuses
Miss Wheeler of the murder and has her run in ;
and later he makes good time round the room
after her with a carving-knife, or a pea-nut, or
something; and he ruins everybody, and is an
awful desperado in a soft hat until something
happens that was completely hidden on Saturday
by the sudden standing up of two people, with
their mouths full of pea-nuts, and a girl with a
frayed fowl in her hat. But the man who had
been accommodating a girl on his knee leaned
across the writer to tell a man with a short
jacket, and a soft hat which he kept on
all the time, and a shock head and a
powerful gin fragrance, that “Diver was an
alright bloke.” And the communication which
the man with the girl on his knee made to
the shock-headed man is endorsed by The Bul-
letin. The person addressed took the same view
and said Diver wasn’t too dashed horrid, and
blew a quantity »£ pea-nut shells on to the floor.
* * • *
The general impression left by “Nancy”
is not at all unfavorable, though it is only a
general impression—a lot of the small details
being blotted out by the distracting influence of
pea-nuts. In addition to the raging villain above
mentioned, Johnston Weir, as an eccentric tight
person devoted to agriculture, leaves a good effect
behind him. It is an unusual thing for Johnston
W. to drop into comedy, but he drops with excel-
lent results. Frank Harcourtand Stirling Whyte
are two reliable stand-bys, and are never seri-
ously misplaced. Miss Maud Williamson falls
creditably in the long brown cloak of t’ e Lost
Girl, though too much pea-nut obscures some of
her efforts ; and Miss Lilian Wheeler is a pretty
Nancy who hugs her returned sailor picturesque y
by a pale-green sea on which Brown of Lon on has
just been not at all well in a boat. Possibly it was
the pea-nuts which upset Brown of London.
How Brown came to be on the sea at all the
writer doesn’t know, for he came back from a
brief absence to find his seat jumped by a person
with cheap hair-oil on his head, and while he was
asking for the return of his seat, and being
informed that the oiled person would see him
blazing first, he missed this point. He isn’t even
certain that it was Brown.
*# * *
That old pale-green establishment, Sydney
Opera House, re-opened, according to promise,
under the management of Stanford and Barnes.
The principal result, so far, is that the ill-luck of
the old York-street curse still keeps up its end of
the log vigorously. On an average night the new
venture is sickbed o’er with the pale cast of
.£3 7s. 6d., or something that looks like that, and
if things got very much worse, and the manage-
ment went out to see a dog after the show, and it
was a good-sized dog, it would about absorb the
takings. Yet, considered in the light of prices
that range from Is. fid. down to 6d., the present
version of “ Shamus O’Brien” is a deserving
show. It would probably be rushed, at these
strictly moderate prices, if it were not that the
public is just about as likely to rush the Opera
House nowadays as old Sunday Brunker is to go
on the burst at Chowder. The public has abso-
lutely forgotten the establishment. Yet the
Opera House has at least the merit of being one
of the few remaining Sydney theatres whose cur-
tain doesn’t call on the public to drink somebody’s
bile corn-plaster at fid. each when it has that tired
feeling, or to hit the kitchen cockroach with
Brown’s patent club and beware of cheap imita-
tions which haven’t the name on the bottle.
• • • •
“ Shamus O’Brien ” is a reasonably good
thing in the way of Irish drama, and the Stanford-
Barnes co. has some good points. There is a
certain tail to it, but here and there a refreshing
pinnacle of merit rises above the dead waste of
eighteen pence. C. R. Stanford is a tall, imposing
rebel who now and then does a patch of really
good work. Phil Walsh’s informer is red and
crawlsome and impressive and beastly ungrateful.
Also Kate O’Reilly, represented by Miss Alice
Senior, stands out conspicuously as a bright and
clever atom of Irish humanity. Miss Senior
doesn’t need to be judged by the eighteenpenny
standard ; her performance would be good even
if she were in much better company. Miss
Theresa Lindahl, the tall and statuesque heroine,
leaves a respectable impression, but, on the whole,
The Bulletin considers it better to hold her over
for further consideration. And, unless the
management can lift the curse off the ancient
green theatre by prayer and incantation, it looks
as if there would be very little time for further
consideration. The writer has a vague recollec-
tion that a black cat boiled without vegetables on
a moonless night over the grave of an unbaptised
infant has been held to be good in such cases.
* * * #
Wilson Barrett makes his first appearance
before a Sydney audience on Saturday week (sth
March) in the “Manxman.” This is a kind of
disappointment to Sydney, which has been yearn-
ing for more stately Roman legs ever since Marcus
Superbus trailed his biled toga over Her Majesty’s
stage, and there was a general hope that the new
hero would open in “Claudian.” It is generally
understood that there are many Roman legs in
“Claudian.” However, the public will wait with
what patience it can raise for the appearance of
that ancient person of wealth and bad character.
In the meantime, the “ French Maid is pursuing
her butterfly way as usual, and her gauzy wings
look very pretty in the gaslight and the glare of
the johnny’s opera-glass.
* * * *
The second Albani concert is fixed for
Saturday, at Sydney Town Hall, and the third
and last of the season is due for next Tuesday.
* * # *
Rickards’s valuable entertainment at Syd-
ney Tivoli is much the same as last week. The
astonishing Hegelmanns do the same astonishing
things as before, and, when these grow stale, the
three break out in a fresh place. And the fresh
place which they break out at is just as surprising
as the place where they made the previous break-
age.
* * * *
The silver coin enterprise at Sydney Royal
Standard has been taking a turn at that venerable
whisky drama, “Ten Nights in a Bar Room.” It
is a long time since this moral snake-horror was
seen in Sydney, and it is also a lontr time since
Sydney had enough loose cash to spend one really
wet night in a bar-room—let alone ten. The
management which would put up “Ten Nights in
a Bar Room ” in these times is lost to all sense of
the fitness of things.
* * * *
Melbourne theatres are having an unex-
pected boom-time just now, owing to the simul-
taneous re-appearance of two old, reliable melo-
dramas—“ Silver King ” and “ The Sliaughraun.”
Thousands of people who seldom go to a theatre
are attracted by this combination of familiar
names, reckoning that if they can’t find room at
one house they can fall back on the other. The
situation is favorable to the prospects of the Cart-
wright co. at the Bijou. The large proportion of
public that seldom reads theatrical advts. is just
now devouring them eagerly, and a desire for
unwonted dissipation is in the air of the church-
going world, so to speak. The rush for “Silver
King ” is easily explained. The name-part is
everywhere associated with the managing actor
who first put it on the stage. He has had years of
preliminary advt. saving up for him in Australia
where references to his identity with the character
are always getting into print. Even the police-
court reports have helped to call attention to the
matter. A prisoner or witness with long, white
hair will be likened to Wilson Barrett in the
“ Silver King.” It is a solid celebrity.
* * * *
“The Squire of Daines,” which opened
the new season at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday
night, is a conversational comedy adapted from
the French by R. O. Carton, the author of
“ Liberty Hall.” It is a pale, dainty-looking narra-
tive of how a grass-widow was more than half
inclined to go astray with the inevitable baronet
until she found salvation in the friendship of the
name-character. The Squire aforesaid is a leisured
bachelor, who combines the social harmlessness
of a tame admirer with a valuable insight
into other people’s amours and a particu-
lar talent for restoring dames to their lost hus-
bands ere anything shocking has occurred. He
represents the Parisian idea of all that is noblest
in human nature. Oscar Wilde, in a virtuous
mood, might have written such a play as this, and
would probably have made it rather more interest-
ing. “The Squire of Dames ” is bright and
clever, and will get a good word from the more
advanced “B. and B.” section of the public, but
the germ of a great Australian popularity is not
in it.
* * * a
First impressions were of first importance
to the ne,w Bijou enterprise, and the management
had bad luck in getting an unduly sotto-voce per-
formance on Saturday. Only three members of
the company—Charles Cartwright, Miss Darragh,
and Hans Phillips—were sufficiently loud to be
always audible. The others (strangers, all of
them) seemed to have found a bad spot in the
pitch of the theatre, and their voices broke to leg,
or somewhere, instead of coining straight at the
audience. This was only a first-night defect, of
course, but it was likely to prejudice the second
night’s attendance. Charles Cartwright’s voice
is the same ringing organ of sound that it
used to be, yet mellower and more flexible.
Also his manner is more suave and pleasing, and
the dramatic force that marked him in “The
Middleman ” of yore l is doubtless ready to come
with a rush when called for. There is no call for
Cartwrightian emotion in “The Squire of Dames.”
The strong actor merely plays the central part in
an easy, graceful manner for all it is worth. Miss
Beatrice Lamb, as the wife who listens to good
advice, promised to let herself go, in a dramatic
sense, several times during Saturday evening,
but inasmuch as the leading lady didn’t loose her
hold upon herself, The Bulletin will wait for an
opportunity to say how far she can go when she
gets the better of her self-restraint. At a first
glance, Miss Lamb appears to be an impertur-
bable actress—a very quiet reminder of the
unfortunate Amy Roselle. Miss Gethyn Darragh
plays a Yankee heiress with great feeling and a
refreshingly correct accent; likewise, successful
first appearances are scored by Messrs. Arthur
Styan and Hartley Manners. The baffled baronet
is treated in a strangely unsatisfactory style by
Chas. Thursby, who may, however, cut an effec-
tive figure presently in a less awkward part. Miss
Katherine Stewart and the non-imported Hans
Phillips fill a pair of irrelevant characters, and
little Miss Noni Rickards appears as their
daughter. Miss Noni, being at present a very
young amateur, is much too young even for the
small part of a damsel who loves a lordling’s beard.
“The Squire of Dames” will be matineed on
Saturday.
** * *
“ The Silver King ” proved a mighty boom
at Melb. Princess’s last week, wherefore the
management withdrew its announcement of “ Six
Nights Only” and finally decided to run the
original Denver for a fortnight. The original
Denver, as The Bulletin remarked last week,
was bound to boom, and the management’s failure
to understand the business possibilities of “Wilson
Barrett as the Silver King ” seems extraordinary to
this journal, notwithstanding that this journal has
recorded the unwisdom of showmen in general for
many years past. “ Silver King ” might as well
run to the end of the season, which happens next
Wednesday, but the programme for the last nights
is “ Hamlet ” (Saturday and Monday), “ Othello ’’
(Tuesday), and one more consecutive production
of “ Virginius ’’ for the co.’s last appearance. That
long-winded Roman father is seemingly Wilson
Barrett’s favorite character.
** * *
“ The Sliaughraun ” is putting in a festive
two weeks at the Royal, thanks to the genial
humor of Grattan Riggs and the all-round quality
of a popular-priced show, and the overflow from
the Princess’s, likewise the change in the weather.
Twelve nights of Boucicault’s old drama will not
exhaust its financial strength, judging from recent
indications, but, all the same, “ The Irish Detec-
tive ” is said to be coming on Saturday. A few
years have joined the majority since Grattan
Riggs’s last outbreak in his old round of detective
characters at Melbourne, and this revival may
consider itself called for.
** * *
There is nothing in particular to say of
“ Jack the Giant Killer,” at the Opera House,
except that the usual eight performances are being
crowded into each returning week, and that Will
Whitburn will join the company on Saturday
next. “Jack” is doing well, for reasons pre-
viously stated and generally understood.
** * *
The Alexandra’s five-act shocker for the
present week is “ Under the Gaslight,” and Dan.
Barry’s unrivalled stage locomotive is providing
some more entertainment for the masses. Another
drama of domestio interest will unfold its gory
length as soon as possible. The first series of
Continental Concerts in the Exhibition quad-
rangle is to close on Saturday, but if Melb. gives
evidence of a growing demand for ballad singing
in the balmy air a second series will follow.
** * *
Next Friday afternoon (25th) the theatrical
forces of Melbourne give a large combination show
for the benefit of the Distressed Actors’ Fund,
which deserving institution, in the natural course
of things, gets compelled to cry aloud for Cash at
regular intervals. Five theatres will contribute
the best of their “talent” to the mixed bill, and
the public is cordially exhorted to book seats right
away.
** * *
At Hobart Royal “ The Two Little Vaga-
bonds ” drew a racing carnival audience for several
nights and paid a fair dividend to the Firm. As
“ boys ” the Misses Titheradge and Terriss were
somewhat unconvincing, Sweeney and Oily Deer-
ing made very fair specimens of villains, while
Julia Merton, in Biddy Mullins’ drunken croon of
“ Mavourneen,” fetched the gods with an amazing
fetch. As Marion Thornton, Miss Emilie Hughes
got along sympathetically, despite her inability to
call her purse anything but a “ pus ”; curse,
“cuss”; and worse, “ wuss.” Regardless of many
Warnings, Frank Thornton opened on Thursday
at the Royal with Miss Brown, and threatens to
perpetrate one or other of his queeriosities while
he can get a bun and a glass of milk.

I think the nil admirari pose is a reaction from too much
admiring. The Australian is impulsive, and almost in-
variably expects the impossible. When he gets only the
possible—no matter how good it is—he grumbles, and
stays that way. But he doesn’t begin by grumbling : his
initial enthusiasm is tremendous.”

Somebody sends Tub Bulletin this paragraphic state-
ment : “ Actor Wilson Barrett will be 41 years of age
during next May.” We print it in all seriousness, and

Crummies, the theatrical gentleman in “ Nicholas
Niekleby ” who never could understand how such thim>s
get into the papers. Actor Wilson Barrett will be 41
years of age during next May. Likewise during March
and April, no doubt.
The story of the first meeting of Albani with the Gye
family (Ernest Gye is now her husband, and is with her
on her Australian tour) is very curious. Col. Mapleson
(who tells the yarn) had engaged Albani when a very
young singer to warble for his Italian opera season in
London. When she first arrived there she directed a
cabman to drive her to the Italian Opera. The man drove
her to the Itoyal Italian Opera. She went to the
manager’s room, said her name was Albani, and that she
had come to sign the contract. The manager said “ Very
handed her a printed contract form to sign.
When she had put her name to it he told her that he
wasnt Mapleson but Gye. There was, he said, “a
manager named Mapleson who ran a theatre somewhere
round the corner, where operas and other things were
from time to time performed, but the opera, the perma-
nent institution known as such, was the one he had the
honor of conducting.” He told her that, if she liked, he
would tear up the contract she had just signed, and leave
her free to go to Mapleson, but he was willing to offer her
her own terms. As it happened, Mapleson was willing to
release her, so she stayed with Gye, and subsequently
became Mrs. Ernest Gye.
S.S. : “ Old friend Grattan Riggs—what a treat he is in
a back parlor ! —is rather too bulky and too far on the
other side of the hill to act the part of Conn; but, apart
from a slight throat trouble, how beautifully he talked
Boucicault’s fine stuff, the other night. I have seen many
pretty Conns, this is the only Irish one—Irish all through,
in his impertinence, and his deference with the devil in
it. Conn set me thinking of Mulvaney. It seems to me
that a modicum of Kipling’s advertised marvellous
divination may have come from Boueicault. But Mul-
vaney would be a treat in the hands of a good comedian
—who would not be glad to see and hear any such tell
the story of ‘The Big Drunk Draf,’ or ‘My Lord the
Elephant,’ as a ‘ turn ’ at a benefit ? ”
While Wilson Barrett revives the “ Silver King,” in
Melb., Arthur Garner, the old original and unsurpassed
“ Spider,” is running the only show on Klondyke—“ Ten
Nights in a Bar-Room ” alternately with “ East Lynne.”
E.D. : “Surely record business was done at Melb.
Princess’s during the early part of last week? Walking
up Bourke-street, at about a quarter to 8, on Wednesday
evening, I encountered a stream of battle-stained, more-
or-less bedraggled and vehement people, all furious at
haying failed to beat their way into the theatre after
being gouged, and crushed, and scrambled over by the
madding crowd, and all seeming to cherish a bitter
grievance against the management because the theatre
was not 15 sizes bigger. The charge up the back stairs
for 2s. seats has never been equalled since Waterloo, the
fights for wicket in Bernhardt’s time being comparatively
mild and bloodless. Nicely-dressed women come through,
tremulous wrecks, bleeding faces being almost as frequent
as battered hats up aloft. Every available gallery-seat
was taken up at 100 per cent, advance, and, down below,
hundreds had to be emptied out of the stalls, some being
accommodated with standing-room in the circle, and
others turned empty away. This is a mild statement of
facts ; as for the why and wherefore, I can only say that
sometimes the public is more like a mob of sheep than
others, and this is one of the times.”
Haresfoot : “Barrett’s Pete in ‘The Manxman’ is the
ne plus ultra of the domestic. Such a kind of thing was
lifted out of the clamps of the Real into the limitless
Ideal by Jefferson’s Rip, and Emmet’s Fritz. I happened
to be much about the theatre during an Emmet season.
Look at his performance any time and it w'ould clutch
you, but you only fail to see Barrett’s wires the first
time.”
In the new Sydney Lyceum drama of “Nancy” there
was a subdued laugh all round, the first night, when
Lilian Wheeler found Maud Williamson murdered, and
cried out “Too late!” It set everybody thinking of
Burke’s wail of “Two shillings” in the recent Majesty’s
panto.
There is a scrupulous etiquette in the musical profes-
sion. Some years ago both Albani and Patti were in New
York. One morning, Albani and husband Gye were
standing at their hotel-window, when they saw Patti and
husband Nicolmi drive by. “ Ernest,”said Albani, “they
are gone out; we must go and call at once.” In the
evening Patti said to her husband : “ Ernest ” (he was an
Ernest also), “ they’re gone to the theatre by now ; let us
go and leave cards at the hotel.”
“ A Regular Attendant” writes to the Argus :—“ I do
not see ‘The Sign of the Cross’ mentioned as one of
Wilson Barrett’s last performances in Melbourne, and I
would like to know whether we are to have the pleasure
of seeing Mr. Barrett in this special play, as I do not
think I am far out in saying hundreds of people besides
myself are living in anticipation of doing so. Trusting
we will not be disappointed.” How like a Regular At-
tendant !
Melba threatens to visit Australia “in July or August.”
Australian girls will probably divide their kind regards
between Albani and the tall, handsome new-comer,
William Pauli, baritone.
“ We have travelled several thousands of miles to sing
to about half the prices readily obtained in London,” re-
marked Ernest Gye, husband of Albani, on hearing that
7s. fid. was to be the highest charge for admission,
Rickards tried on the early-door dodge at Melb. Bijou,
but it didn’t come off. The ticket-seller soon had his cage
surrounded by a chaffing crowd who calmly war ed off
any promising sixpences. This went on tili 7.45, when
the man retired from the struggle and a free fight for
shilling tickets began. “Ladies first, please.” “No,
ladies be damned !”
L.P.W. :—“One of the musical critic’s choicest privi-
leges is to be able to slate artists who give the public
what they like instead of what they ought (according to
the critic) to like. Melbourne Argus musical man, for
instance, holds in lofty scorn the performance on the
Town Hall organ of popular operatic and other selections.
Nothing but Bach fugues and Mendelssohn sonatas for
this painfully superiah person. Poor David Lee was
railed at foryears, and then altogether ignored, because he
persisted in giving his audiences the music they liked.
Recently, Charles Sykes, organist of St. Pat’s Cathedral,
got badly slated for playing music which delighted one of
the largest audiences that has attended the Town Hall
recitals for many a year past. Charles scored heavily,
though, with a similar programme the following week,
drawing an even bigger ‘ house' and receiving tumult-
ous applause. Let the Argus rave as it will, the musically
unregenerate will persist in being delighted with the
strains of ‘Faust’ and ‘Lucia,’ of ’Pinafore’ and ‘Madame
Angot.’ It’s a weary world for the critic who sets out to
‘ educate the people.’ ”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
A big Sydney bubble burst last week. On Feb.
® on the information of one of Australia’s most capable
patent-agents—Bulletin pointed out that investors might
‘ So slow ” re an alleged invention for making inflam-
mable gas out of ordinary air, which didn’t look too well
guaranteed. Since then the two “ inventors ” have fled
with detectives after them, and with, apparently, about
£oooo of dupes’ cash in their pockets. The third applicant
for patent was, it seems, honestly diddled like the rest.
The modus was absurdly simple. Something in
the guise of a machine was produced, into which a certain
amount of sulphuric ether was poured, and while promin-
ent citizens gaped O! O! O! a gas-stove and an engine
and other trifles were apparently run with a minimum of
expense and bother. Then the prominent citizens rushed
to purchase shares—and to tell the good thing to their
friends—with astonishing results. The co. was to be
floated in 1600 £lO-shares —1200 of which the “inventors”
took fully paid-up, though 400 were to remain in trust for
the time being. Four hundred shares were offered to the
public—and of the proceeds the “inventors” were to
mu 6 ****' *lOOO going- for patent and flotation expenses.
The public simply tumbled, over one another in eager-
ness to buy. The shares were all taken up instanter, and
began changing hands at £3O, £6O, even up to 190, and
still there were not half enough shares for all the people
who vyanted to get in on the ground-floor of “ the greatest
llluminant of the century.” And all the time two of the
“ inventors” had a neat little pipeattached to the machine,
through which gas or gasolene was conveyed wholesale
from next-door, wherewith to work the marvels.
Only one cute individual seems, being a
chemist, to have smelt a rat. One of the ingredients of
the marvellous generating-compound was citronella-oil—
something with a strong smell—and the chemist imme-
diately drew the conclusion that the smell was intended
to cover up the stink of the gas or the kerosene, on which
tne inventors really relied. This, considering- the boom, and
the fever, and the names which backed the “ invention,”
argues considerable acumen on the chemist’s part. Any-
way, there has been more swearing in Sydney speculative
circles last week than an ordinary, average-sized gehenna
would hear in a month. The two “ inventors ” (Lee and
Elliott) fled to Wellington, M.L., and took ship on s.s.
Aorangi, bound for’Fr's io via Honolulu, where an attempt
to intercept them will be made. Lee is a Yank—full name
George Washington Lee—and the omen duly came off 1
•lake Garrard, N.S.W.’s Minister for Edu-
cation, himself a mechanic with a Mort’s Dock train-
ing, was one of the victims ; and the account of his flab-
bergastment when the cat came out is rich indeed.
Apparently Minister Garrard, after the flight of the in-
ventors, had been experimenting with the invention, and
there was an explosion in the room in Vickery’s Chambers
where the Light of the Age hung out. Proprietor
Vickery went to investigate, and saw “ a man with his
coat off, his shirt sleeves turned up, standing in an ab-
stracted fashion near a generator. There was, Mr.
Vickery believes, another man in the other room. But
Mr. Vickery’s attention was caught by the attitude of the
first experimentor. He was in the position of a man who
looks but sees not, and who is having his mind led by
easy gradations to the comprehension of a profound idea,
or is but half-conscious of occupying a ridiculous situa-
tion. ‘ What do you mean, sir ?’ demanded Mr. Vickery,
fresh with the idea that the whole of his beautiful block
of buildings might have been sacrificed by the uncertain
researches of an amateur experimentor with gases, ‘by
using my rooms to experiment in? Out of the place at
once, sir. Pack up ybur things, and put them in the
street.’ The unknown, to Mr. Vickery, experimentor was
by this time coming to, and the interview was somewhat
abruptly ended by a member of the fire brigade, who had
stepped in, asking Mr. Garrard if he was hurt. “MR.
GARRARD t !!” said Mr. Vickery, “why, I didn’t know
you!”
Marvel is that when they found Sydney’s
biggest heads so eager to be taken in, the amateur John
Laws didn’t make their bubble ten times as big. Majah
Neild, who was induced to act—quite honestly and
uncomprehendingly, of course—as decoy-duck, and re-
ceived £3OO for his services, has actually a current suit in
equity claiming 60 shares in the invention—which were
promised to him, but never handed over, because, it was
said, he didn’t have to do any work to get the money ; it
simply rolled in on top of him !
By the way, it is said that Minister Garrard and
Col. P. B. Walker, Superintendent of N.S.W. Telegraphs,
who acted as trustees between shareholders and “ in-
ventors,” handed over the latter’s whack when patent was
applied for—instead of, according to instructions, when
patent was granted — so there may be more trouble in
store.
* * * *
Last year’s trade returns showed a large in-
crease in N.S.W. commerce, and how much good that
increase did to the province may be gathered from these
figures:—
Imports. ’ Exports. E E “ ts ° f
1891 .. £25,383,397 £25,944,020 £560,623
1892 .. 20,776,526 21,972,247 1,195,721
1893 .. 18,107,035 22,921,223 4,814,188
1894 .. 15,801,641 20,577,673 4,776,032
1895 .. 15,992,415 21,934,785 5,942,370
1896 -.. 20,561,510 23,010,349 2,448,839
1897 „ 21,744,350 23,602,991 1,858,641
N.S.W. has to -export about £4,000,000 a year more
than it imports so as to pay interest on its * foreign
debt. If it doesn’t do this it is getting further
in debt and growing more and more bankrupt.
It can only do it by increasing its exports, which
seems impossible at present, or by doing with less
imports. In the brief Protectionist period it did with
less imports and paid its way. The “great expansion of
trade” which attended the glorious Reid policy was
mainly an expansion of imports—not of exports. In other
words, the expansion consisted in the country starting to
buy a lot more than it could pay for, and the recent
resumption of the old device of floating loans in London
was the natural result. The free and generous process of
buying more than the country could pay for was reducing
the local gold-supply so near panic point that something
had to be borrowed in London to keep the game afloat.
» * * »
An insolvency case, heard the other day at Mel-
bourne, goes to show that the nevv Vic. law, if it is
decently enforced, has its advantages. The pity is that
the first man it fell upon was a small German owing £l5O.
The German applied for his discharge on the ground that
three-fourths of his creditors had agreed to accept 6d. in
the £. Judge Molesworth replied that under the old Act
anything which three-fourths of the creditors accepted
was binding on the rest—therefore, an insolvent could,
if he chose, give back-dated p.n’s to all his reliable
friends, and thus constitute them creditors, and they
could accept anything in the £, and compel the
real creditors to do likewise. The new Act, however,
gave the Court power to refuse its sanction to any
arrangement which seemed inequitable, and the Judge
therefore fired the small German out of court. Two
weeks later, the insolvent suddenly found that he could
pay 7s. in the £, and Molesworth put him through on
that basis. But, alas ! almost all the million-pound lions
of the boom have got their discharges under the old, easy
system, and the new law only chews up the mice that are
left.
* • * *
The Western Australian Bank closed its half-
year at 31st Dec., 1897, with a very handsome profit of
£18,251. This equals 36J per cent, per annum on capital,
yet, all the same, it looks as if the great and sudden boom
of the Westralian Bank was on the turn. The following
are the profits for eight half-years:—
June, ’94 .. £8,387 June, ’96 .. £12,284
December, ’94 .. 9,623 December, ’96 .. 16,703
June,’9s .. 10,315 June,’97 .. 19,685
December, ’95 .. 10,972 December, ’97 .. 18,251
The present is, therefore, the first half-year since the great
boom set in when the profits have shown a decline. Still,
the Westralian Bank has done well, on paper anyhow,
out of the boom. It still keeps its dividend at 17J per
cent. (£8750 for the half-year), which is large enough cer-
tainly, though nothing compared to what it might have
been of late, and has raised its reserve in three years from
£lOO,OOO to £175,000. It has also £7861 left at profit, so
there is an accumulation of nearly £183,000, and a capital
of £lOO,OOO at the back of the little institution. It took
the Westralian Bank 51 years to accumulate the first
£BO,OOO of that reserve, and the other £95,000 was piled
up in 5| years—so things have gone Well with it in recent
times.
Other signs of the fading boom are: The note-
circulation has decreased £7OOO in the last half-year.
Deposits have decreased nearly £200,000 in the half-year,
and £275,000 in the year. Advances are £20,000 less than
they were six months ago, and cash (£614,000) has
diminished £170,000. Still the cash is equal to 35
per cent, of all liabilities to the public, which is a very big
reserve. The Westralian Bank never publishes an audi-
tors’ certificate—so far as this paper knows the accounts
have never been audited in the orthodox fashion in all its
56 years’ existence. This doesn’t really matter much,
considering that every auditor The Bulletin knows of has
always alleged that the accounts, however awful, were
quite right “according to the books.” Still, when one
considers how horribly a bank can go wrong when it is
audited, there is a certain interest in speculating how
much worse it may, or may not, go wrong when it isn’t.
* * * *
An arrangement is now under way by which
the Queensland Mutual Insurance co. sells out its business
to the Manchester co.—which will make the third Aus-
tralian fire office absorbed by a British co. within the last
two months. In 1889 there were 33 Australasian fire
offices in existence, and the number is now down to 16.
Unless an absentee-tax strikes the foreign fire-insurance
concern heavily on the jaw, the absorbing process will
apparently go on till this once-flourishing Australian in-
dustry is extinct. And the foreign fire-insurance co.—
which doesn’t necessarily bring any money worth speaking
of to this country, or bring anything, in fact, except its
name and its credit, and which, if it has ordinary luck,
merely opens an office, and humps away the profits—is a
really-deserving subject for taxation. The Queensland
Mutual was a promising little concern, nine years old.
It paid 8 per cent, dividends very steadily, and had
£15,500 paid-up capital and nearly £lO,OOO reserves. Ap-
parently, however, the loss of £3OOO or so in the Melbourne
fire scared this small affair out of its boots, and induced it
to sell out just when it was beginning to attain a respect-
able size and a good position.
Somebody makes a big rise out of the business,
anyhow. The shares (ss. paid) were lately down to about
3s. 6d. Certain persons in the know have been buying
them up lavishly at or about this figure—and, as the Man-
chester co. (so the report goes, anyhow) is to pay 10s. a
share, these knowing people should make a good profit.
Apparently, the chief thing that the miserable ordinary
shareholder pays a director for is not to tell him, till after
he has parted with his shares, that there is any special
reason why he should keep them.
* ♦ * *
The statement that M.L. Government will in-
troduce, next session, a Bill providing for the compulsory
State audit of banks, insurance offices, &c., naturally
raises the question—will the Bill also provide for the com-
pulsory shutting-up of institutions which the State audit
proves to be unsound ? Seddon’s provinoe possesses, in the
Provident and Industrial Insurance co., the most curious
insurance concern in the Seven Provinces. This affair
makes its accounts square by including among its assets
£3562 of money spen t in .expenses, said money being put
in among the assets as a sort of suspense account to be
wiped-off by instalments. Its balance-sheet, when boiled
down (The Bulletin published it in a boiled-down state
in its issue of 18th December, 1897), shows that the reserve-
fund and the life-assurance fund have no real existence at
all, and £1239 of the miserable little £4875 capital is
apparently lost as well. In other words, it seems that all
the premiums have gone in losses and expenses, and a
good deal more besides. This paper, therefore, desires to
ask if the proposed State-audit system will be so con-
structed as to promptly round up concerns like the P. and
I. Considering that the Provident and Industrial does
business both in Sydney and Melbourne, the Vic. and
N.S.W. Governments might seriously consider whether
they don’t also need a State-audit system very badly.
• * * *
The N.Z. Accident Insurance Co., one of the
most consistently successful businesses in the Seven Pro-
vinces, shows a profit for 1897 of £6187, and £1894
brought forward from last half-year makes £Bl7l.
An office run on the lines of the Victoria Insur-
ance Co. of Melbourne might have considered this a
favorable opportunity to pay away £6OOO in a 30 per cent,
dividend, but the N.Z. Accident, being conducted on
different principles, pays 10 per cent. (£2000). It also
adds £6OOO to its reserves. The co. has now a paid-up
capital of £20,000, but the accumulated funds of the
fidelity, plate-glass and burglary insurance business —
which have not hitherto been taken' into the profit
account —have increased so largely that £5OOO is to be
transferred to capital, making it £25,000. It has also
£14,000 reserve-fund, and £lO,OOO reinsurance - fund.
The great bulk of the funds are invested in State
and municipal securities which are not liable to the
awful slumps that characterise the delusive mortgage
business, and the income from investments alone (£1839)
is sufficient to pay 9 per cent, on the present capital.
Altogether, the N.Z. Accident looks like the most fortu-
nate insurance concern in Australasia.

• * • #
Bung-bank ex-directors are invited to observe
that the lately-deceased Orlando Fenwick, formerly a
director of the Metropolitan Bank, instructed his trustees
to pay to his two nieces £lOO, being the amount which he
had induced them to commit to the keeping of the
rickety institution, and which sum he felt “ in honor
bound to refund.” They were his nieces, it is true ; also,
it was only £lOO, it is further true. Moreover, if they
hadn’t got the money this way they would have got most
of it in another, as they were among the principal bene-
ficiaries. In the circumstances it seems pretty clear that
Mr. Fenwick wanted to make a post-mortem suggestion
to other men who were in the same delirious old swim a
few years back. And, so far as it goes, the suggestion is
good enough ; but it is a chastening reflection that not
every victim of the confidence-trick of those days had an
uncle on the board.

Dear Bulletin, —Who is your correspondent,
“Sulphur”? Is he a practical smelter, or a “ pamphlet
metallurgist ” ? He refers to the Lake George mines ex-
traction being I*7B per cent., but does not say on what.
The public will generally understand that I'7B per cent,
on assay value is meant, when the figures actually mean
I*7B per cent, on tonnage of ore smelted, and not on
assay-value. Perhaps the superior knowledge and ex-
perience of “Sulphur” will enable him to suggest a
better method of treatment. — Shareholder.

Though new cases of lead-poisoning at Broken
Hill are now comparatively rare, the old poison still
Works. Mine-manager Rowe, of the Sulphide Corpora-
tion’s Central mine, has been forced to retire on account
of lead paralysis, though a long holiday was lately taken
in the hope of expelling the slowly-working virus.

* * * *
The White Cliffs Opal Mines, Ltd., was a very
quiet London float of ’96 —so quiet that until J. Bull had
actually been hooked very few on this side knew that the
deal was to be attempted at that time. When they did
know of it they waited in the full expectation (now just
realised) of hearing Bull roar. Opal-mining, as The Bul-
letin has said more than once, is more precarious even
than alluvial gold-getting ; for when you have got your
opal you have next to find your buyer. In its first year’s
operations the co. has found all this out; and it seems to
have come as a revelation. In fact, it isn’t quite realised
yet. Instead of the £30,000 a year profit alleged to have
been promised, the co.’s net loss is £1325, though the
office and incidental expenses, which have been prepos-
terous, partly account for that. As usual, instead of con-
fessing that it has been a great ass, the co. is inclined to
complain that its employes and tributors are great knaves,
who rob it of the best gems. The chairman of the co.
put it to the shareholders —“ There is no doubt a
leakage of fine opal exists and will exist until per-
mission is obtained to amalgamate the various leases,
so that labor can be concentrated and efficiently
supervised.” While local inquiries fail to sup-
port the charge that gem-stealing is common,
it nevertheless is perfectly true that supervision is difficult
at present. But there is the further point to consider
whether concentrated labor is suitable for opal-getting
any more than for kauri-gum digging. And, if it isn’t,
then it’s a question whether opal-getting is a suitable in-
dustry for a big co. to take in hand at all.
The London chairman of the White Cliffs Opal
Mines, Ltd., seems to have suggested to the shareholders
that the opal-buyers at White Cliffs are, for the greater
part, illicit dealers in gems stolen by workmen from the
co. “ They would not,” he added, “ suffer the inconveni-
ence of living in such a place unless they were making
large sums of money by buying opals there and sending
them to Europe, or elsewhere, for sale.” True. But the
co. doesn’t own all White Cliffs, or even a fourth of it.
The men who suffer are the miners working on their own
claims—men who, not being on a co.’s pay-sheet, have to
sell the gem almost as soon as they have raised it, and for
whatever a very select coterie chooses to pay for it.
Before the co. was formed, there was hope of the claim-
holders co-operating and sending the gems to Europe for
direct sale ; but the formation of the co. knocked the pro-
posal out, and ever since then the opal market has been
the despair of every digger—or fossicker ; for, generally
speaking, he is little more than that.

Copper-mining in South Australia : —Wallaroo
and Moonta mines, since their opening, have produced
over a million tons of ore ; copper to the value of
£9,218,482, and dividends £1,750,000. The old Burra
mine, from less than 250,000 tons of ore, produced over
£3,000,000 in copper, and paid over a million in divs.
* * * *
Napier Bell has reported re the Macquarie
Harbour (Tas.) bar scheme. His estimate of the cost is.
£219,000, and he reckons it will take nearly five years to
complete the works. His figures are slightly over those
of the local ditto (£50,000), and it is pretty safe to bet
that it will be much more than five years before the bar
is shifted. It is merely a local want, as in the near future
a line will carry all the stuff they can produce on the west
coast to deep water at Emu Bay. However, if the Hobart
crowd don’t get their Great Western Railway, they must
have another costly toy—deep water at Hell’s Gates.
Twelve miles of Emu Bay railway is now ready
for the rails ; and the biggest bridge, over the Mackintosh,
is to be commenced immediately. They seem to get up a
little earlier than the Slowbartians down there. Mean-
while the Western is not yet floated, and is spoken of.
under the breath, as a myth.

East Murchison United Mine is a consistent
producer. Has been crushing, night and day, over 16
months—machinery only stopped 10 working hours first
12 months —and has crushed, in all, 17,'. 13 tons for
18,000 oz. with 10 head of stamps.

At a certain W. A. gold-mining co.’s directors’
meeting at Coolgardie, in June, ’96 —present (say) Wilkins,
chairman ; Brown, Jones and Smith, directors; and
Robinson, secretary—it was proposed by Smith and
seconded by Jones that Brown should be allotted 1000
contributing shares at ss. as originally applied for.
Carried. Then proposed by Smith and seconded by
Brown that the directors and secretary should have the
privilege of taking up contributing shares, not exceeding
1000 each, to be paid for at the rate of ss. per share within
14 days from the sth June, ’96, or allotment cancelled.
These shares were taken up at dates from 4th to 10th
June, and the scrip, when issued, was antedated to
April 10, though previous scrip was dated May 24. Brown
took up 2000 (1000 on June 4, and 1000 on June 8).
Market price of shares on June 1,12 s. 3d. to 14s. ; 2nd,
14s. ; 3rd, 13s. to 14s. A delightful unanimity charac-
terised the meeting ; and magnanimity as well —in the in-
clusion of the secretary. The directorate didn’t want to
hog the lot. There’s a heap of good reading locked-up in
the minute-books of Westralian mining-companies.
♦ • * *
The rumor that Kaufman had rejoined the
London and Globe Finance co. has not been confirmed
and is probably incorrect. His reply to the secretary’s
statement that innoeventwould his engagement have been
renewed is forcible. —“ Every pressure has been brought
upon me to reconsider my decision, but I have persistently
declined to do so. I was then requested by Mr. Whitaker
Wright to proceed to British Columbia in the interests of
the British American Corporation, and also to negotiate
for the acquisition of certain W.A. properties belonging
to the Associated co. All these requests, however, I de-
clined to entertain. I trust that in the interests not only
of the L. & G. shareholders, but of the W.A. mining in-
dustry generally, the discussion may now end.” Good
advice, no doubt. After the sickening speech made by
Bottomley many persons probably imagined that Kaufman
would want to get back. A bit of it: - “ Those who
know us will say we have ridden a consistent, straight
and honest race in connection with W.A. We have given
full room and berth to all our opponents, with the result
that for some time, far away in front of the rest of the field,
we have been running a neck-and-neck race with one other
group, which I will not further particularise. But, now
that we have secured their first jockey in the person of
my friend on my right, I am certain, knowing the cattle
we have in our stables, knowing the form of our oppon-
ents to the ounce, that with Charles Kaufman up the
W.A. stakes are at our mercy.” “ A Disgusted Share-
holder ” wants to know if the person referred to in such
flattering and hysterical terms is the same O. Kaufman
who advised the purchase and flotation of the Wealth of
Nations, Paddington Consols, Hannan’s Golden Treasure,
Golden Crown, Mainland Consols, Wealth of Nations
Extended, and Hannan’s Golden Group ? If so, he would
like to know how he squares their present position and
market values with his own reports made on these pro-
perties ? Too bad ! Under the Hush ! Hush ! policy of
the L. and G. it was obviously intended that these babes
should pass away in a sweet, undisturbed sleep.

N.S.W. Govt, geologist Pittman says the Kal-
goorlie ore formation consists of dykes frequently inter-
sected by irregular quartz reefs, and he has little doubt
that deep leads of alluvial, the result of denudation
from these dykes, will be found on the field.

“Yankee shrewdness,” writes a malignant
Bulletin correspondent, “ must be—so far as Australia is
concerned —a myth ; writer thinks that said alleged
‘ shrewdness ’is merely a subtle advt. Of the 50 odd
Yankee firms which 10 years ago were going to ‘ corner’
various lines of business down North only two remain,
one with tobacco and timber monopolies, and the other—
Canadian, not Yankee —too ‘ near ’ to lose anything ex-
cept its breath and perspiration. In re the latter, a friend
of the Canuck’s asked him lately ‘ how he was getting oh ? ’
‘ I lost eight pounds,’ began the Canadian. ‘lmpossible,’
retorted the Australee man. ‘ln weight,’ interrupted the
Canuck. ‘Ah ! that may be so,’ concluded the local gent.
‘ I feared you were going to work a Yankee-notion lie on
me, and say you lost eight pounds in money. Which I
know is an utter impossibility.’ ”

A Bulletin par. to the effect that Scentral
Deddicks fell out of the Melb. market almost as soon as
they got there was responsible for an immediate “ move”
in the shares. As a demonstration that the Scentrals
had been not dead, but sleeping, somebody bought five
shares at 705., after which the stock relapsed into silence
for a week.
* « * *
Transpired at the last half-yearly meeting that
the Koch’s Pioneer Co., one of the most consistent div.-
payers of Bendigo, and owned by about the wealthiest
proprietary (Lansell, Atkinson, the Boyds, and co.), has
been paying its manager £3 a week! Also, the Koch’s
battery is one of the biggest crushers for the public in
the district, and its manager has been getting £3 a week,
too ! Now, after long debating among shareholders,
they’ve had a rise of a whole 10s. a week each. Also, the
directors’ fees are increased from £5O to £lOO a year for
the lot. Lansell participates in the latter extravagance.
This mine has upwards of 100 stamps, and has paid an
occasional div. from the profits of public crushing, as well
as developing the mine out of it. A million tons would
not exhaust the tailings heap, and it includes those from
the Kentish mine, from which Paddy Watson is known to
have taken nine tons of gold.
* * * *
In a recently-suppressed fraud, the “ firm ” —a
very big one in its line—threatened gaol unless the whole
shortage was made up. There was a laudable attempt
made by the wife to save her husband from hominy, but
even the sale of all her belongings left a big sum still
owing. It looked a “ blue duck ” when the man of mani-
pulation suddenly remembered that he held shares in a
local trading concern on which over £20,000 was owing in
unpaid calls. He also remembered that these shares were
transferred to him by the “ firm,” and that he could prove
beyond doubt that he was nothing but a “ dummy.”
Accordingly, he instructed the “firm” of his sudden
recollection, and the threatened prosecution shut up with
a dull sickening thud.

Cabled that a Bill has been submitted to
the Commons providing for the admission of
colonial solicitors to practise in the United
Kingdom in same way as British solicitors are
admitted to practise in the colonies. In New
South Wales the Supreme Court admits English
and Irish solicitors without examination, but re-
fuses admission to Scotch solicitors, unless they
belong to the high-toned, exclusive corporation
practising in Edinburgh, known as Writers to the
Signet. Until this injustice to Scotland has been
removed, Scotch M.P.’s have been advised to block
the Bill giving admission in Britain to colonial
solicitors.
*.* * * .
Bishop Smith, since he was turned into
an archbishop, has engaged a domestic chaplain,
who comes out with him. Chaplain’s duties are to
carry the big man’s crook on solemn occasions, and
to look after the bishop’s house when he is taking
tea at Govt. House or with pious old maids. If
the chaplain gives satisfaction, he is presented
with an incumbency after some time. The Syd-
ney clergy are asking how many more domestic
chaplains the bishop will cart out here. Already
one of these gentlemen has a fat_ living some-
where down Kiama way, to the detriment of sons
of the soil, who have prayed on their knees ever
so long for something better to do.
* * * *
Director Holtze, of Adelaide Botanic Gar-
dens, is anxious to open up a fashionable drive
through the enclosure for an hour or two each
warm afternoon. Westralianaire Brookman has
taken the project up and promises to help with a
fitting contribution. But if the rich man’s car-
riage passes the entrance, how can the poor man’s
dray be kept out of a place maintained by Govt.
money—for the latter vehicle is at times utilised
for carrying pleasure-parties.
The contention that artists should be called
in to adjudicate on the work of artists finds em-
phatic support in the art-judging efforts of the
City Fathers of Sydney. Originally they intended
to have two groups of statuary of absolutely
similar design for the new markets. They
have now decided on a little novelty, and have
ordered a second group. We are told (vide
the daily press) that the central figure of this
second chef d’ceuvre, “holding aloft a torch,” re-
presents “ the genius of civilisation.” He really
looks like an un-uniformed sub-lieutenant making
an awkward salute to the colors in a perfunctory
march-past. Two ladies sit on either side of him,
the one using compasses on a globe at which she is
not looking ; the other writing on a tablet with a
stylus, while her gaze is devouring a tomato-stall
(in futuro) somewhere in the bowels of the
markets.

Godless education again ! At a recent
meeting of the Sydney Builders’ and Contractors’
Association, the deplorable discovery was made
that not one member could name the date of the
approaching Easter Sunday. The mystery that an
ungodly pressman was better posted is easily ex-
plained. The scribe smells holiday-time afar off—
because for him, unhappy wretch, it means double
work !

Again, “ consider the J.P. how he
performs.” At a Southern township,
recently, the hour for the court to sit
had arrived ; the rural litigant had raked
the straws out of his hair and assembled
in force. Specially clever lawyers had
been brought up from town regardless of
expense, and everything was in order,
when war broke out between two occu-
pants of the bench. “Sir, lam chair-
man of this court ! I am the senior
magistrate, sir ! How dare you, sir ! ”
“How dare you, sir ! I am president of
this shire, sir.” “I’ll see you in Sheol
first, sir ! ” “ And /’ll see you
first, s ir! ” Then the court broke up
in disorder, and the man that was suing
the other man for allowing his cow to
eat two-shillings-worth of his grass, and
the squatter who was bringing an action
to make the selector remove his dead
horse off the front verandah, will have to
wait another month for the “law.”
F The Judge in a recent N.S. W. divorce-suit
found that the husband had received “cruel bat-
teries ” with broom and tongs, and that the
deliberate throwing of carbolic acid in his face
(which utterly destroyed one eye and injured the
other) was also a “cruel beating.” By the way,
how is it that The Bulletin hears nothing of this
carbolic-acid-thrower being prosecuted, while the
Sydney police hustle so desperately after John
Norton because he got a fly on his nose and used
language in a hotel, and tried to put his arm
round a lady’s waist? Also, what about that Wall
case ?

Mrs. Morris, lady missionary in the New
Hebrides, writes mournfully to a small Melb.
Presbyterian paper: “We make one condition
only, i.e., that they (the heathen) wear a garment
when they come (to Bible-classes), for you know
one of the sad sights we see here is the people
foing about without any clothing.” Why sad?
. <ots of women in Melb. see nothing to groan over
in the sight of a number of males bathing in a
state of nature. Quite the contrary. And they
are not what are called improper women, either.
The latter wouldn’t cross a street to look at a
whole regiment of undressed sons of Adam.
* * * *
At Flinders-street station, the other day,
two ladies of the new-woman type stepped into
the smoker. “Smoking carriage, ladies !” called
out several male voices. “Oh yes, we know,”
said one, “ but no gentleman would smoke in the
presence of ladies.” No one took any notice and
the lady pulled down her vest and continued,
“Oh, I see—there are no gentlemen present.”
Then a wretch puffed a blast under her nose and
remarked: “No, madam, we belong to the New
Men.” The ladies retired beaten at the next
station.

The Argus, always girding at the Vic.
Shops and Factories Act, prints a long par. to the
effect that Premier Turner one afternoon went to
a St. Kilda barber’s for a shave, but found the
shop closed for the weekly half-holiday. Where-
upon Turner cursed the Factories Act, and got
somebody to shave him somehow, that he might
put in a clean appearance at the Mayor’s banquet
an hour later. “As to how the trick was done,”
says the Argus chronicler, “ well, that is a
domestic secret.” Why not suggest a solution of
the mystery in a new nursery rhyme?—
Dr. Lloyd, J.P., of North Melbourne,
Victoria’s Squire of Dames, hearing a recent case,
declared himself a physiognomist and a disciple of
Lavater, and, on the strength of this, pronounced
the complainant to be “ a very respectable
woman,” whose evidence he believed. Which
should induce the man with the broken nose to
take his case as far from Lloyds as possible.

sealed his lips for ever
about the comfort and

don’s poor. A mother
of 36, with nine chil-
dren, 16 years to eight
months, broiled and
fused into three or four
shapeless, inseparable
masses, lying on the
blackened rafters of a
ten by sixteen by six
room for which two-
and-ninepence was the
weekly rental. Slaves
to the cruellest and
worst-paid because
dying —trade in Eng-
land, the half-starved
wife and her children used to rise each
morning at three (and ’tis bitter cold now
at three a.m.) to begin the day’s match-
box-making. The two-year-old child, pre-
maturely forced like Chinamen’s vegeta-
bles, midst rags and dirt, had to put the
paste on the wrappers. There is a row
of such stunted houses in the court, with
herded families in each room, and miles
and miles of such courts off the slum
streets of the East, but Reid and his
brother Australasian Premiers never
heard of or saw them from their royal
carriages.”

Who shaved G. Turner? From Mrs. T.
His worthy wife,
With carving-knife
She met the case,
And scraped his face
Both up and down ;
Then back to town
Despatched her lord.
At banquet board
He sat, and fed,
With sidelong head,
And stroked his cheek,
Too full to speak.
But, point of fact,
He d —d the Act
Which shut the shop,
And left his crop
Of bristles grey
To find a way
Of getting shorn.
Each blessed morn
Thereafter he
Accepts a scrape;
He can’t escape
His awful doom.
Observe the gloom
That gathers now
Upon his brow,
And all because
Of Peacock’s clause,
Which shut the shop
Of Barber Strop,
And taught a wife
To use the knife
With skilful touch —
Nor cut him much.
The gentle bard,
Who finds it hard
To neatly close
This rhyming prose,
Again will ask
(Here ends the task) :
Who shaved G. Turner?

Anyone visiting Coranderrk
(Vic.) black settlement expecting
to see aboriginals will be disap-
pointed. There may be a stray quad-
roon or octoroon about, but the
majority look more like centaroons.
Yellow-haired children abound, and,
in fact, it is hard to distinguish be-
tween the ordinary blondes and bru-
nettes of the district and the alleged
aboriginal children.
Wragge gives a very simple and lucid expla-
nation of the superiority of the summer climate of
Queensland to that of N.S.W., S.A., and Victoria.
He says that when there exists an area of baro-
metrical depression in the Antarctic, there is a
natural influx towards this area from the north.
Consequently, the hot air from Central Australia,
being violently sucked southward, travels over
Adelaide, Melbourne, and neighboring districts in
its course, and is, naturally, deflected clean away
from Queensland sea-board.
» * * *
Northern Tasmania has caught the bush-
fire epidemic badly. At “ New Paradise,” on the
N.W. coast, a family loaded a dray with provi-
sions, in case of a surprise, and dragged it clear
of the scrub. Then the fire came along, and, in
quick time, all was swept clean, food and all,
nothing remaining but one wheel of the dray. In
another instance, a woman, carrying a child, had
the boots burnt off her feet in her flight from the
flames. The line of fire near Table Cape was six
miles wide, “and the whole coast smells of roast
potatoes.”
HIS TALLY.
GRANDPA : “ Well, Johnny, how many good marks
did you get at school to-day? ”
JOHNY : “I shan’t know till I’m going to bed,
grandpa !”
In a far north-western town of N.S.W.
two Totties, elderly and in bad disrepair, were
recently charged with being two Totties within
the meaning of the Act. As a rule, the lady with
the thin tail of hair a-hanging down her back only
gets out into the back-blocks in her old and un-
comely days, and then the bench treats her with
very little consideration. In this case it sternly
ordered the two faded angels of the sidewalk to
gaol for a month, but when they offered to leave
the town the sentence was reduced to 14 days.
Generally the small offender gets off scot free on
promising to leave the town, but at the very
worst she can get her sentence considerably miti-
gated on giving her solemn pledge to go and be
equally obnoxious on somebody else’s premises
—though why the promise to go and be the same
nuisance on somebody else’s premises should be a
mitigation of the offence no one knows. Some
day, on the same principle, the man with the
dirty back-yard will have his fine remitted on his
promise to throw the offal over the fence into the
next fellow’s yard.
Dear Bulletin. —Your remarks on paying
patients in hospitals are to the point. To exclude
a man who can pay would often be a cruelty.
For instance, the man in lodgings. If he gets
sick, he cannot expect proper attention from
strangers, and probably can’t afford to pay his
weekly bill with medical attendance and comforts
superadded. To temporarily transfer himself to
hospital and pay 30s. a week—that is, say, 10s.
more than it has cost him for board and lodging—
is to him the easiest and most satisfactory solu-
tion of his difficulty. Often, too, for many reasons
easily found if sought, it is even preferable that
the family man should go to hospital and pay,
rather than remain at home to the discomfort of
himself and all around him. —M.

» * * *
At Wallsend (N.S. W.), last week, one man
was charged with biting another’s nose all but off.
“ The evidence tended to show that the men
quarrelled while under the influence of liquor,
and it was mutually decided that they should fight
a duel without weapons in the local cemetery, the
conditions being that the survivor should inter his
victim.” Yet people say the Age of Chivalry is
gone. Did Ivanhoe bury his carcases? Did
Bayard gallantly offer sepulture to a sword-pierced
antagonist—let alone a nose-bitten ditto? No,
Mr. Burke, that Age has just dawned—and in
Wallsend cemetery!
* * * *
“Wallaroo”: All over the northern
N.S.W. farming districts eyes and voices are
being raised in solemn thankfulness to the
“ Board ” for providing a glorious harvest —and
destroying it when it had almost reached maturity.
Some flabbergasted farmers, however, are indig-
nantly inquiring what they are to be thankful for ;
but the ecclesiastical eye, ignoring the disastrous
effects of late rain and hail-storms, remains ob-
stinately fixed on the flourishing crops of three
months ago, and the harvest festival farce is to
be carried out with due solemnity. What a howl
of holy horror would arise if, in place of the
wizened vegetables, pinched grain, and maggoty
fruit, the churches were decorated with Starve-ali,
Fat-hen, and similar agricultural triumphs ! Of
these alone have we an abundant crop.
* * * *
About the.hollowesb of all the hollow little
devices to which churches and chapels have to
resort in order to keep the parson’s pot boiling is
the “ harvest thanksgiving ” —celebrated whether
there’s been a harvest to gather or not. If there
hasn’t been any—well, they have to go out and
buy the pumpkins, that’s all. But with many of
the type who run the churches, buying is a beastly
bore ; and it was quite characteristic of these
people’s methods that lately a couple of “highly
respected ” church officers should have been taken
in the act of pilfering from the basket-reed plan-
tation alongside a Vic. prison. Yet, poor devils,
one can’t help sympathising with them in these
days of fierce competition, when the big drum has
to be banged more vigorously than ever it had to
be banged before.
What humorists you do find among the clergy !
In a city church not long ago, among the “harvest
festival” decorations were water-melons and
carrots, also turnips, doubtless Chow-grown.
Thanking God for having sent us Chinamen !
* * * *
According to Prof. Schenck, of Vienna,
sex is primarily a mere matter of victuals. Ex-
periments conducted among families of his inti-
mates justify him, he says, in asserting that by
means of diet women can be made to bear sons or
daughters at will. The prospective papa will now
be able to form definite resolutions about his son
(yet unborn, but sex-fed and scientifically fixed
up), and the mother will be able to dream of his
straight legs and his blue eyes, his courtship, mar-
riage, and settlement in life. Which would be all
pleasant enough but for the fact that Schenck
can’t establish, on the food-basis, the character of
the unborn brat. Also, the lady may entertain an
opposite desire to that of her husband in the sex
of the approaching pledge. And, though she
might ostensibly follow the prescribed regimen
when under observation, she would not fail to eat
in secret of the forbidden fruit, the result of which
opposition of diet-forces would possibly be of a
nature to interest the proprietor of a dime museum.
Altogether, Schenck may have carried his investi-
gations too far to be profitable to society. It is
enough to know that man is what he eats —when
he can get anything to eat —and it is uselessly
irritating to know that he may also be what his
mother eats.

Sydney Sunday Times’ offer of half-a-
sovereign—ten silver shillings—for the best female
poem on “ How to Manage a Man ” came to the
ears of a Bulletin penlady only last week when
the competition had closed. She sent her poem
along to this office. She is married, as the wedding-
ring of the sentiment naturally suggests.—
Three vessels were lost recently off the
Australian coast, and, according to the published
lists, the hardy British seamen on board panned
out as follows : —Atacama : 1 Englishman, 1
Scotchman, 3 Australians, 1 Dutchman, 1 Finn,
5 Chilians, 2 Americans, a Swede, a West Indian,
and a German. Malcolm : 2 Irish, 2 Germans, a
Norwegian, and a Swede. Amy : 1 Irish, 2 Nor-
wegians, 2 Swedes, 2 Germans.

THE POETS MEANING
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
SOULFUL person lightly tapped
The Poet's blistered door ;
His brow was broad , his mien was rapt ,
| An anxious look he wore.
He knocked again and yet again,
Then heard the poet rise
And stub his toe and swear hi pain
At someone's blanky eyes.
He wept to hear the Poet curse
And through the window yell ,
“ You hawkers here get worse and worse
Clear out of this to H —//”
The soulful person called : “ I'm not
hawker , as you think ;
I'm one who seeks the Sacred Spot
Where kindred souls can link.
“ I seek the cultured , flowing tide
That common clay has not."
The Poet spoke. “ Then come inside ,
Do?i’t stand there talking rot."
“/ thank you , sir " the stranger said ;
“ I've worshipped now Jor years
The art that has my fancy fed.
The art that forced my tears.
“ Your every ode , and verse and hymn
Rolls in my memory.”
The Poet grinned , and it was grim—
“ You've got more time than me."’
“ And now I wish that you would say,"'
The other paused intent ,
“ When you penned this exquisite lay
Exactly what you meant."
The Poet thought . “ That's hard , because
Perhaps 1 never knew
As far as / remember 'twas
To make a bob or two."
[?]
How is a woman to manage a Man ?
Flatter and fondle as much as she can ;
Gammon and grieve when her cooking’s amiss ;
Bid him good-bye with a lingering kiss,
Welcome him back with expression of glee,
Smile all the time "he is taking his tea.
Laugh at his jokes, though she knows them of old;
Never reply when it suits him to scold,
Nor question his statements, whatever she think,
Should “ work in the city,” smell strongly of drink.
Bear half of his troubles, and all of her own ;
Reprove him at times —in a worshipful tone.
Preserve her good looks till her figure grows stout,
Then gently and tenderly peg herself out,
Leaving him free in new bachelor life
To maudle, at times, o’er the loss of his wife—
Her tombstone recording—“ Here lies Mary Ann,
Who died of the effort to Manage a Man.”
