A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ Sydney, February 22, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
The bold, bad insurance-canvasser has already
marked as his prey the welcome little stranger
sleeping innocently in his first-quality cot up at
Govt. House. To be first in the field for the
Gov.’s grandchild would be a grand ad. for any
enterprising co., and Advertisement has marked
the aristocratic cherub for its own from birth.
An enterprising lady-visitor called on Capt. Fer-
guson the other day, and, after preliminaries,
worked in a casual hint that the company which
she represented would be proud and ’appy to give
a policy for the darling. And the wearied-out
parent replied with a tired sigh: “My dear
madam, you are the thirty-seventh person who
has asked me to insure that baby’s life.”
Vic. R. and I. is being extensively used as an
advt. for the Albani concert-boom by an astute
promoter, who knows that loyalty at advanced
rates can be made a delirious success if properly
worked. People who don’t know any tunes, and
°7*'y recognise the National Anthem because
they feel grateful to it for winding-up so many
evenings of boredom, have booked their seats with
a reverent idea of gazing on a lady who often
drops in casually on H.M. for afternoon tea and a
little gossip. Since the days of S. Bernhardt no
new-comer has caused such a neck-craning and
tip-toeing to behold her, and Sarah travelled quite
on her own without any royal backing. The Aus-
tralia is doing big business at lunch-time, for
curious persons stroll in, j n hopes of seeing the
tuneful visitor.
A few musicians and a large section of public—
the well-dressed and white-skirted kind of public
—went to the mayoral reception of Albani in the
Town Hall on Monday afternoon. Leading
musicians were absent in large numbers, in that
they hadn’t received the necessary pasteboard,
the cards having been dealt out promiscuous-like
with large hiatuses in the lists. It was an un-
satisfactory grovel at first, as the company had to
prowl about the corridors and put their heads in
at all the doors of the Mayoress’s suite of rooms
to get a glimpse of the recepted diva, but after-
wards they sat down in the big hall and plaved
the organ at her, and sitting between Mayor and
Mrs. Harris, and flanked by Mr. Albani and
J. C. Williamson, she looked in custody and as if
she couldn t run away even if she wanted to.
Albani’s pictures don’t do her any sort of justice;
she isn’t nearly as fat and dull and commonplace
as her picture-libels show her. She was gowned
in ivory white with spangly sparkles, and a purple
bonnet with a veil to match topped her off. Her
co. sat back respectfully, Miss Berry’s smashed-up
tomato-colored hat showing up, and Miss Clench,
the violinist, unremarkable in white. The
Mayoress, in a black-and-yellow silk, rose to
the occasion. Orlando Harley, as one who knew
his way about, went off on his own, and left the
shivoo early. Mr. Gye was playing the r6le of a
celebrity’s husband with quiet force. Florence
Schmidt, in a grass-lawn frock, was led up and
introduced as a promising local product. Mrs.
Burdekin in bright pink, Mrs. J. Brunton in a
frilly muslin, and a copper-haired girl in green
and pink, helped to give the afternoon a fashion-
able tone—which was needed. Most of the
musical women gloomed in black, and the men
were shabby some wore tweedy greys and
browns. Signor Hazon’s hair stood more erect
than ever with pleasure as he chatted to his old
friend the new conductor. The music was pro-
vided by the “ Firm’s ” orchestra in the corridor,
and songs were sung by H. W-hrand Miss Leitch,
W. and M.’s new voice.
A stern man at the door pounced on all cards,
and nobody was admitted without a displayed
invitation. Begging for invites raged until
almost the hour of opening the ball. At the
theatre-office, a string of applicants filed in all
morning, and even sat patiently waiting until
fired by impatient officials. And all the inviting
was done by the civic authorities. All the mem-
bers of the “ French Maid ” co. were absent, and,
except by Mr. Bracy, the profession was almost
unrepresented.
The Albani arrival at Redfern was a frost. So
thought the waiting welcomers of the musical pro-
fession and sundry sight - rushers, who were
patiently lined-up on the platform, on the look-
out for the Melb. express. But Albani, escorted
by husband Gye, and taken in tow by J.C.W.,
was hustled off into her waiting conveyance, with-
out a glance to right or left, and all the snubbed
welcome-delegation tailed sorrowfully home again
with unshaken hands.
Mayor Harris has determined to shut down on
pushers, and has decided that the only way to
curb the numerous greedy throng that fastens on
to every local little tin-god for what it can get out
of him is to hang ou 1 no inducement whatever in
the way of free feeds and dances to the self-seek-
ing fringe of sassiety. The wholesale cadging for
invitations that goes on before every big public
entertainment, especially if the party includes any
gratis refreshments, is nothing better than a high-
toned version of “the eight o’clock rush” at a
charitable eating-house. No more banquets and
no ball—a great set-back for the noble army of
sassiety sundowners. But— Mayor Harris made
rather a sad mess of his first invitation-list—that
is to say, if the catalogue of those invited to meet
Mme. Albani was really intended to be musically
represen tati ve.
Last week’s mail-boat took away for an English
sojourn Mr. and Mrs. Alf. Marks, who, three
years ago, were the hero and heroine of a much-
discussed love-match, the bride being Claire
Leighton of the Gaiety Co. Contrary to pro-
phesyings of some kind friends, the tall, fair ex-
G.G. settled down naturally to country life, and
after her ruralising at St. Mary’s, where she and
her husband had their home, has lost none of her
good looks. As she is innocent of make-up or
peroxide and doesn’t totter about on four-inch
heels, she is a disappointment to those good folk
who like to imagine every woman from behind the
curtain a mask of artificiality. She is an ardent
backer of horses, and as such regularly turned up
at every meeting important enough to have one
bookie there to lay the odds. A fellow-traveller
by the Noney Seabrook, formerly leading
lady with Thornton, leaves Australia after a
chequered career of 18 months.
Somebody asked baritone Pauli, at the Albani
flurry on Monday afternoon, if he intended mak-
ing a long stay in Australia. The clean-shaven
singer s reply was worthy of Ryley : “I shall, if
the girls are nice,” he pronounced, sweeping an
approving glance at the groups around him.
Ordinary country folk are so much scared at the
exclusiveness of Ranji and co. that they now hesi-
tate before offering them any civility, lest it
should be met with discouragement and chill. It
would appear that the reason why they did not
take part in the dance got up in their honor at
Glen Innes was that, owing to timidity in ap-
proaching the guests, none of the promoters of the
hop led forward partners for them !
Interest waned in the last week’s cricket struggle.
Clem. Hill doesn t as yet rival His Royal Darkness
as a draw, for only a scattered hundred women
watched him make that duck on Friday—to be
atoned for by a brilliant score in the next innings.
Ivext to the women who pose as cricket innocents
and gabble continually inane questions, in the
hope that some lurking scribe may hear them and
quote them in print, are the cricket critics with a
pet scrap of technicality which they apply to
every bit of play. A distressingly well-informed
girl on Friday capped every move with her criti-
cism of “loose fielding,” and everybody around
her looked impressed until seventy times seven,
and then they eyed her with suspicious misgiving.
A refreshing point about an inter-Australian match
is the absence of the professional and amateur
feeling. When the English team drove to the
gates before their late match with N.S.W., it was
instructive to see the hirelings drop off and humbly
hump their own baggage inside, while “the
gentlemen” were driven stylishly inside the
grounds right up to the entrance. That kind of
thing makes one squirm, as somehow in Australia
everyone feels that sport should mean camaraderie.
The wedding of Miss May Roberts with Mr.
Ambrose Pratt, which happened at St. Mary’s last
Wednesday afternoon, was an extensively-clothed
ceremony—there was more white satin and orange-
blossom to the square inch than in any fashionable
fixing-up for some time in this city. The bride’s
train was a billowing wake behind her as she
walked up to have the knot tied, and her lace and
floral top-hamper were handed down from the
wedding of her sister, who made a match under
romantic circumstances a few years ago. The
chief robe and most of the others were made by
Hordern Bros. The bride’s stock of store-clothes
is calculated to last her until far into the future.
At the subsequent health-drinking at the bride’s
home, the brilliant novelty of speechless toasts
was introduced—a precedent that ought to be
widely followed by an overspeeched-at and
preached-at generation.
One of the passengers to Sydney this voyage of
the China is a Sydney girl, Miss Inez Bensusan,
who left here some two years ago to make a bid
for fame and fortune on the stage, and has now
come home for a holiday. She had a welcome-
home tea-drinking last week, and discoursed in-
terestingly to her guests about her doings and
sight-seeings in London and other parts of Eng-
land, where she toured with the Kendals. Ter-
riss’s funeral was the last event which Miss Ben-
susan witnessed before leaving London. The
scene in Brompton Cemetery showed a wild
struggle of spectators to see the “ show,” men and
women furiously pushing each other and trampling
vigorously over graves and flower-beds in their
eagerness to behold the sensational burying.
Among Australians in Fogland Nellie Stewart
was holding up her end of the log in the “ Scarlet
Feather” by her taste in stage-costumes, and
looked as young as when Australia last saw her
shapely form, but Decima Moore had gone off
both in singing and fascination. Miss B. saw
“Charlotte Corday” at Islington. The house
was papered and cold, and the doleful drammer
didn’t draw.
As Nancy, in the Lyceum drama, Lilian
Wheeler wears ankle-revealing skirts for the first
time in her stage career, and is graceful as the
sweetheart of the long-legged sailor, Walner
Gregory. But her neat-waisted prettiness doesn’t
indicate the muscles of the vigorous young woman
who throws off two able-bodied men when they
attempt to lock her up at the villain’s bidding.
There is much clawing and pawing in the play.
Every character clutches every other character.
The villain is a perfect octopus at grabbing, and
the hero’s principal occupation is dragging him off
every member of the cast in turn.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Freeman-Thomases are giving a ‘servants’
and tradesmens’ ’ ball preparatory to their de-
parture from Melbourne. Mrs. F-T. sighs for
the pleasures of London and won’t come back
with her pa. The question is, who will come back
with him ? Shaftesbury is gone, leaving a trail of
smoke-room anecdotes (and other things) behind
him; Freeman-Thomas is not returning; and
Dicky Nevill has family troubles on hand. The
Brassey household, in fact, is bursting up, and will
want replenishing.
“ Mr. Byron Moore, enlarging on the predatory
habits of the women who visit the ladies’ retiring-
room on the lawn at Flemington, declares that
they remove everything portable, set after set of
toilet - services disappearing in one day. The
V.R.C. now chains everything to the walls but the
soap and the hairpins, and these commodities have
to be laid in by the ton. On the Hill, according
to the secretary, there is comparatively little lost.
He cannot explain why. I can. To remove a
toilet-set on the Hill is thieving ; to remove one
on the Lawn is merely kleptomania.
“ Invitees flocked to the Mayoress’s welcoming
of Albani, and flounced into the Town Hall
between two mobs of cardless women who had
evidently come with the idea of being able to get
through ‘on the nod.’ Mrs. M'Eacharn, with
her agonised society smile in full working order,
pump-handled everybody on entrance, and the
illustrious Albani followed suit. She so smilingly
greeted everybody that she made them feel as if
she would like to take them in and introduce ’em
to the Royal Family if she should ever happen to
meet them outside Windsor Castle.
“ The crowd passed the hostess and her show
guest, and then sought a point of vantage from
which to gaze their full upon the judiciously-
advertised warbler. Then, for the first time, was
noticed J. C. Williamson, positively effulgent, and
visibly swollen with the importance of the occa-
sion. Propping up the door-post stood his satel-
lite, the ever-juvenile Henry Bracy, resplendent
in a grey frock suit and nursing tenderly a tile
which would have served admirably as a mirror.
Meekly in the background were the other two
ladies of the new concert co. The contralto, of
the usual fleshy order, aired a fawny pink-looking
gown, striped wonderfully with peach-color, and
a hat of startling pink with puce flowers. The
little violiniste looked fetching in a demure frock
and hat of palest grey. Caron’s band pounded
away down the corridor at various operas favored
by Mme. Albani, and occasional squalls from the
distance announced a concert in progress.
“When the people had gazed sufficiently at the
cantatrice in the Mayor’s room a desire for novelty
impelled them to the Council Chamber, whence
the wails proceeded, and soon the Mayoress and
the show guests rustled in and took front seats.
Alice Coy chortled pleasingly ; Maggie Sterling
gasped through a sentimental ditty; dolly Walter
Kirby plaintively bleated to some unknown she
‘Good night, sweet love.’ Regina Nagel (when
is she going ’Orae to study ?) devoted her beautiful
voice to murdering a ditty of the heart-broken
order.
“ Then came the scramble for tea and ices by
the women—the Mayor had been considerate for
the men and provided something with a big stick
in it.. Actor Wilson Barrett and his ladies were
formidable rivals to the singers, and, during the
first part of the afternoon, the eminent actor’s
anxiety about the non-arrival of his leading lady,
and his fidgety hunt in and out for her, caused
some amusement. Finally, he stationed himself
at the head of the stairs, and when La Belle
Jeffries arrived at a fashionably late hour greeted
her rapturously and conducted her to the hostess.
The Silver King by daylight, despite his bright
brown hair with the bottle-brush-front effect, dis-
appointed romantic girls by looking—well, older
and less interesting than on the stage. Mrs.
M‘Eacharn cut a fashionable dollish figure in a
pale blue rig-out with a white and blue hat; while
the one and only Albani looked big, handsome, and
gracious, in a grey brocade which could stand
alone, worked up with ivory lace and glittering
with cut steel crowned by a toque of violets and
crimson roses—also a violet veil, which behaved
cruelly to her complexion. Maud Jeffries posed
her massive, proportions admirably, and aired a
sweet thing in black and blue ; with a floppy black
hat of the picture order. Languishing Lillah
McCarthy sported white, in which lace played a
prominent part. Her mushroom-like hat had two
sweeping feathers resting on the brim in the style
loved by ’Arriet. Daisy Belmore also went in for
a white effect, and faced the public in a white
silk gown, without a wrinkle, which looked
as though glued on. Sassiety generally, which
turned up in strong force, wore its best
bib and tucker for the occasion. Mrs. Croker
was in sable garb ; Mrs. Rupert Carington sported
white, with a blob of intense blue for headgear.
And so on. Mrs. Sandes, nee Claire Berry, was a
modest form in smoke-grey, which trailed the
ground, and made her look still a foot or so taller.
“At the Albani reception the women gazed at
the great singer \\ ith awe and reverence, for the
fact was whispered about —as though it were
Albani’s only t'itle to fame—‘ She is a friend of
the Queen’s ; the Queen likes to hear her sing. ’ It
is difficult to imagine how being the favorite
songstress of a dull old lady with an obsolete
musical taste and imperfect hearing would add
one laurel-leaf to an artist’s reputation, and to
trot out the royal friendship as Albani’s great
‘double-barrel ’ is nothing short of an insult to
that artiste’s magnificent gift. During the recep-
tion Mr. Albani distributed smiles and hand-
shakes and played the part of the celebrity’s hus-
band with a good deal of power. And at the con-
cert every artist sang straight at the great singer
as much as to say ‘ What do you think of my little
lot ? ’ The vice-regal carriage and Lord R. Neville
appeared at five o’clock and whisked Albani and
husband off to Govt. House where they stayed
till Friday afternoon —when they left, for Sydney
by express.
“ Like David Copperfield’s dinner-party, ‘ The
Squire of Dames ’ first night would have gone off
ever so much better if everyone hadn’t been so
genteel. An icy air of most pronounced ‘ toney-
ness ’ and correctness pervaded the whole house
and made the atmosphere almost chilly, although
the glass stood above the nineties. On the stage a
hard frost of top-loftiness set in soon after the
raising of the curtain, and continued increasing
in severity until the four small acts and three
gigantic intervals came to an end. All the
women seemed much too ‘ smart ’ to infuse
any life or meaning into their high - toned
parts, so that interest in anything but their
superb finery was impossible from the beginning,
but every woman of them scored heavily as a
dressmaker’s model. As each fresh confection
was trailed behind the footlights, every woman in
front recognised that all the gowns in her own
wardrobe were hopelessly obsolete. Godets have
disappeared, and fibre chamois as skirt-lining is
entirely wiped out, for skirts are now droopy,
narrow about the knees, and ‘waggle-tailed,’ sug-
gesting the mode common in photos, of belles of
the year ’75. The latest snap in sleeves appears to
be to have them skin-tight (shoulder frilling is
absolutely dead), and terminating much above
the wrist. Of the four gorgeous creations worn
by Beatrice Lamb (who much resembles
Constance Deorwyn, by the way), the most
admired was a walking dress of amber
brocade, elaborately trimmed with cinnamon
velvet; though she looked at her best in the dinner-
gown of rose-pink bengaline most fascinatingly
fixed with fine, black lace. A young lady of
Bernhardtian contour, appropriately named
‘ Getthyn ’ Darragh, struggled slightly with an
American twang, but the prevalent top-loftiness
soon got the better of her and she dropped it for
‘culchawed’ English tones. Her chief success
was a lime-green satin dinner-gown beruched and
beflounced. The inaudible Katherine Stewart
swept four very handsome trains over much hand-
some furniture, and seemed deeply depressed
at having to own ‘Elsie’—a lank child with
vast lengths of red arms and black feet who
suffered painfully from stage-fright. The ever-
brilliant Cartwright was the Clem. Hill of the co.
He seems not to have added an hour to his age
since he was last here. Thursby is a well-set-up
person distinguished for his shoulders and his side
and his knack of comic song in private. On the
voyage out the whole company was busy study-
ing and had little time to fritter away in ship-
board flirtation. Beatrice Lamb daily sought
upper-deck seclusion and pored over bulgy MSS.
“ ‘ Smart ’ Melb. was much in evidence clus-
tered round the vice-regal box and in the stalls.
Mrs. Freeman-Thomas, blithe as usual in black
with many diamonds, and Mrs. ‘ Big ’ Clarke 11.,
emerging from mourning for the first time and
sporting a vivid rose-pink gown, were the most
admired women in the circle. In a box with Mrs.
Rickards was Cartwright’s pretty daughter—a
fluffy-haired girl with a vivacious face, who will
appear in the next piece the eo. produces.
“ The ‘ blushing modesty ’ and reticence which
one time were considered necessary adjuncts to
every bride are scornfully laughed out-of-date by
the new woman. A pretty Melb. girl, always
noted for her ‘ advanced ’ ideas, was married, the
other day, to a professional man from another
province. The wedding, a very simple ceremony
and absolutely ungarnished by the usual orange-
blossom, favors, rose-leaves, &c., was followed by
a dance, at which bride and groom were present
and danced energetically till dawn. Then the
happy pair, with a large party of assorted friends,
caught the early boat to a down-the-bay resort,
where the honeymoon was spent amidst a large
and giddy house-party.
“A fierce and exciting scene took place last
week in the lobby of the House between two dele-
gates. There had been an ancient enmity between
the pair till Federal proceedings patched up a
kind of armed truce. This was broken the other
day by a chance word of one of them. The bigget
of the two men instantly and emphatically chal-
lenged his adversary to ‘ come out and fight,’ and
he seemed to be quite ready till other delegates
stepped between and separated the combatants.
“At Caulfield, on Saturday, the Y.A.T.C.
meeting encountered so hot a day that frocks
with little beyond coolness to recommend them
were more successful than less seasonable costumes
of ten times the value. Miss Maude Jeffries, in
white muslin, wore hat, veil, and silk sash all to
match. The Convention delegates, invited by the
committee to lunch and afternoon tea, arrived in
force, with their womenkind. Premier Forrest,
in ‘ slummocky ’ tweed suit and overgrown black
boxer, escorted both his nieces—one in heliotrope,
the other in yellow, tucked and ruched with
buttercup chiffon.
“ Apropos theatrical waist-line. Mummers,
like vocalists, often pant comparatively early in
life under a burden of flesh into which they were
not born. Hygienic theorists trace the unroman-
tio plumpness of ex-stars of melodrama, and the
aggressive embonpoint of pantomine ‘boys,’ to
vocal athletics and consequent chest-expansion.
Steamboat officials, whose pet passengers are not
pros., have their own interpretation of the parable
of the ‘ fat kine ’of stageland. ‘ Theatricals are
greedier and cheekier than commercials,’ these
experienced observers will tell you.
“ Exile Willard, with dark hair falling over his
pallid brow, palpably knowsa thing or two re grace-
ful arm and leg deportment d la Wilson Barrett.
His returned-colonist coat is free-and-easy about
the neck, and, when he takes it off to escape from
prison, it is evident that the Byronic collar effect
is due to the shirt having been cut in a ‘ V.’
‘Studied carelessness ’ with a vengeance ! Moya
Deorwyn wears a scarlet cloak and shows her
ankles in proper colleen fashion. His Reverence
Holloway, milder and less jolly than the ordinary
stage-priest, boasts a glossy top-hat, and Daisy
Coppin, with gown uplifted over a jaunty blue
petticoat, takes energetic part in an Irish jig.
“ On the whole, the Convention women have
kept the pace wonderfully this meeting. A few
of them have even ‘ chummed-up,’ and some of
the girls are off to Sydney, presently, to witness
the wedding of a delegate’s daughter.
“ The Austral Salon Committee has shaken the
dust of precedent off its feet and provided a bag
of new notions for 1898. Henceforth each mem-
ber can only entertain one outside friend per
month, but, by way of compensation, the hostess
for the day may ask 50 acquaintances of her own.
This innovation really should encourage hospi-
tality. A hostess invites 50 of her very
own chums to central rooms with many
conveniences. Leading lights of the club
provide the music, &c., gratis. In return, she lets
sister members and associates, and a few of their
friends, have a cup of her tea and a morsel of her
cake. On Tuesday evening (Feb. 22nd), the Salon
is booked to take formal leave of its clever
habituee, elocutioniste Charlotte Hemming off to
show her professional paces in the ‘ big smoke.’
On the following Monday (28th), Salonites will lay
their farewell tribute at the shrine of the Wilson
Barrett company.”
“ From 23 to 30 years ago Melbourne was
startled by a romance of which the facts never
came fully to light. Wild, mysterious yarns of
elopement, conversion and priestly interference
were whispered freely at the time, but nothing
definite became known. Romeo’s father, satisfied
that the son of a great statesman had riot de-
meaned himself by marriage with a mere actress,
left his will unaltered, and 15 years later his vast
estates fell in due course to the principal in the
romance, who has, up to now, always been re-
garded by the world as a hopeless bachelor; for
though numberless opportunities of marriage with
well-gilt and attractive women presented them-
selves, he passed by on the other side. Now, some
years after the old man’s death, Romeo comes out
with the announcement that ‘Mr. and Mrs.
are at their town residence.’ That lady.has been
Mrs. since the date of the original romance
in the early seventies. A well-kept secret.
‘‘At the Royal, Grattan Riggs’ ‘Shaughraun’
is beguiling enough to ‘ come the comether ’ over
an audience of Orangemen. Nevertheless, the
‘endearing young charms’ of the light-heeled,
quiek-tongued Irish bhoy lack one essential, to
wit, an unobtrusive waist-line.
“The Vic. Governor, when it suits him, can be
quite ‘ a merry old soul ’! Surely, in his long life,
he must have suffered as much as most of us at
the hands of the drawing-room elocutioniste; yet
it’s wonderful, at this time of day, to see him en-
tering into her hackneyed tommy-rot with ap-
parent zest, and shaking his elderly sides with
well-simulated glee. Sometimes the nice old thing
exerts himself to keep up a brisk fire of sotto voce
comment, or answers the reciter’s would-be artless
questions as if he thought she really wanted to
know, you know!
Yours faithfully,
CLEO.

As The Bulletin prophesied some months
ago, Lawyer Geake has beaten the Vic. authori-
ties who, after extraditing* and convicting him for
one crime, proceeded to punish him for another.
Geake made out a masterly statement of his case
which was referred to England for decision. The
result means that his contemplated confinement is
shortened by two years.

. Q* : “ ‘ M‘Gr.’s ’ and ‘Rap’s’ generalisa-
tions on convicts’ facial expressions are superficial
and valueless. Clean-shave almost any man—saint
or sinner-wearing a full beard, and he will pro-
bably look hideous, simply because beards are so
often worn to hide unbeautiful features. To begin
with, most men are ugly, and mere ugliness goes
lor nothing whatever as a criterion of criminality.
As a class, criminals, high or low, arc as handsome
as any other class—priests, journalists, or any
other crowd, for example. Some of the ugliest
men on record have been the most intellectual and
eminent Johnson, Gibbon, Renan, and others.”
The other day while Gov. Brassey was
attending an Executive Council, an old dead-beat
planked himself alongside the vice-regal carriage
and informed the man on the box-seat that he was
blanky well to stay there till the blanky
Governor gave him a shilling. In due course
Brassey and the lath-like Lord Dicky Nevill
Bailed out, and were saluted jovially by the sun-
downer, but without result, which so disgusted
the man of the roads that he remarked : “ Garn,
yer blanky, stuck-up, old blanker ; yer father was
only a blanky navvy on a railway‘line ! ” Then
the vehicle rolled away, leaving a few mud-spots
on the ancient as a memento.

G.B. ; “ Former Melb. P.-M. Call used to
afford quite a humorous relief from colleague
Panton. Once, when the writer was fined for
taking a dog through Fitzroy Gardens, he ven-
tured to remind Mr. Call that he (C.) had himself
offended in the same way. ‘ Quite right,’ said
Call,; ‘make the declaration, and I’ll pay the
fine. It was done—and the newspaper man came
out on top for once. ”
Some years back a notorious Brisbane (Q.)
embezzler, out on bail, drove up to the local
Supreme Court in a neat little pony-trap, from
which he stepped, well-gloved, and with overcoat
over his left arm. “ Call for me at five,” he said
to his coachman. At five he was in the cells
under sentence of a few years. Which recalls the
dinner that Alphonse Bechet ordered at Sydney
Metropolitan to celebrate his acquittal. lie did
not help to eat it —he got seven years.
AT THE WAXWORKS.—112° IN THE SHADE.
Justice has not yet been done to the
beauties of S.A. Federation delegate Vaiben L.
Solomon. Vaiben L. has a big, oiled, glossy,
black beard, glossy black eyes, a glossy black hat,
a glossy black necktie, glossy black clothes, and
glossy black patent-leather boots. There is a
sheen all down him, and a high light on his feet.
Also Vaiben L. is quick of speech and occasionally
saucy, and has a secret idea that if. the Queen of
Sheba were to reincarnate she would know him
again at once. It is told of Vaiben L. that at an
election meeting a well-irrigated elector asked
him, “ Are you Solomon the wise man who built
the Temple?” “No,” said he, “I am Solomon
the fool who built the Arcade.” This same joke
is told of Johnny Solomon, who built a theatre,
but it isn’t an uncommon thing for one joke to do
for two Solomons.
By the death of Thomas Bracken—he died
in poverty after a long and hopeless illness—
Maoriland loses a genuine poet, an intense lover
of his adopted country, and a highly-attractive,
all-round personality. Bracken’s varied life ex-
periences left him to the end a thorough Irishman
of the peculiar Celtic stamp in which rollicking
merry-heartedness so strangely co-exists with the
more melancholic order of pathos and the gently
pessimistic tendency exemplified in the subjoined
verses (printed many years ago in The Bulletin).
They are perhaps as good as anything else
Bracken has written :
The blind faith of the man
who buys a bottle of infallible hair
restorer from a bald barber, is dis-
counted (at Jewish rates) by that
of the shrewd Melb. bank manager
who, a few weeks ago, paid 2s. extra for a young
rosella on the verbal guarantee of the shoeless
vendor that the bird was a male. Fact!
Adelaide citizens, who had not seen rain
for five months, were visited the other day with a
small shower of rain—just enough to make the
prevailing heat more unpleasant than usual.
Whereupon Caldwell, M.P.—one of the Tory
Oppositionists—broke out into beautiful poetrv.
A hunk : v J
Some cried : “ Will heaven upon us pour
Such maddening.heat for evermore ?
Has God forsook the human race?
Has He withdrawn his love and grace?”
While glad ones cried in many a home,
“ The rain has come ! the rain has come 1”
Caldwell was a member of the original Board
which started the God-forsooken Hospital dis-
putes in Adelaide.
Lond. Saturday Review says of “XXI.
Poems,” by Chris. Brennan—the smart young
Sydneyite who graduated at Sydney University,
and now shakes long locks as an assistant in
N.S.W. Public Library
Mr. Brennan’s work is interesting and puzzling : he
seems to have, very acutely and subtly, the poetic sense,
but hardly the poetic voice. His poems impress one, for
the most part, as being cold, fastidious translations from
some beautiful original, written in alien metres, which
the translator has tried conscientiously to convey. Thus
the actual verbal expression and rhythm have seldom
any keen .charm about them, vet there seems to be some
underneath smouldering of poetry.
But it is rather a “ precious ” than a poetical piece
which the reviewer selects for justificatory quota-
tion.
“ Dr. Lennox Browne, the fashionable
Portland Place surgeon,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, “bestowed upon one of
his serving-maids an honor which an unsenti-
mental Bench decided required ss. a week to
support. ‘ Larynx ’ Browne, as he is most widely
known, attends all theatrical she-vocalists for
throat troubles free of charge, and has reaped a
splendid advertisement therefrom. But this
departure from the ‘ blazed ’ track means prac-
tical ruin, although the case was one of black-
mailing impure and unsimple, as Dr. Browne had
already paid a lump sum. As a further foolish-
ness, he endeavored to get the Times to suppress
all account of the police-court proceedings, where-
upon, of course, the virtuous heavyweight rose at
the insult and erected half-a-column in its next
issue.”
A MILITARY ITEM.
TEACHER OF BUSH SCHOCL (on a wet day, to solitary pupil): “Form—fours! ”
Some men have all the luck. Several
years back an important personage from Siam
arrived in Newcastle (N.S.W.), and the raggedest
of local reporters was sent to interview him. The
pressman seized the golden opportunity to impress
the visitor with the importance of having a white
amanuensis. About five years later, a bell-
toppered young man, with lavender kid gloves
and a sweet smile, stalked into the local Herald
office and asked the editor—haw—to notify that
the “ private secretary to the King of Siam” was
touring Australia. Astonishment couldn’t have
been greater if the frayed reporter had been sud-
denly transformed into Archangel William of
Germany.
Related of a N.S.W. station-manager and
well known judge of merinos, that he was travel •
ling with his wife in a backblocks coach, when a
fellow - passenger proceeded to fill and light his
pipe. The s.m. said the lady objected to smoke.
“ Perhaps I may chew ? ” said the passenger.
“Yes, you can chew,” replied the s.m., “but, if
you spit, I’ll be the death of you.”

Dear Bdlletin, —lie the article in your issue of 4th
December. 1897, under the heading of “ A Modern Buc-
caneer.” Your statement of the case is perfectly correct,
save for the fact that “Rolf Boldrewood ” d.d make an
honorable acknowledgment of his indebtedness to the
author of “ By Reef and Palm ” for the material in “ A
Modern Buccaneer.’’ In the second edition of the work,
published by Macmillan in 1894, you will .see l lie acknow-
ledgement on a fly-leaf of that melancholy book.
Ferule: “Demise. George Morrison, head
of Geelong Academy. The most comprehensively
learned of our pundits, but not quite such a good
tutor as his brother Alexander, identified with
Melb. Scotch College almost since the memory- of
man. George fathered the gentleman who walked
over Australia and China. It is noticeable how
extra-smart brothers go in trios, and there were
three Morrisons, the third being about the fore-
most Glasgow pedagogue. Of the three Higin-
bothams, George and Thomas always held that
the other surpassed them. The three clever
Frondes were the historian, the theologian,
and the mathematician. And there were, three
Fitchetts.”

tence meself to three months’hard labor and ask

As showing the magnificent, sweeping way
in which Horace Tozer —who goes to London as
Q. Agent-General—was wont to surmount diffi-
culties, this incident is worth recording (writes a
Bulletin correspondent). A few years ago an
influential petition, signed by certain R.C. spi-
ritual navigators, was forwarded from Rockhamp-
ton to the Home Secretary, praying for the imme-
diate liberation of a prisoner on the ground that,
through a death-bed repentance, the confessional
and other mysterious agencies, positive evidence
of the confinee’s innocence had been obtained.
Tozer apparently regarded the bulky documents
as explosives to be handled with care ; anyhow
he promptly pigeon-holed the petition and let
things rip. Four months later the instigator of
tie release-agitation humbly enquired if any
answer would be vouchsafed the petitioners
before the prisoner’s sentence of 20 years expired.
He received an official reply, stating that the
Home Secretary—Tozer-had submitted all the
evidence supplied to the Judge who tried the
case, and that his Honor could not find anything
to warrant a reversal of sentence'or a re-opening
of the matter. The answer nearly flabbergasted the
reverend recipient. For the Judge who tried the
case was Mr. Justice Mein, who had been safely
interred, with military honors, in Toowong ceme-
tery—just two years before the petition was drawn
up or dreamt of !

THE STREET ORATOR.
With waving arm, and voice that seemed to fret
The air, he cursed the world and all its jars—
This small, black, prancing, raging silhouette
Outlined against the calm, cold, cynic stars.
Melb.
C.R.
NOT UNDERSTOOD.
(THOMAS BRACKEN.)
Not understood. We move along asunder,
Our paths grow wider as the seasons creep
Along the years ; we marvel and we wonder
Why life is life ? And then we fall asleep
Not understood.
Not understood. We gather false impressions,
And hug them closer as the years go by,
Till virtues often seem to us transgressions ;
And thus men rise and fall, and live and die,
Not understood.
Not understood. Poor souls with stunted vision
Oft measure giants by their narrow- gauge ;
The poisoned shafts of falsehood and derision
Are oft impelled 'gainst those who mould the age,
Not understood.
Not understood. The secret springs of action,
Which lie beneath the surface and the show,
Are disregarded ; with self-satisfaction
We judge our neighbors, and they go
Not understood.
Not understood. How- often trifles change us 1
The thoughtless sentence, or the fancied slight
Destroy long years of friendship, and estrange us,
And on our souls there falls a freezing blight;
Not understood.
Not understood. How many breasts are aching
For lack of sympathy ! Ah ! day by day,
How many cheerless, lonely hearts are breaking,
How many noble spirits pass away
Not understood.
Oh God ! that men would see a little clearer,
Or judge less harshly where they cannot see ;
Oh God ! that men would draw a little nearer
To one another—they’d be nearer Thee,
And understood.

The merciless climate ! Around Barcal-
dine (Q.), till lately, drought and stock-starvation
have prevailed. Then 12in. of rain fell during 10
days, filling dams and watercourses and con verting
plains into sheets of water. Whereupon signs of
footrot among the sheep became visible. Fate
either burns you out, or drowns, you in.
How the Singapore Chinaman does things.
From a recent Straits Times ■ :
On view to-day at Mr. de Silva’s in High-street a
magnificent jewel, manufactured by Mr. de Silva
to the order of Mr. Tan Khiam Hock, of the
Opium Farm, who intends it as a present for his
wife. It is a lady’s belt buckle, made of solid
22-carat gold, richly carved in a beautiful floral
design, and set with 281 diamonds. There are thirteen
gold roses, each having a diamond in the centre, and in
every one of the six petals, a large 181-carat diamond
occupying the centre of the design. The diamonds
altogether amount to 210 carats, and the value of the gold
used is $750. The total value of the jewel is $15,000.
In these days, when N.S.W. positively re-
fuses to become jolly and fat, the next best thing
is to make it reasonably moist, and the best thing
to do that with is the Five Crowns Scotch Whisky
sold by Harrison and Attwood, of Sydney, and
otherwise known as the Real Mackay. It is a
beautifully mellow, straw-colored beverage—the.
tint of a poet’s dream. In point of age, it is a
good second to Methuselah, and the aroma of the
ripe corn is in its soul, and the breath of the, dales
where that corn grew is in the soul of the person
who drinks it. Also, to descend to common, dull
particulars, it has a pleasant flavor, and a long-
established reputation ; its purity is guaranteed by
the analyst’s report on the label; and it never
“ puts a head ” on any man.*

Dear B,—One more for M‘Cooey. Some
years ago I was in the milking-yard at our farm at
Gerringong, and a few yards from my bail as a
gt one wall. (Nearly all the “ fences ”in the South
Coast are stone walls—splendid breeding-grounds
f o r snakes.) I was about to turn out my cow after
milking, when I noticed a 4ft. black snake coming
o ut of the wall, but before I could move a hand
there was a “ whish ” past my ear, and a laughing-
jackass had gripped the snake behind the head,
given it a “ thwack ” on the stones, and started
off to the nearest tree. The snake twisted itself
completely round the body of the bird, beginning
a fc the neck, but all the time “ Jack ” was ham-
mering the snake’s head against the tree. Pre-
sently the snake uncoiled, and no sooner did it
hang down full length than the bird let it drop,
followed like lightning, picked the snake up again,
an( j repeated the performance over and over for full
half-an-hour, by which time the snake was appar-
ently dead. Then “ Jack ” began to gulp it down
slowly, head-first, and when I last saw him sitting
there, some two hours after, he had 6in. of reptile
hanging from his beak.—J. Black, Nubba.
Dear Bulletin, — Nearly everybody wil
go “ najj ” that the kangaroo makes a spring-board
of his tail in jumping. Fact is that when jumping
he simply uses it as a balance-pole, holding it per-
fectly rigid, about six inches from the ground.
Now then, M‘Gooey—lay on, and spare not 1—OlI)
Man.
BISHOP STONE- WIGG, of New Guinea.
Dear Bulletin. —Struck an old-estab-
lished Darling whaler, and asked him what he
thought about the alleged “ rattiness ” cf himself
and co. “ Ratty ?” he asked. “ Who says we’re
ratty? Oh, them coves in the cities. They’re no
’count. They think we’re ratty ’cause we ain’t
openin’ our heads all the time. And we know
they’re ratty ’cause they are openin’ theirs. Gawd !
to think a man’s off his napper ’cause he’s sick of
their skite, and so hides hisself.” And that’s
all I could get out of him—except the compliment
that I was no doubt one of ’em myself.—P.

A wail from wayback :
Ed. Bulletin.- .Tint a line or two to let you know how
the shearers in the country districts gull the business
people. They go down the rivers and get their cheques,
and travel on them as far as they can. People won’t cash
them when they are on banks other than those in the
town they live in. They put up at the hotels, get meals
and horse-feed, and clear off before the hotel-keeper is
up, taking towels, combs, and very often blankets with
them. Two staved at my hotel on Wednesday night;
tea, horses fed, and eight drinks ; then sneaked their
horses out of the stable, got on them, and told me to go
to hell for payment. I gave them in charge, and they
were fined £1 each in the morning. I came here a
stranger, and my landlord told me when 1 was taking the
house to wait until the shearers came back and I would
do a good business. I have waited, to my sorrow'.—S.M.,
Quean bey an.
The horizontal and
bowra (bauera) scrubs
are generally supposed
to be the boss tangle
on Tas. West Coast.
The botanical name of
the former is Anndo-
petalum Biylandu/o-
sum and it doesn’t go
back on it. But there
is another sweet plant
known as the “ fagns ”
(fayus ff-unnii), which
doesn’t flourish below
the snow - line. It
sheds its leaves in au-
tumn, and a patch of
it looks like a field of
gold at a distance, but
on closer inspection
gives one the idea of
a gooseberry garden.
Though thornless, it is about eleven de-
grees more caressing than a gooseberry-
bush—and tougher, and thicker, and uglier,
and less useful, and more accursed. You
can’t cut it, unless you can find the main
stem, and you can’t find that under half-
an-hour, with a frightful waste of language.
If you walk over it you lose your boots,
and if you crawl under it you part your
hide. A surveyor told the present scribe
that it took his party of four men and the cook
a long day to cut eight chains through a fagus
gully.

S.O’B. : “On the Palmer River, when
boots ran from £>2 a pair for ‘ cossacks ’ to =£3 10s.
for Wellingtons, leather, tools and grindery were
impossibilities ; so the diggers did not wait for
their boots to wear out, but protected them in
time. They cut out a piece of greenhide that would
overlap the sole and heel of the boot by an inch or
two and pierced it all round with a knife, and
then laced it across the upper and around the heel
with strips of the same material. Cheap, effica-
cious and easy to renew.”

A “little stranger” had arrived at the
blacks’ camp, near a N.S.W. homestead, and of
course the station youngsters were all agog to see
it. Tea-time found the household assembled ; the
children just returned from the camp. “Well,”
said squatter M'-Misery, “did ye see the pican-
niny ? ” “ ’Taint a picanniny at all, father,” said
young Jack, “ it’s a white baby, just as white as
little Willie,” pointing to the youngest of the
family, “and the dead spit of him, too,” Mr.
M'Misery hereupon began to look a bit uncomfort-
able. Mrs. Mac. eyed him hard. “ It’s got Mr.
Brown’s nose and mouth,” chipped in little
Emily (Brown was the tutor). “ Well, you silly,”
said Jack, “ ain’t Mr. Brown just like Willy, and
Willy just like Mr. Brown; so what’s the differ-
ence 1 ” It was now Mr. M‘Misery’s turn to
glare.

Raspberry: “I knew a Tas. bullock-
puncher who went by the name of ‘ Angel Bob.
He could drive a team of 12 bullocks miles on a
bad road and never swear, and seldom speak, yet
was acknowledged the crack ox-persuader of the
district. Bob has joined the other angels now,
and almost the last words he uttered were, ‘ Use
them bullocks gently, boys ! ’ ”

C.H.R. : “Once saw a small tiger snake
protruding from the mouth of a larger one. Both
were alive. The former had tied a single knot in
its middle which proved too much for the cannibal
to swallow. I dragged the little fellow out, when
he immediately crawled away to a neighboring
tussock and untied himself. Then, the entertain-
ment being concluded, I despatched both parties.

THE FISHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
ALL night a noise of leaping fish
Went round the bay,
And up and down the shallow sands
Sang waters at their play.
The mangroves drooped on salty creeks,
And through the dark,
Making a pale patch in the deep,
Gleamed, as it swam, a shark.
In streaks and twists of sudden fire
Among the reeds
The bream went by, and where they passed
The bubbles shone like beads.
All night the full deep drinking-song
Of Nature stirred,
And nought beside, save leaping fish
And some forlorn night-bird.
No lost wind wandered down the hills
To tell of wide,
Wild waterways; on velvet moved
The silky, sucking tide.
Deep down there sloped in shadowy mass
A giant hill,
And midway, mirrored in the tide,
The stars burned large and still.
The fisher, dreaming on the rocks,
Heard Nature say
Strange, secret things that no one hears
Upon the beaten way;
And whisperings and wonder stirred,
And hopes and fears,
And sadness touched his heart, and filled
His eyes with star-stained tears:
And so, thrilled through with joy and love
And sweet distress,
He stood entranced, enchained by her
Full-breasted lovliness.
RODE RIG QUINN.

“SURPLUS."
The term “surplus ” is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate.
The natural tendency of distribution is to diminish
the surplus for the moment; but in the case of a
prosperous company, the decrease is speedily more
than covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, .the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of t.he;pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. Of course it
is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require under any possible circumstances ; and,
when that course is followed, injustice to present
participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they are entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of-
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but. empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by’ The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was .£6,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance —of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896? Not six millions, but upwards of
04 millions sterling I This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
<£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus Thk
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
thiscourse, the Mutual Life of New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,”“ the Co. which does the most
good ? ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excess of
any possible needs of the generations to come.*

Melba and her “ gilded cage,” with the gold and salmon-
pink and lime-green, and the “ wondrous sofas and
fauteuils,” and the photograph signed “ Eddy,” in which
inscription “ you recognise with a sigh the writing of
that gracious prince,” the late Cuffs-and-Collars —with a
vast quantity more of similar gentle slush, amongst which
the only notable point is the eagerness_ with which Melba
grabbed the chance of inferring that it was all very well

A private letter from London, from a lady, contains the
following comments on the first night of “ Peter the
Great,” at the Lyceum : —“ ‘Peter the Great’ is full of
horrors, and quite inartistic. There are bits which have
dramatic promise; but why did Irving go in for such
horrors ? There isn’t much greatness about his Peter. It
is brutally small. He might be some common assassin—
raving, wild-haired, and spidery. Not a touch of the
powerful barbarian looking forward to civilisation.
Russians will be furious. . . . Lady Irving flounced into
a box, and made herself most conspicuous with other
emartl.v-dressed ladies. Ellen Terry’s part is utterly com-
monplace and untrue. She did what she could with it,
but it was low farce all the same. Several ladies fainted
at the horrors, and had to be whisked out. A , whom
we smile at for being so conciliatory, boldly said she had
never seen such bosh, and would keep on repeating it on
the stage afterwards. Mr. L said, ‘ You must own
the acting was worse than the play.’ I hardly heard any-
one who had the audacity to pay compliments, although
there were heaps of Nice people on the stage afterwards.”
Dear Bulletin.— Whatever truth may be in Clement
Scott’s opinion of stage-morals, it is certain that the world
has more leniency’for a slip from virtue in the case of a
great artist. If a great singer or actress should have a
little accident, she becomes merely the more interesting
and popular, whereas a poor little devil of a chorus-girl,
bought with a few trinkets, would earn only the foulest
of reputa'ions. A diva’s intrigue with a prince or a mil-
lionaire is glozed over as an amiable aberration of genius.
We allow privileges to the exceptional that we deny to
the commonplace, just as we call the drunken gentleman
a jolly fellow, and the drunken laborer a degraded sot.
But. there is one comfort for the cynic. We all go the
same road, and, in the end, the impartial worm is the
arbiter and critic. —G.B.
Haresfoot: “The ‘National Biography’ article on
the great Mrs. Siddons, mentions a thing I never heard
of before. In her lifetime, a fencing-master’s wife got hold
of a batch of Mrs. Siddons’s love-letters to the fencing-
master, a man of the most ordinary station, and published
them in a book. The ‘National’ biographist says there
is no thought of charging Mrs. Siddons with anything
more than making a dreadful fool of herself. All the
grand dames of the stage appear to have their weak heel.
Mrs.. Langtry gleefully pitched on a mare’s-nest about
Mary Anderson. If you know anything about a prim
actress, you are sure to know the joint of her armor, a
laughable she-Quixotic affair with some ugly little supe.
or fiddler. This is a stage corollary. The present writer
once was let into the secret of the most charming woman
he ever saw, by her mistake in enclosing, in an envelope
addressed to him, a photo, and letter intended for her
god, by universal consent the ugliest and hardest-natured
little wretch in the whole theatrical profession. What
did this scribe do?—but that is another story.”
John Edwards the younger was a well-known lawyer in
earlier Melbourne, and a very popular man. George
Fawcett (Rowe), who was running the Princess Theatre in
’62, produced a sketch called “ The Waxworks,” in which,
among other notables, he burlesqued Edwards. This riled
the lawyer so that he rushed round behind the scenes.
The curtain was rung down. Fawcett dodged around,
and so led Edwards on to the stage ; then, at a signal, up
went the curtain. There stood Edwards, foaming, while
from the wings Fawcett warbled :
“ John Edwards the younger, a very good sort,
Who defends all the boozers in Collingwood Court."
The end was peace.
During the first rush to Back Creek (Vic.) there came
along a company of theatrical “hadbeens.” The star
was a Miss St. Clair, with a voice that had been buried
and dug up again to serve the new generation. At the
first and only performance, the lady essayed a song com-
mencing with “ 0 Charming May,” but every time she let
this out, with a screech that went right up’ to the ridge-
pole of the tent, a big man brought it down, and sat on
it, with a deep bass “Oh ! my God ! ” Then everybody
rushed out for a drink, and never went back.
It was down South that a certain comedian put up the
record in the matter of pay. He was engaged at seven
pounds a week, and had to utter only one word in the
piece, and that was a name. One night when he came on
the stage his articulation was so tangled that he was
scarcely able to utter the word. Prompt sack. Seven
pounds for one word !
When Antoinette Sterling was in Sydney she gave such
an untraditional rendering of an oratorio aria that city-
organist Wiegand became quite indignant. “You do not
sing ze musique,” he expostulated, whereupon Antoinette
sweetly replied, “ I sing it as the Lord taught me.”
“ Umph 1 ” commented Weigand, with strong nasal
emphasis, “I did not know ze Lord was such a bad
musician. ”
Holdar M. : “It was at a morning rehearsal, several
years ago, and the parts were sustained by a stage-
manager, the village villain, the sweet-souled heroine,
and a scribe on the staff of a small feeble weekly. The
scribe was then more-or-less engaged to the heroine.
The she-actor was being directed how to skilfully avoid
landing the villain one on the ear with a heavy weapon,
but she made a mistake, and, instead of glancing off
the shoulder, the stick landed the footlight-fiend a
tremendous welt on the ear, badly injuring that organ.
The stage-manager’s language was unpublishable, though
perhaps excusable, under the conditions. The paper-man
expostulated about the language aimed at his girl, but his
position was uncertain and his voice unheeded. The
only result was that he ceased to give it a name at
that manager’s expense. As already mentioned, it hap-
pened a considerable time back, but the erstwhile
manager now plays leading parts, and the scribe, having
risen in the world, criticises him severely in a rather
influential paper. Thus does r-revenge come to the man
who knows how to wait.
Wizard Morritt put people in mind of another wizard,
Jacobs, familiar here at Coppin’s Olympic, the Iron Pot,
over 40 years ago. Some time after hia Melbourne appear-
ance Jacobs was showing at Scarborough. He made an
omelette in a gentleman’s hat, singed the hat badly and
covered it with grease, and flatly refused to pay for the
wreok. The gent summoned him in the police-court.
There the dirty hat was produced, and shocked the
Bench. The wizard took it in hand and said there was
nothing' the matter with it, at which a roar of disgust
arose. Then he handed the belltopperback to the Bench,
quite restored. It was only a deep-laid scheme of advt.
Viator: “In Australia one is always encountering
Augustus Glover, as a Triton amid the minnows, with a
cart of Thespis going round for bush education. Looking-
for somewhat else, I dropped to-day in a London news-
paper on his d6but in that city, just upon 30 years ago, as
Col. Briton in the ‘Wonder’ at the Princess’s. At that
time Irving was acting Bob Gassitt, a minor part in
‘ Dearer Than Life,’ with Toole, L. Brough, and Wynd-
ham. He was three years short of ‘ The Bells.’ A Clement
Scott or Archer of the era, asked to pick the comini; man,
would mass a batch of Glovers and Irvings. Frank Cates,
as a fashionable amateur, was just then working up for
his ‘Hamlet,’ in which his subordinates included Beer-
bohm Tree and Herbert Flemming.”
Dear Bulletin, —My faith in your critical acumen is
for ever shattered. You have permitted a correspondent
to ca-11 Henry Lee a “dreary egotist ” ! Granted that his
opening address to an audience smacked of the “ Je suis,”
was it not condonable in a man who, in the course of 20
minutes, lost his identity in twice as many famous per-
sonalities? And granted that there were intervals (none
too long for the perfect effaceinent of each preceding
character), were they not justified by results? Bismarck,
Dickens, Pope Leo, Kipling, identities as asunder as the
poles, pourtrayed by one man ! And the pictures were
photographic. Lee was not a “ lightning-change artist ”
—your paragraphist’s soul does not soar above the variety
halls—he was something more, something vastly more ; an
artist who gave us famous men each “ in his habit, as he
lived,” who brought us in touch with other worlds and
beings of a wider growth. He was practically a failiire,
of course, and a paragraph like that to which I take ex-
ception explains the reason. He didn’t do a “trick”
change, therefore he was “dreary.” He should have
appeared as George Reid-and changed his coat in sight
of the audience.—B.E. [The Bulletin— and, it believes,
the public—thought Mr. Lee very dull and unconvincing
save in a couple of characters. —Ed. B.]
Consider the murdered Terriss as a “ rolling stone.” At
14 joined the navy as a middy ; at 17 came into some
money, left the navy, went to India and started as a tea-
planter ; got tired of this, started home again and was
shipwrecked on the way, all hands suffering great priva-
tions. Next became apprentice to a firm of- engineers,
tired of this quickly, took a super’s billet at the Prince of
Wales’ Theatre, Birmingham, 15s. a week. Drifting to
London was introduced to Mrs. Bancroft, who gave him
a small part. Shortly after went to the Falkland Islands
sheep farming, didn’t like it, started home again, and the
ship was lost in a gale off Gibraltar. After two days’
exposure in an open boat was picked up by a passing
steamer. Next got an engagement from F. B. Chatterton
to play Malcolm Graeme in “ Lady of the Lake ” at Drury
Lane. Went to Kentucky, U.S., as a horse-breeder, soon
dropped it, returned to England in ’72, took the stage-
name of William Terriss, and found his cue at last. Born
’49, name William Lewin, father a barrister, mother a
niece of Grote, the historian.
Rickards’ panto, at Melb. Opera House is advertised as
containing local allusions jerked in at great expense by
two Australian pressmen. The present writer, after a
close and careful analysis, has discovered one allusion
that may claim to be quite local. That is in reference to
a mace that was lost somewhere in early Victorian his-
tory—half an allusion per man. Is the topical allusion
business paid for by piece or the job ?
Haresfoot: “Clement Scott and Punch Burnand are
Roman Catholics. Years ago, they both wrote bitterly
against the moral condition of the London stage.”
The gentle Jap. has no flies on him. When a theatre-
manager fails to pay his co., the Chrysanthemum Govern-
ment locks up the theatre till the co. is paid.
AT THE CHURCH CONCERT.
Last night we saw' the chairman rise,
With beaming face and pleasant,
He said, “I must apologise
For some who are not present 1 ”
The audience noted not his wink—
They let that pass unheeded—
’Twas not “ the absent ones,” I think,
For whom excuse was needed.
Harsh noise from rough, uncultured throats
Is apt to sadly try one—
They grunted all their low'er notes,
And hooted every high one.
Oh, did you hear them try to sing,
And watch them stand and stare there !
You’d swear excuses he should bring
For vocalists—who were there!

PRAYERS FOR RAIN.
In many of the'churches in the city and
suburbs yesterday was, at the request of the
Bishop of Melbourne and the Council of Ibe
Churches, observed as a day of prayer for
rair., and also for God’s guidance and grace
throughout the sufferings and difficulties
caused from the recent drought and bush
-fires. In somn instances also allusion was
made in the sermons to the distressing
events of the past few weeks.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Q. Labor-leader Glassey: “ The Nelson
men are all bunglers. Our own side is equally
capable." !!!
“ God hath made man upright ; but they
have sought out many Conventions.”—“Ecclesi-
astes "up to date.
Dengue-fever came to boss Tozer’s assist-
tance when he didn’t want to meet Tomjoe Byrnes
at a Q. Cabinet meeting.
The usual burst-up has occurred on the
Vic. Labor paper, the Tocsin, resulting in the se-
cession of C. M. Prendergast, ex-Labor M.P.,
from the managership.
Purves, Q.C., when fighting for Orangeism
at Brunswick (Vic.): “No true Irishman can be
a true Australian. A true Australian is a patriot
of the Empire, and an Irishman is not.”
Andrew Petrie, M.P., was chosen leader
of the latest Q. Protectionist party. But there’s
really nothing in the gentleman but sleepiness and
good humor, and it seems likely that he will be
superseded.
Attorney-General Isaacs once told Gov.
Brassey that when Peacock ceased to laugh the
Turner Govt, would die, and people now begin to
notice that the Chief Secretary’s cacchinations are
less frequent than hitherto.
“Wully” Affleck, M.P., the dreariest of
right-thinking bores, is described on leaflets cir-
culating out Yass way as the only honest politi-
cian in N.S.W. Parliament. Political honesty
will, at this rate, soon figure as a repulsive vice.
Brisbane (Q.) Worker has taken Drake,
M.P., to its brotherly bosom again. The Worker
crowd has at last realised that half-a-loaf is better
than no bread, and will try and work with the
Opposition as far as the fiery backblockers will
permit.
Q. Labor-leader Glassey : “I have worked
in season and out of season to place the Labor-
party in a position to take over the Government
of the country.” Excellent sentiment, but
“Scotch navigation “ main strength and
awkwardness. ”
Sydney and suburbs are supposed to have
had a population at 31st Dec. of 410,300—about
300 more than last year. The N.S.W. metropolis
reached its biggest population (423,000) at the be-
ginning of ’95. Then it dropped in one year to
408,500, and it is making good the loss very slowly.
Mat Reid, ex-M.P., who will probably
knock out Tom Finney at next Toowong (Q.)
Parliamentary election, has been converted
to the idea of a combined Opposition in t”he
Assembly. Reid is the man who publicly told the
late ex-Chief Justice Lilley that he could not run
as a straight democratic Oppositionist, but must
either join the Govt, or the Labor-party.
So Wrixon and sich complain that people
won’t settle on the land. Won’t they? Just
give them the chance. Every time Q. Govt,
throws open.forest lands for settlement the coun-
try is simply-rushed by intending settlers from all
parts of Eastern Australia. For every successful
applicant there are often nine or ten disappointed
ones. Settle on the land ! Why, they’ll eat one
another to get it.
LEADERS ARE BORN, NOT MADE.
Q. Attorney-General Tom joe Byrnes may
find himself in a tight place at next general elec-
tion. It is pretty certain that Arthur Morgan,
ex-member for Warwick, is thirsting to re-enter
political life. It is equally certain that Byrnes’
seat will be his battle-ground. Groom and Morgan
—ex-Speaker and ex-Chairman of Committees—
control the only papers on the Downs that have
any influence -Toowoomba Chronicle and War-
wick Argus— and they are making a frantic effort
to resuscitate the moribund Liberal-party.
Discovered by Rockhampton (Q.) Bulle-
tin, t’other day, that the Colonial Boundaries Act
(Imperial) of ’95 prohibits the alteration of the
boundaries of any Australasian province without-
the consent of that province. This knocks the
Central and North Q. Separation movements back
by three years. The only way out is to send sur-
reptitiously Separation candidates to South Q.,
carry the constituencies (if they can !), and then
get a Separation resolution adopted by Parlia-
ment. Even then the Council would fling outany
Separatist resolution or bill.
Two of Brisbane Worker’s severest critics
and candid friends were, it is said, disappointed
applicants for A.L.F. billets—Hoolan for the
editorship of the official organ, and Wallace Nel-
son for the position of bush lecturer and organiser.
Under Hoolan the Worker would probably have
become the laughing-stock of journalistic Aus-
tralia ; and Nelson, who now edits a smart paper,
with-the indispensable assistance of a large scis-
sors, believes—if ho really believes anything—in
political rather than industrial organisation. Both
would have been the wrong men in the right
places.
The Federal Convention rejected with
scorn the proposal of Premier Turner that each
State of the Commonwealth should be allowed to
encourage its own industries by granting bounties.
So if any State wants to encourage, say, its own
coal industry, instead of offering a bonus of so
much a ton, it will have to do it the other way by
carrying the coal by rail at so much a ton less than
the present charge.
According to Sydney Catholic Press,
Cardinal Kopp wired to William the Goat that,
“The voyage of your illustrious brother to the. far
East for the protection of the Cross is consecrated
by the sinoerest blessings.” '“Consecrated Per-
son !” Consecrated Voyage ! Consecrated
Press! And “ Protection of the Cross!” As if
the “illustrious” young skite didn’t go simply
to “ Kopp ” the Land of Chow !
Leaders of Protection deriving revenue
from the Australian Star should be regularly
asked during the ensuing parliamentary cam-
paign why it is that their organ, perhaps more
than any other newspaper in Australia, lives on
journalistic and literary matter scissored out of
English and American papers. Why should Pro-
tectionists pocket 8 per cent, mainly by excluding
Australian literary work and rifling foreign ex-
changes ?
Liberty, the organ of the N.S.W. National
Ass, brays that the impending effort to reform
N.S.W. Leg. Council emanates, not from Premier
Reid and his party, but from the Labor-Socialist
corner. Judging by the Toryism of Jollyanfat’s
25-year-nominee proposal, it must, have emanated
from the Ass itself. Any Labor-member found
believing in such a reform should be prosecuted by
his constituents for obtaining .£3OO a year by
false-pretences.
It takes the unpatriotic Australian a long
time to get over his prejudice against everything
that is locally made. In Victoria a very large
proportion of the boots now sold as French and
English and American are of local manufacture,
but the fact is concealed to pander to the Austra-
lian’s yearning after imports. The authorities are
beginning to jump on this practice, but, even if it
is jumped upon with feet like two gum-trees, it
will probably flourish for a long while yet.
The Nineteenth Century re Chinese :
Wearing as they do about six times as much clothing
as natives of India, and dressing their dead in several
layers of suits, both living and dead they should prove
admirable customers for our principal industry.
The soaring ambition of Bull the Commercial !
Even the unclean corpse of the malodorous Chow
is not safe from his enterprise. He’s going to
fight the Russ over the right to stick up on his
sign-board : “ Clothier-in-chief to the Oriental
Cadaver! ”
The Turner Ministry is the first Vic. Go-
vernment whose members have divided the money
appropriated for Ministers’salaries equally amongst
them. Turner himself is the only man in the
team who gets more than any other Minister. He
draws .£1200; all the others get .£lOOO each.
Usually the Attorney-General and one or two
others get bigger sums than the “junior Minis-
ters.” Possibly the equality of screw is the real
cause of the singular harmony that has prevailed
in the Turner Cabinet.
If the Vic. Government can effectively
irrigate the mallee country and make it decently
habitable, there is lots of room in front of it for a
big experiment. For instance, the county of
Weeab, in North-western Vic., spreads over 4004
square miles. In ISBI the population of Weeah
county numbered 4, and in 1891 it had increased
to 14. If there had been one decent-sized dog-
fight across the county border just before the
census was taken, the whole population might
easily have stepped across to see it and left Weeah
county absolutely empty. r l here isn’t a pub. and
often not a single boiled shirt in all Weeah county.
The next worst ease is Millevva county, of 3572
square miles - population, IG7 in 1881, and 172
in 1891.
Re the suggested withdrawal of free rail-
way-passes from M’sP. on the dissolution of the
Assembly. A N.S.W. Minister explains that he
is opposed to the idea in practice because members
would, if they could not travel free in preparation
for the general-election vote, use tip the whole of
the last session talking in the House to their con-
stituents. In other words, they would convert the
last session into an electioneering campaign, trust-
ing to the facilities offered by the Postal and Govt.
Printing Offices to bring them into intimate con-
tact with their people. The knowledge that they
can run about as they like aft'-r a dissolution saves
the last session of every Parliament from being
openly (even now it is covertly) turned into a
hustings.
KNOWLEDGE IS POWER .
CALLER : " I shouldn't have such a pretty maid
where my husband was about."
HOSTESS: “ Well, you know, I like to see what my
husband is up to.”
The most luckless proviso in the Federal
Bill is that which declares that there can be no
Federation unless N.S. W. comes in. In case of a
deadlock, the other provinces could otherwise
start by themselves, and trust that N.S.VV. and
Queensland would come in later on. As matters
stand, these three dreary provincialists, Reid,
Lyne and M'Millan, settle things by the one
argument—-unless the other provinces give way,
N.S. W. will retire, and over goes the show.
There is going to be a large increase in the
number of N.S. VV. factories next year as an
evidence of the blessings of Freetrade—if the
most vociferous counting can do it. As an ex-
ample of how they do things now an attempt was
made last week to force Sydney Lyceum Theatre to
register as a factory on the familar ground that
“ four or more hands were employed.” Also, the
familiar threat of prosecution if the place wasn’t
registered was dumped down at the front door,
and a huge return was left to be filled up.
Gairns (N.Q.) Post item :
The Kanaka leper, who is still “ isolated ” in the police-
court. yard, is a pitiable object. The Government will
neither release nor confine him, and he spends his time in
solilude in the police-court stables. He has several times
begged the police to shoot him, and put him out of his
misery. The Government seems to be quite callous over
the matter, and nothing seems to impress on them the
necessity of either isolating or releasing him.
But obviously the Ministry is holding the leper in
reserve as Govt, candidate for Cairns at the gene-
ral election, subsequently elevating him to Cabinet
rank with the others. Where can Cairns find a
better representative?
The blessed absence of Brunker has done
a lot to brighten up the third Federal Convention.
There is no more fire or enthusiasm in Brunker
than there is in a directory, yet the poor old man
felt called upon every now and then to give way
to a burst of flowing rhetoric about the national
destiny or something. And the flow of poor
Brunker’s poetry was just the same as a large iron
safe flowing upstairs—it was just as easy and it
bumped just the same way while getting round
the corners. Dear old Brunker was just starting
for the Convention some days ago, but Premier
Reid wired him to stay in Sydney and keep on
governing the country.
During the second last heat-wave at Ade-
laide a valuable collection of Indian birds at the
local Zoo perished to the last fowl from apoplexy,
and a rare Indian monkey did likewise. These
specimens could live in India and couldn’t in Ade-
laide. Yet the white Australian went about and
earned his living as usual. From this it would
seem that, when the dreadful Fatman wants to
import coolies to labor in North Australia on the
ground that the white man can’t stand the climate,
he is moaning through his hat. If the Indian
monkey is anything like a criterion of the heat-
bearing capacity of the Indian brown human,
then the Australian can outlast both of them.
That great tactician, N.S.W. Premier
Reid, is clearly “ the chap to save the country,”
especially when a general election looms large.
He has just wired from Melbourne to papa
Brunker: —
I rejoice that N.S.W. has had such a magnificent fall of
rain, but deeply regret to hear this morning of the heavy
damage done in various parts of the country. Settlers
reduced to absolute want by the floods should be looked
to at once. Arrangements must be made to replace all
necessary bridges destroyed, and to repair any damage
to roads interfering with traffic.
To which Brunker replies, promising best atten-
tion, and gives a copy of Reid’s wire to the
reporters. Reid, P.C. —particularly cute!

The relative
importance to the
casual Australian
cuss of Cricket and
Federation is well
exemplified by the
press prominence
given respectively
to the two subjects. In a recent Bendigo
Advertiser, for instance, while the fourth
“test” gets a top-page 3in. caption and all

proceedings have to be content with about
one-fifth of the space, under an unpretentious
mid-colUmn two-line heading. Well, and what’s
the ‘ ‘ pendent world ” after all but a ball ?
Higgins, of Victoria, wanted a clause
inserted in the Federal Constitution prohibit-
ing any “ interference with the free exercise
of religion,” but a wiser majority rejected
the idea. Considering how many horrors, from
burial-alive and mutilation down to-hard dry
buns and the habit of all drinking out of the
same communion cup, form part of various creeds,
to guarantee absolute freedom to every kind of
piety to rage promiscuously in Australia would be
a very large order.
One of Boss Tozer’s political bombshells pt
his Gympie oration was that Premier Nelson will
stick to his billet till prorogation, and will seek
re-election for the Assembly. This apparently
extinguishes the idea of the retirement of Bully
Palmer from the Council Presidency, and Nelson’s
installation therein. Don’t you believe it! Nelson
will stay on as Premier to the season’s end, because
his seat is pretty safe. But if he found his chances
getting “dingo fever,” then “ up goes the don-
key ’’ —into the Council as often aforesaid.
‘‘ Thrift ” has given place to “ Trust ” as

the man of lard wants anything, or doesn’t want
anything, he whoops for “ Trust ” :

The Victorian Government reckons that it
has suddenly discovered something that has been
staring it in the face for any number of years. It
has dropped to the fact that the Murray at
Tooleybuc is 190 feet above sea-level, and that the
huge depression known as Lake Tyrrell is 50 feet
lower. Therefore, if a channel is cut through the
country—almost entirely plain—between the two
places, the Murray, in flood times, will fill Lake
Tyrrell without any worry or expense, and leave
70 square miles of deep water available for irriga-
tion. The levels, it is alleged, were taken long
ago, but it never having occurred to anybody
that water would as soon flow downhill as not, the
relevance of the facts wasn’t noticed.
Writes E.D. :
Higgins, member for Geelong, is putting in a solid
foundation for his political career as a member of the
Federal Convention. Higgins has shown himself the
most strenuous, most resourceful, and gamest democrat
of them all. He has fought many losing fights, and one
or two utterly hopeless ones, but he saw clearly, where
Turner and other alleged democrats peered feebly
through the smoke, and he struck like a man who had
convictions and honored them, whilst his political chief
doddered round, hobbled by his K.C.M.G.-ship, and clat-
tering his other Imperial tinware. Victorian hopefuls
begin to look anxiously to the Geelongite over the heads
of Turner and Peacock, and even Isaacs. It is our tragedy
that we soon cease to expect much from the men who are
up top within reach of the baubles, the fruit of the Royal
Oak. Strange that sleepy, rich, respectable Geelong
should turn out so many fighting radicals—it must he the
reaction.
