Another Newcastle Ship.
0, landsmen '* lives are guarded,
From governors’ down to tailors’ —
For why! The landsmen make the laws!
But God help the sailors!
Some three months ago the Chilian ship Ata-
cama put into Sydney harbor leaky and unsea-
yicorthy. It is reported that she was sold for
£7OO ; “ repaired ” for £lOOO ; and she was
then sent to Newcastle to load coal. The
Atacamsf is stated to be a wooden vessel of
1572 tons register; and even a soft-wood vessel
can hardly be built under £5 per ton of regis-
tered tonnage : a teak or oak ship may cost
anything up to £2O, or even more. And the
Atacama was sold for seven hundred pounds I
A full-rigged ship for £7OO ! less than 10s.
per ton ! It is true she was, according to
the insurance list, twenty - three years old 1
At Newcastle, a captain and crew were
shipped ; 1778 tons of insured coal were
put aboard; and, on January 29, the vessel
sailed for California. She had not been out
a week when she started leaking. The
captain’s log tells the rest of the story. The
weather was no more than moderate ; the sea
was high, but not very exceptionally high. Yet,
though the pumps were continually going, the
water continually increased. The vessel ap-
parently couldn’t stand the weight of the
cargo ; she seems to have been opening her old
leaks all over; and on February 6, at
540 miles from Sydney, the captain wore ship
and made for port. Doubtless beating against
the westerly winds strained the vessel still
more ; but apparently the captain had to choose
between that and foundering in mid-Pacific. On
Feb. 9 the 23-year-old, £7OO-ship had water
over the coals fore and aft, and captain and
crew took to the boats. Then, and then only,
did they meet with heavy weather. The cap-
tain’s boat, a 24ft. life-boat, was capsized and
all stores lost; but she was righted again, and
on Feb. 16 was picked up by a tug near Sydney,
after the occupants had suffered extraordinarv
privations, and one lad had been drowned. The
other two boats—not life-boats—with 12 men
aboard, have not been heard of up to time of
writing. Probably they never will be heard of.
The Bulletin wants to know what authority,
Marine Board or other, permitted this £7OO
ship to leave Newcastle in such a condition
that she opened up like a sieve in moderate
weather, and—in all probability drowned
two-thirds of her miserable crew ? There
must surely be some authority responsible.
Or, if there was no responsibility—if the Ata-
cama was a perfectly sound ship, and it is sim-
ply the nature of a sound ship to go
down in moderate weather a few days out
from port that point ought to be made clear.
The Bulletin calls upon N.S.VV. Govt, to
move in the matter at once, and to see that no
evidence is suppressed, no facts are hushed-up,
but that the blame for those drowned sailors
if blame there really be—is fixed on the
shoulders of the responsible persons.
For on land and on sea Fate’s arrows fall,
And landsmen have many bewailers.
God ? He is busy watching His sparrows fall—
The devil may take the sailors!
It has just been disclosed that a very far
Northern doctor owns an undertakery.
In windy Wellington (M.L.) you know a
stranger by his hat; it is 200yds. in front of him.

When Grady, the bogus “ guessing ’’-com-
petition -person, came before Sydney Quarter
Sessions Court, the jury promptly “ guessed ” that
Mr. Grady ought to go to gaol. Grady told the
arresting detective that “ between what you get
and what you expect to get there's a wide differ-
ence.”
Edwin Suttor, perhaps the last of the old
Bathurst pioneers, died the other day at Peel,
N.S.W., aged 80 years. His father, George
Suttor, was a free emigrant early in the century,
and settled at Balkham Hills, near Parramatta.
In 1822 he sent his eldest son, then a lad, with a
party to settle on the Plains. The lad was fol-
lowed by his brothers as they grew up. Of them
the recently-deceased Edwin was the youngest.
The “purification” of Victorian suburban
electoral-rolls recently has drawn further attention
to the fact that in these days a prodigious number
of Melbourne men live in their wives’ houses.
The department instructed its inspectors to object
to such men’s names remaining on the “proper-
tied” list; but magistrates have called for the
rate-receipts, and when the latter have been in
the husbands’ names the bench has winked and
dismissed the objection.
Premier Turner, of Victoria, being appar-
ently of sound mind and body, and not drunk,
nor winking at anybody, proposed last week that
the Federal Government should guarantee to pay
each province as much customs revenue as it gets
now—this despite the abolition of interprovincial
duties, and should borrow the money if need be.
Which is probably the first result of David Syme’s
constant girding at Turner because he doesn’t
borrow money and “go it.” Turner is trying to
go it, and doesn’t know how.
The Victorian law authorises divorce on
the ground of habitual drunkenness, but the diffi-
culty of defining what is habitual drunkenness has
led to much trouble. A few days ago, however,
a Bendigo woman asked for separation on the
ground that the old man had only been sober
three days since 1892. And as he wandered into
court just then in a beery and amorous condition,
and wanted to hug his wife right under the eye of
the law, it was decided that he was habitually
drunk within the meaning of the Act.
Norddeutscher Lloyd’s Kaiser Wilhelm der
Grosse, which recently broke the Atlantic record,
is all but one-eighth of a mile long, by 66ft. beam,
and 71ft. high from keel to sun-deck (four-storey
house). Displacement, 20,000 tons; h.p., 28,000,
from 14 boilers holding 2000 tons of water and re-
quiring a daily consumption of 500 tons of coal.
The twin-screws are 23ft. dia., on shafts 6ft. in
circumference. There are 66 : auxiliary engines,
and the full complement of souls would be 2000—
1440 passengers and 460 crew. The water-tight
compartments can be closed in 15sec. ; the pump-
ing-out power is 1,000,000 gallons per hour.
Yet this enormous mass was rushed along, on her
record trip, an average 600 miles per 24 hours.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Ihe Australian Cup, run next Tuesday, is quite
jikely to result in a “ surprise”—Luster, for instance, or
Majestic, or La Carabine, or Manfred. On the ottier
hand, Aurum is likely to win, and of the two probabilities,
a victory for the favorite or a “ turn-up,” The Bulletin
will predict the former. Next to Aurum, the most tempt-
ing chances are Mischief, Amberite, Battalion, Parisienne,
Cravat, and Luster. At time of writing, the Australian
Cup is really a more open event than the Newmarket
Handicap, but only one horse can win it. If Aurum
carries the St. Albans’ money, he will surely be prominent
at the business end of the journey.
* * '* •
Tbe fact that three or four Victorian stables are
in great “form” will arouse a deal of slumbering local
interest in the V.R.C. autumn meeting, which starts next
Saturday (26th). Since The Bulletin said of Resolute,
“ the trouble will he to guess exactly how good he is,”
the big hay horse has won another big stake, and it now
looks as though he ought to win the Newmarket Handi-
cap for Mr. W. R. Wilson. Anyhow, Resolute is a
most attractive place investment. Two Saturdays in
succession he has come with a brilliant run at
the finish of a sprinting race, and his latest win
was a strong Newmarket tip. Resolute got a bad
start in the Oakleigh Plate at Caulfield, but although
the distance was half a furlong less than he will
have to go in the Newmarket, he managed to catch the
leaders in time to win by a length and a half. A remark-
able achievement under the circs. Weight, within
reasonable limits, is not a matter of much moment to an
animal of Resolute’s stamp in a six-furlong race, and if he
keeps his pecker up during the current week—well, what
is to beat him ? The present writer prefers Resolute to
Bundook, The Parisienne, Amiable, Mischief, Olaf, For-
tunio, and Amberite. His old-time fancy for Wait-a-Bit
is not as strong as it was. The Ballarat horse has a ten-
dency to shut up when pinched.
» * « *
E. M. Brennan, lessee of the Hobart Clip
winner, Rosed la, is a “ tall and freckled and sandy” in-
dividual with Celtic cheekbones and lots of luck. He it
was who rooted out the 4130 z. nugget on a Westralian
field, and tramped 121 miles to Southern Cross with the
lump of wealth in his water-bag. He has struck it lucky
on most modern goldfields in the world, and is now off to
Klondyke. His mare paid £25 for 10s. tote-tickets in the
Hobart Cup ; what Brennan thought of her chance is in-
dicated by the fact that he had only eight tickets —£4
on the machine, and no book bets. *

x couple of years earlier.”
* * * *
Followers of W. R. Wilson's horses will be
BOrry to hear that young D. Callanan has left the St.
Albans’ stable. Callanan is one of the best, if not actually
the best, of the light-weight boys.
» * * *
Young St. Albans Bowden, the erstwhile rider
of Progress. &c., now keeps a nice pub. in N. Fitzroy,
Melb., and, like his old rival, Tom Hales, has done well
financially ; also, like him, lie suffers liadly from asthma.
* * * *
Dear B. — Met a man lately who seems to be
thriving on a system of backing horses. He explains :
“ I rely on one favorite netting home each day, and I am
on that favorite. Igo out and back the favorite in the
first, say 10s. at 2 to 1 ; if it wins, I stop ; if it loses, I
double ray bet on the favorite in the next -say £1 at 6 to
4. If if wins, lam still in ; if not, double again on the
next. Very seldom have a day on which no favorite gets
home. Sometimes I don't catch till the last of six races—
10s., £l, £2, £4, 8, 416 at say 6to four : £24 to £l6. I
win £8 10s. on the day.” Looks genuine, too, because he
never offered to “ bite my lug” in return for the revela-
tion. —Sob.
Several other people have also rushed in to
draw The Bulletin s attention to this “ system,” which
seems to be epidemic just now. It is a very old affair,
anyhow. The men who have followed this scheme have
mostly given it best because—(l) Often, after a long run
of bad luck, t hey had to bet so heavily to net square that
they couldn’t get. the money on ; (2) because, after a long
run of bad luck, they had to bet so heavily that it became
worth the bookies' while to stiffen the horse ; (3) because
their system became known, and the bookies simply
refused their money on any terms when the doubling pro-
cess reached a certain point; (4) because, after a long run
of losers, they had to put on so much money to get level
that they shortened the odds against themselves to a
ruinous extent—when a man has to back so heavily that
the favorite goes to, say, 5 to 2 on, then, even if he wins,
he may easily be left a loser ; (5) because the booky isn’t
an automaton. Anyhow, if the system was any good, the
man who lives on it wouldn’t give it away. Even if one
man could live on it, a hundred couldn’t.
Taking the scheme literally, however, there
isn’t, even on the alleged inventor’s own showing, much
in it.. The punter on this system can only have one win
a day at most. Now and then he won’t have one. Say
six races a day. Say he always gets 2tol on the favorite
—which he won’t do. Say that he won on the first,
second, third, fourth, and fifth races on five consecutive
days, and the sixth day he went right through with-
out a win. Also say that he had no expenses,
was never robbed, there was no hitch at all,
his bookie always paid up, and nothing went wrong.
Then, under these reasonably favorable circumstances, he
would have a loss of £l3 10s. to show for a week’s work.
If he won three days on the first race, once on the third,
and once on the fifth, and one day had no win at all, he
would be £l7 10s. to the bad in a week. If he won
once on the first, second, third, fourth, fifth and sixth
races, in six consecutive days, and then had no win on
the seventh day, he would only be in £3. If he won
eight days on the first horse, and four days on
the second, and one day on the third, and
then had one day without a win, he would
have a loss of £l5 to show for a fortnight’s toil. There-
fore, some more weak points in the scheme are : If there
are six races a day, and the punter stops after his win,
he can only have one win per day ; though he may always
have six losses. And his biggest possible win, at a steady
2to 1 average, is £l6 10s ; his biggest possible loss is £3l
10s. So the odds are against him all the time.
* * * *
Horatio Bottomley, the financial genius who
came to grief with the London “ Hansard Union,” and
afterwards made a million sterling by floating gold-mines,
is about to try his luck on the English turf. Bottomley
has bought a racing stud, and if it is in the power of
mortal man to break the betting ring Bottomley intends
to do the deed.
« » * *
Clem Hill’s scores (at time of writing) in first-
class cricket this season : Against England, 200 and 45
for S.A. ; 19, 96, 58, 81, 18S, and 0 in the tests ; 20 and 2
against N.S.W.; 11 and 135 against Vic.; Oand 170 against
N.S.W,; or a total of 1025 runs for 14 innings, average
over 73. Clem’s 21st birthday is March 18th. He is one
of a family of 14.
* * * *
Australian eleven players had great fun with
Howell when he first played in big cricket. They used to
get around him in a circle and ply him with questions
about bees and bee-keeping, all pretending to be deeply
interested ; but the big man has dropped to the game
now. The latest idea is to seek an explanation from
Noble as to how he makes the ball “ curve ” in the air.
The explanation takes something like an hour. Noble"
who has been asked the question several thousand times
now, hasn’t yet tired of demonstrating the process, but
he will drop to it by-and-bye. They call him “ Rats ”
* * * *
South Australia doesn’t seem justified in build-
ing much hope now on its midget, Tommy Drew who
did big things against the Englishmen in their on’enin--
match in Adelaide. In the Sheffield shield match, in Mef-
bourne, he pottered around miserably, and blundered
t oo, in the field. With long Trumble bowling against him’
t he ball rises too high for the little fellow every time. ’
The failure of S.A. men in the second innings of
their match in Melb. gave Yic. the Six ffield shield. They
wanted only 14/ ; and, on a good wicket, they cmld not
make it against Trumble and Roche, both of whom beat
every batsman. Jarvis, who made 31, and so topped
Hill’s score, did fairly after the first 20 minutes ; during
that probationary period he didn’t know where he was, a
dozen times at least. S.A. had the worst of the lock, so
far as wicket is concerned, in l he first innings, no doubt;
but the Victorians’ fine fielding, and Trumble and Roche’s
excellent bowling, fully entitled them to their win.

* * « *
Iredale’s failure in recent first-class cricket helps
to establish a rule that the cricketer who turns news-
paper scribe is doomed as badly as the boxer wh . takes
to play-acting. Ranjitsinhji has lately touched low level
probably through Striving to play cricket and do cricket-
reporting at the same time. Turner also helped to run a
short-lived sporting-sheet ere his downfall became final.
* • * •
Iredale does not go far enough for some people
in stating that he intends to ask the other selectors not
to consider his claims when the team for the final test-
match (this Saturday) is being picked. The querulous
would prefer ad. finite statement that he will not play,
for there is a feeling in some quarters that when the
names appear Iredale’s will be amongst them, although no
reasonable argument can be brought forward why he
should be selected before Mackenzie, whose figures
against the Englishmen are 80, 59, 13 ) and 52. It is an
utter mistake having active players on the selection com-
mittee. Until the crowd got nasty Garrett helped to pick
himself and other “has beens,” with the result that N.S.
Vlales was badly beaten by Victoria. So soon as
Garrett resigned a team was picked which beat
the Englishmen. With the three active players
composing the last-match selectors it is a case of “ You
scratch my back and I’ll scratch vours.” When Darling
was off-color Trott and Iredale stuck to him ; w hen Trott
was off, Darling and Iredale stood his friend ; and now
Ireffale’s turn comes a lot of people feel that the men he
has befriended will not throw him over, and that the
picking of the last test-match team will probably be on
the same lines as the first—“ the three gentlemen present
for a start.” Iredale, on his present form, should not be
in the team, even with Mackenzie out of the way as
Donnan s figures against the Englishmen read 104 29* 41
and 59. • > .
* * * .
Jones staggered Melbourne by making 66 for
S.A. against Vic. He did it, too, by good play. But the
surprise isn’t justified. Jones can, or at least could bat
When he was taken down to Adelaide from Broken Hill
it was almost as much for his batting as his bow ling—he
had made up to that date the record Barrier bi<» r score
It really seems, since he established his bowling'reputa-
tion, thathe has been trying how far he can burlesque
the other branch of the game. His reply probably is that
he wants to keep the eyes of the selectors and the public
riveted on his bowling, and that he isn’t going to risk
black marks by disappointing them as a batsman. Yet
as the bowler has to bat, and as even 1 run is better than
0 any day, the idea (to which, by the wav, more than one
bowler clings) seems pretty rotten. It might be in-
teresting, though, to know how far selectors and the
public are responsible for it.

The English amateur cricketers dodged the
Brisbane (Q.) mob by getting out at Roma-street instead
?*% b KK Central. Quo’ Maclaren : “ VVe were mobbed
(and robbed) once, but—never again ! ” “ Yes, an’ you’ve
been licked three times ! ” yelled a Brisbanite.
A deep growl from Toowoomba (Q.) at Brisbane
because the cricket selection committee at the latter city
practically ignored the Toowoombavians who made the
only pretence of a stand against Stoddart’s team in Nov.
last. Thomas, of Toowoomba, made 85 against the Eng-
lish bowling, and is a cool and rising youngster. But
Brisbane has ignored him and boycotted Toowoomba.
It is maintained by many Q. cricket authorities that, if
the very best men of Brisbane, Ipswich, Toowoomba,
Wide Bay, Rockhampton, and Townsville were secured,
there would be no difficulty in finding a purely Q. team,
fit to face the Stoddart eleven with a reasonable chance
of success.

It was noticed in Melbourne that when stumper
Storer, after bowling, resumed the pads he stooped down
and stripped them off Druee. Whereupon the amateur
Adonis walked off and a brother pro. lent Storer a hand
in re-adjusting the leg guards. That’s quite Hinglish,
don’t you know.
* * * *
He winning the toss, “ Mao (an Irishman)” puts
four reasonable questions : (1) “ Why are the Australians
so jubilant when they win the toss?” (2) “Why were
they so dejected in the first test-match when they lost
the toss?” (3) “Why did they lose that same game? ”
(4) “On what occasions did the Australians win test-
matches when the Englishmen batted first?” The last
question is a reminder that Stoddart’s previous team and
this one have, between them, played nine test matches
up to date, and have not, as a matter of fact, been beaten
when they batted first. They were once beaten when
Stoddart won the toss (at Sydney), but their injudicious
captain sent the Australians to the wickets on that occa-
sion.
• * * *
Harry Trott, questioned as to whether he was
illegally drinking champagne and soda in a hotel t’other
Sunday, explained that he drank something which didn’t
taste stronger than soda straight. Moreover, he almost
succeeded in looking, for the moment, like a cricketer
who generally takes cold tea on Sundays.
* * * *
Giffen’s book, like all other cricket chatter, is
chiefly foolish in its oxer-estimation of the “greatness”
of the game’s notable exponents. First-class players are
so many in number, and so various in their workmanship,
that no thoughtful outsider can fad lo see the littleness of
cricket itself as a science. Luck, and the natural limita-
tions to physical strength and activity in even the
strongest professors, make it practically impossible for
any batsman to achieve a wonderful and long-lived
supremacy' over his fellows. A test-match performer mav
wrinkle his brow and tell us that it “ takes a lifetime to
learn cricket,” but he can’t get over the fact that Clem.
Hill, at 20, is the most successful scorer in Australia,
and that W. G. Grace played for the Gentlemen v. Players
when he was a boy of 17. W.G. has been a foremost
batsman for 30 years, “ learning ” the game all the time ;
yet even this exceptionally able player was on the decline
before he had ceased to be a young man. Cricket is a
boy’s game, with plenty of manly virtue in it. The
simplest way of judging its scientific possibilities is to
study the heads and faces of leading cricket scientists.
How much brain-work is needed to knock up a “ cen
tury ” or accomplish the “ hat-trick?”
* * » »
Jones and M‘Kibbin, and other throwers, Aus-
tralian and English, are fond of quoting a foolish request
by “ chucker” Crossland, re the complaint that he didn’t
bowl tbe ball “ Well, give me permission to throw and
see the difference.” This was no answer. Nobody sug-
gests that Jones, M‘Kibbin, and co. are full and complete
throwers. They start with a bowling action, and go
through a series of more or less misleading arm move-
ments until they get near to the crease. Then they
throw the ball. Spofforth wrote a year ag*o to London
Sporting Life his opinion that M'Kibbin’s delivery of the
ball was a chuck, not an over-arm bowl*. A chuck ham-
pered by the necessity for dissembling preliminaries.

“Five bob a day ” is what an unfortunateMelb.
daily pays a man to do test and international cricket-
matches on the M.C.C. ground.
* * * *
A few figures to keep the “ dailies ” in counten-
ance with their endless cricket statistics, records and
comparisons : During the late test-match the record
speetatorate put up an aggregate of 17,426,921 hand-
claps, drank 3,872,176 drinks of various kinds, and
smoked 156,001 cigars, 1,296,732 pipes of tobacco and
19,272 packets of cigarettes. After the match 1,560,000
people said “What do you think of it?” and 1 559,976
answered “ What’d I tell you !” 200 publicans and 500
other business people said “Damn the cricket-match;”
and two dozen journalists got shilling cricket annuals and
the office back files and wrote a couple of hundred feet of
“ slosh.”
• * » *
The S.A.-N.S.W. match, played at Sydney, re-
sulted in an easy win for S.A. by 295 runs—thanks mainly
to big second-innings scoring by Clem. Hill and Jarvis,
who totted up 170 and 154 respectively off their own little
bats. S.A. went in first, and scored 289; N.S.W. fol-
lowed with 310, and things looked promising ; then S A.
came with a mighty 442, and N.S.W. disconsolately
petered out for 126. In the last innings, Jones, for S.A.,
took five wickets for 62, and Evans four for 61.
* * * m
Arthur M. Cary, the Boston cyclist, now tour-
ing Australia on his way round the world, is pursuing a
method entirely new. He has actually undertaken the
job without making any wager of $20,000 with Asa O.
Thompson, of Syracuse, U.S. ; he is spending no money
in advertising ; and, awfullest shock of all, he is working
at his profession of mechanical draftsman at each place
he touches.
Sigmund Bachmann, the other cycling en-
thusiast, who is whirling round the planet for a wager,
has been telling a Melb. interviewer that he is a member
of the Vienna Aristocratic Cycling Club. What a sense.
of humor, and what cheek Viennese Naicest must have
to brand themselves “aristocratic.” Even the Lady
Mayoress of Dingo Flat would hesitate at that.
* ♦ * m
Of the Yeomans brothers, formerly well-known
asathlet.es, footballers and jockeys, there remain Billy,
the jockey, and Joe, who resides at Bathurst. Fred.,
once a celebrated runner, pole-jumper, step dancer,
rough-rider, footballer, and horse-trainer, is dead. Billy
is now a well-to-do Victorian squatter ; Joe, father of 18
children at time of writing, w*as noted for his feat of running
down on foot an emu which escaped from the police-
barracks at Bathurst. The Yeomans were natives of
Gulgong district.
* * * •
George Carey, in a recent interview, brought to
mind a bit of old Sydney when referring to Ned Bitton’s
free-and-easy on the site of the Clarendon Hotel, Castle-
reagn-street (writes a Bulletin cormp mdent). Avery
sea of gore must have been spilt, in the scanty in the rood
old days when kid boxine-gloves were unknown. The
audience sat at tables and ordered drinks whilst “ Duck”
Ingram, as M.C., would introduce “Two friends, gents;
time,’ and the aspirants would take as big a hiding for
mere fame as the latter-day pug. would want a century
for. And then “ Duck ’ might invite any gent in the
audience who wanted a spar to have a go with one of the
hangers-on or triers-out who were always about the place.
For these bouts a special set of gloves was kept on the
premises, one pair of ordinary chamois-leather, and the
other with an underneath coat of greenhide as hard as a
b ick ; the soft pair, of course, being rung-in on the mug
from the audience. And then the skin and hair would fly
from the “ gent,” until the “ pro.’’ found the butt of his
ear, the “point” being then an undiscovered quantity.
Ah, me! it makes one sigh for the good old days.
* * • *'
It is years since two of the biggest smarties in
—say—Jerusalem had a quiet game of poker with a
moneyed “mug,” whom they took down for £5OO or so.
At the end of the game the mug handed over his cheque,
and, whilst one sharper went out to order some wine, the
innocent asked the other to give him the cards as a
memento of the game. The wine was drunk, and,
after the mug had departed, bookie No. 1 said, “ Give us
the cards, old man ; 111 lock them up.” And then it
flashed upon No. 2 that he had given their victim the
pack of marked cards with which they had taken him
down. The pair, thinking it a plant "which would put
them away at the club, early next morning called upon
the mug, and, after beating about the bush, one pulled
out the cheque, saying “ Look here, old fellow, we had no
wish to take your money—the whole thing was a joke,”
and threw the cheque on the fire. Some wine was drunk,
and, as they were going, one asked, in an offhand sort of
way, for the pack of cards they had played with. “ Well,
I’m sorry to say I can’t give them to you,” was the reply,
“ for when I got home I took an oath that they should be
the last cards I would ever handle, and I burnt them.’’
Fact!

At a recent Yic. trade picnic one of the guests
was known to be so anxious for athletic distinction that,
in a race for a prize “ w-orth a guinea,” the other 14
starters were squared. The hero in question pulled
off the event to his immense delight—changed to deep
disgust when he had formally handed to him a box of
Beecham’s pills—“ worth a guinea.”

Bishop J. R. Selwyn, whose death in Eng-
land was reported last week, retreated from the
field of Melanesia, as his father, Bishop G. A.
Selwyn, did 30 years ago. The connecting link
between the two bishops was the courageous
Bishop Patteson, who was murdered in the Santa
Cruz group.

N.S. Wales, since the establishment of . its
Supreme Court Bench, in 1823, has had five Chief
Justices, or, including Salomons, six, the average
reign (on the basis of five) being 15 years. Forbes
held office 14 years and Stephen 29. Salomons
held the formal appointment just 12 days.
TO THE UP-TO-DATE CRICKET REPORTER.
’Twas an easy thing reporting
In the days when games were fought in
The dim and distant ages
When the first few convicts came ;
Now the largest, weeklies claim
Some three or four big pages
For the progress of the game.
When the provinces are meeting
For a victory or beating,
Where the company most mixed is.
Are they bound to give a list
Of the batsmen long dismissed
And the bowlers of the sixties,
And the way they used to twist?
Are the dead ones, stiff and staring,
To be dug up for comparing,
To be weighed upon a steelyard
With a pen and ink to check,
Till the writer dies a wreck ?
Is that poor old Hariy Hilliard
To be dragged in by the neck?
Must you fix a greedy talon
Upon Cosstick and Frank Allan?
They could bowl and take a wicket,
But, for God’s sake, let them rest
From the Inter’ and the Test,
For they’ve given up'hig cricket,
And they know their business best.
Several score of years ago
There were men could play, I know,
But their bats have long been barren
And their bails have ceased to clinic.
And the thoughts those ancients think
In the matter of M’Laren
Are not worth the waste of ink.
Now, do not think I’m sneering,
For I don’t the least mind hearing
H >w Ranji hit a fourer,
Or the way that four was got;
But I bar this.jolJy rot
Of what Stoddart said to Storer
Or some idiot said to Trott.
Tell us all about the cricket.
And the wearing of the wicket,
And the manner of the playing;
But be sparing, if you please,
Over details such as these :
How you heard Bill Howell saying
That he saw George Gitfen sneeze !
W.H.O.

In view of the directly-conflicting evidence,
the seaworthiness of the unfortunate brig Amy
(whose crew of eight were all lost off Bulli, N.S. W.,
lately) remains a very doubtful question, but a
general impression appears to prevail that, heavy
as the sea was, a reasonably staunch vessel would
have held together much longer. It is, anyhow,
plain that, as regards the antique argosies so largely
bought into the coasting-trade, local marine-sur-
veying wants a lot of turning-over.
There’s a weird pathos about the circum-
stance of the Amy castaways cheering the efforts
that were being made for their rescue. One
moment the faint, hopeful voices out of the
tempest—then sudden perdition, and the voice of
the storm alone. Recalls the cheering of the
out-fighting Calliope by the doomed crew of the
American war-ship at Apia.
Imitation may be the sincerest flattery,
but it is a flattery which many people would, no
doubt, be pleased to dispense with. Among the
sufferers from this form of deception are those who,
while wishing to obtain Scrubb’s Cloudy Am-
monia, which is a most reliable form of cleanser
and water-softener, are supplied with inferior
cbunterfeits, often most deleterious, and liable to
bring on various skin troubles, more easy to ac-
quire than to get rid of. Every retail dealer
seems to sell his own preparation of ammonia, and
so the public often obtain an inferior article, a
form of fraud which can only be avoided by in-
sisting upon Scrubb’s, and no other, while it can
always be obtained from the makers by application
to Messrs Scrubb & co., 32b Southwark Street,
S.E.*

The inquest-jury in the terrible Fry case,
at Adelaide, were sagely of opinion that the '
slayer was “troubled in his mind,” and Sydney
S.M. Herald correspondent gravely remarks that
“it is understood his mind was affected.’’ Great
God of Bathos ! A hitherto tender and affection-
ate husband and father suddenly slaughters wife,
children, and self—and it is understood his mind
was affected. Also, this same overpowering
scribe speaks of the eldest girl's face as being
“covered with a repulsive purple blood.”
George Upward, the athletic Sergeant-at- !
Arms of Vic. Leg. Assembly, lost his brother !
John, the other week, through a railway accident
near Winchelsea. John Upward acted as an in-
spector of line repairs, and when travelling along
the rails on his tricycle he was overtaken by a
ballast train and done to death. Having been
given to understand that the train was away he
had no thought of danger, and when last seen
alive (by the guard) the poor fellow was casually
raising his hat to get a breath of wind on his head.
And then—he left a widow in delicate health and
a young family for whom the Government will
probably do “something.” Deceased, like his
brother George, was a first-class oarsman, and a
fine fellow until that unexpected ballast-train
came to pass. The irony of fate !
“Grip
Re Cohen. —Concurrent with the marked mental de-
velopment observable throughout Jewry, are attention to
diet, rigid observance of domestic regulations, and the
infinite care and solicitude in the up-bringing of children,
common to Hebrew mothers of all grades. Cause and
consequence are here not easily distinguishable. While
not ignoring modern progress, the Chosen are mainly
content to stand by Moses of the Tablets, in his secular
capacity of general level-headedness, even as the Gentiles
of to-day are to leave the Submerged Tenth to be success-
fully dealt with by Salvation Boss Booth—himself perad-
venture a ’verted fin-de-siitcle descendant of Ishmael.
Besides, the dominant brain leverage which enables
Benjamin to hold his end at about 45 from the dead
horizontal, is the indefinite social factor of the fre-
quent and undeniable loveliness of Rebecca—when young.
The latter was, probably, the latent motive of the pro-
longed detention of Israel in Egypt, the Coptic subtlety
of the Pharaohs finding the 6bject justified the means.
During the present century, in northern and eastern
Europe, there has been, from various causes, Judaic
deterioration. Brighter climate and more genial sur-
roundings are needed as in, say, Australia, where the
Tribes flourish like Moreton Bay fig-trees. If the con-
servative jealousy among Jews in English-speaking
countries as regards intermarriage with the tribe of Bull
could be modified, good all-round results might be ex-
pected. The cross of the alleged Anglo-Saxon (Celt,
Teuton, Scandinavian, and other remnants of Japheth)
with about the purest of Semitic races, might prove a
temporary substitute for Zionism. For, though the
average Briton, with his übiquitous kith and kin, openly
reviles and affects to despise the Jew, he secretly adores
and would very much like to make love to the Jewess—
when young. Moreover, Bull, with his partners, Solo-
mon, Levy, M’Moses and Co., finds the latter like the
number nine, even when disintegrated turning up in some
other form or combination. But, severally or jointly,
they don’t favor a hurried exodus to Palestine or any-
where, and are not taking any Zion stock at present.

Deep growlings greeted the decision of
Vic. police authorities on the case of the prisoner
who, the other day, escaped from the detective-
office. A sergeant and a constable were impli-
cated—one quite as seriously as the other—but the
subordinate only has been punished, by being
transfered back to uniform. Favoritism is declared
to be the explanation.
E. A. : The Fatman who talks so eloquently
about the honesty, industry, and manly independ-
ence of the Chow will possibly be a little broken-up
by a report just issued by Ballarat Benevolent
Society, According to this document, the Mongol
is the hardest beggar, impostor and mendicant on
the society’s books. In the large and populous
district which the society deals with there are 537
recipients of charity, and of these 190 are Chinese !
As the yellow man is only about one-twentieth of
the population, the society considers this rather
rough, and has thoughts of knocking the whole
generation of Ah Cat off the relief list in one act.
[This is something quite new to The Bulletin.]
Melb. Tocsin has started on the warpath
against local brothels, and is fossicking out and
publishing the full names, addresses, &c., of the
respectable landlords thereof. As some of them
are church-officers the exposure promises exciting
results. Meanwhile, the police have instructions
to clear the bagnios out of the main streets, and
drive them into the slums, so that respectable
Christianity won’t be shocked by the spectacle of
the elderly and somewhat decayed Tottie cooling
herself on the edge of the pavement on heat-wave
nights.
Dr. Cockburn (S.A.) at the Federal Con-
vention :—“lt could not be held that the Al-
mighty belonged to any particular religion.”
The cocksure Cockburn ! What does he know
about the private religious views of his Maker,
anyhow? The fact is that, judging by the state-
ments of theologians themselves—from the red-
hatted Cardinal and the Bishop in lawn sleeves
down to the humblest rusty-coated itinerant
expounder of the “Gawspel ” —there are as many
Almighties as there are creeds, or even denomina-
tions. And each Almighty appears to look upon
all the others as Impostors.
Melb. Leader tells a little story of the
Gippsland bush fires:
One family was found in the heart of the black desola-
tion living in a humpy hastily constructed of charred logs
and a few sheets of burnt iron. There were a husband
and wife and two children ; their clothes were the scanti-
est, and they had nothing else that the visitors could
discover. The mission of the latter was to help the most
necessitous amongst the victims of- the fires. “Can we do
anything for you, B ?” asked the authority. B
looked about him thoughtfully. “ Dunno as there is,” he
answered. “ But, man,” continued the astounded visitor,
“you’ll surely accept some assistance in your ex-
tremity.” Then B remembered, and said brightly :
“ Well, yes, there is somethin’ I want, and if you got sich
a thing as a porous plaster for the missus I’d be takin’ it
kindly.”
London possesses a magistrate, one
Francis, who habitually refuses to fine or im-
prison wife-beaters, his reason being that wife-
beating is one of the institutions of the lower-
class Bull, and is merely a habit, and the woman
who gets married in the East End must regard
her regular black eye as part of the contract.
And wife-beating is so far an institution in the
land of Bull that magistrate Francis still remains
on the bench. Recently a dweller in grimy
eastern London, feeling that he couldn’t wallop
his wife sufficiently to do justice to his feelings,
kerosened her and set her on fire. Francis dis-
missed the case and the fried female as a mere
“piece of mischief,” and even after that he is still
on the bench. The right of the Briton to bang his
“missus” has been always recognised.more or
less, but his right to set her on fire is something
new.
Suffield is still in his living grave, and the
N.S.W. Justice Department is jumping on him
with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N.S.W.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
in justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with
this monstrous wrong has just been published.
Post-free from The Bulletin office for 7d. in
stamps.
Curious feature about the penny George-
street ’bus proposal is that it was engineered by a
leading Sydney undertaker.
Brisbane (Q.) Echo has a fine and large
article on “ The Far East,” in which the Echo
claims to own the British navy, or something like
that. Witness :
Germany, France, and Russia . . . thought to sup-
plant our power, and usurp our influence in Eastern seas.
... In case of war, the more foreign war-ships that are
away from their home-stations the better for us, because,
although we give them any amount of latitude in time of
peace . . . Thus do we always keep tally with our foes.
(“ Rule Britannia” comes in here.) In fact, we intend to
hold our own, and as soon as we are more powerful in the
East —in a few weeks’ time—than the combined navies,
they will be quickly told to back down or be blown out of
the water. Requiescat in pace.
Bull, Buller, Bullest!
“ Ranji ” Hutton, in Sydney Daily Tele-
graph of 18th inst., talks “ through his hat ” on
the subject of the volunteer movement in N.S.
Wales, and endeavors to show how inferior the
volunteers are to the militia in dear old England.
He states that the volunteer corps are a “ broken
reed to trust to, ” and describes them as “ worse
than useless.” The general has evidently forgotten
certain statements made by him out here?, when,
in the presence of the writer and others, he clearly
and emphatically stated as his opinion that “ a
volunteer force was an ideal one for Australia,”
and that “ he was in favor of reducing the allow-
ance of the partially-paid forces so as to make
them more purely volunteer,” and this he was
afterwards instrumental in doing. Why this
sudden change ? Socially, the volunteers in Eng-
land are vastly superior to the militiamen, who
are mostly the scum of the cities, and enlist for
the little pay attached.
Pattisons, Limited, of 16 O’Connell-street,
Sydney, distillers and whisky merchants, and
dealers in all the miscellaneous drinks that gladden
the soul of man (and he generally needs it), desire
to call special attention to their Morning Dew
whisky. All their liquors are above par, but they
draw attention to the Morning Dew as a fair
sample of how far above it they are. It is a soft,
persuasive liquor, and the man who drinks it over-
night awakes bright and fresh in the morning
from a dream in which he was married to 16
houris in Paradise and had seized the streets of
pearl for arrears of rent. Morning Dew whisky
is, in a metaphorical way, food, nectar, purple
and fine linen, a mansion by the harbor, peace,
happiness, poetry, fair women, and the ownership
of the earth generally. And the man who isn’t
satisfied with that lot can buy more Morning Dew
whisky and have the solar system as well.*
Messrs. E. J. and D. Curran, of 112 King-
street, Sydney, and Exchange Buildings, Bathurst,
now measure their customers by electricity, the
process being the invention of Mr. E. J. Curran.
The results are exceptional accuracy of fit, a great
saving of time, and much less pawing of the cus-
tomer. Ladies’ costumes are one of the firm’s
specialities, and the advantages of a lady not
having to be needlessly pawed by a complete
stranger are obvious. The electric dodge gets
there so accurately that the wearisome fitting pro-
cess can, in most cases, be entirely dispensed with.*
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

The Perth (W.A.) fire-
-I,ents have been until latel_
V small hose and reel was trotted out behind a
ab when required. On one occasion a resident,,
n discovering a fire, rushed to the station, but,
ound that the person in charge of the reel was
absent, so he ran out the apparatus himself. Arid
when the fire was doused he was gently taken into
custody for “ illegally' using the reel!
Westralia had, down to the beginning of
this year, a system under which if you—that is to
-’ay, your enemy—were sentenced to, say, 25 years’
imprisonment, he could get a ticket-of-leave at the
end of 7\ years, provided he were a first offender;
Now, he will have to serve at least 14 years and
5 months. The idea is that the Judges, in deter-
mining the sentence, now take into eonsideratiori
the previous conduct of the prisoner. Further,
"he change, though announced only towards the
middle of January, is to take effect from Decem-
ber 1 last, so that Anderson’s case may come under
it. Which is right enough, provided the Judge
didn’t have the old system in his eye when he
sentenced the young brute. And also, by the
way, it is probably right enough if he did ; for 7 k
years, and a ticket-of-leave at the end of it, would
hardly meet the case, anyhow.
Dear B.—Justice for black and white, if
you will, but give us even-handed justice, please.
Over here, young Anderson, whose case you com-
mented on, got penal servitude for life for thrash-
ing niggers to death. Over here, also, O’Brien,
recently convicted of brutally murdering his wife
and cutting her up into convenient-sized parcels for
removal—seven years hard labor. Also, “Cap-
tain,” aboriginal, convicted of murdering four
I white men, for no cause, and two blacks who
Pried to catch him, five years. How’s that for
Lady with the Scales ?—Goev, Marble Bar,
W.A.

The new Westralian Attorney-General has
been tumbled into a hole. Somebody was charged
at Geraldton with larceny from a convent school,
and it was believed that a certain Sister could
alone give evidence necessary for a conviction.
But Father Strong declared that the“ Sister in
question should only be dragged to court Over his
dead body. The case was adjourned ; and then
the P.M. got this wire :
Be paltry Stealing case. Sister Theresa’s evidence sub-
mitted to us, and cannot he material or have any value-
We suggest dispensing with her attendance. ' Kindlv
adopt our suggestion. Horgan and Pennefather.
Attorney-General Pennefather was, luckily for his
own reputation, away from Perth at the time;
but, if the wire wasn’t bogus (and that is not
suggested), the sooner he gives his private office »
lesson in discretion the better.
Rev. William Fulton, the Church of Eng
land clergyman who is to participate with Fsthet
Harrold and others in the N.S.W. ’9Bcelebrations,
enjoyed the singular destiny of returning volun-
tarily to the land of his exile. He was a Water-
ford man and one of the first fruits of the Eftim- ?
conspiracy which flowered into the Irish Rebellion,
and, on conviction, he was despatched in irons to
Botany Bay. _ When the Rebellion was stamped
out Fulton, with others, received a royal pardon,
and returned to the old land and the old duties.
But Marsden, the parson blacksmith who scourged
the convicts into hate and fear of all religion, had
contracted a friendship for Fulton, and offered
him, the position of Colonial Chaplain at what
was then a good salary and great expectations.
This offer Fulton accepted, and came back, a free
man, to the land in which he had been a convict.
He worked in Sydney for years and again returned
to Ireland—this time with a hatful of money.

“You never know a woman until your
children have quarrelled with hers, or a man until
you have owed him money.”
A belated digger dropped in upon the city
of Bendigo, the other day, and proceeded without
interruption to put down a shaft in Mitchell-
-Btreet. He rigged up a three-legged windlass of
the old sort, and started to send up pipeclay.
Apparently a pillar of ground had been left un-
worked from the lively “ Fifties.”

The Case for the Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Dear Miss Welsh—how well she understands
the nature of men ! ”
The speaker was an elderly spinster, whose
locks were of a golden brown.
The room—Miss Welsh’B room, in Adelaide-
street, Potts Point —was crowded. Many women
were there, and here or there a man or two, or
what passed for a man in the assemblage. There
were exceptions. Sir John Blake, D.C. and
F.R.S.C., &c., was one of them ; and the massive
head, with its bushy eyebrows and crown of
silvered-hair, was noticeable when contrasted with
the gathering of pale curates, a canon, an arch-
deacon or two, and a few members of that class of
weedy men below the humble standard required
for the British army (5.3£, isn’t it?), which
strongly, though in a thin shrill voice, advocates
justice to women.
The occasion was a welcome to Lady Blake —
one of ’the great English leaders of the movement
in favor of the franchise for women. The wel-
come was given at Miss Welsh’s house —very pro-
perly. As a leader of the local forces, Miss Welsh
was acknowledged to be chief. She had sum-
moned around her the sympathisers with her
movement, male and female —those who, like her-
self, knew man as he is. Erom that point of
view, men not members of the female circle don’t
know him. ...
Let us admit the larger knowledge of the virgin
female. She knows that man has no other inte-
rest in life than the seduction of virtue. He
doesn’t care about money, professional success, or
anything else; even scandal and the threat of
social ruin will not deter him. He wants to
destroy virtue ; no other pursuit has any interest
for him. He laughs at history. Helen would
be unattractive because she had given way
to Paris. Cleopatra would be a waste of time
because —well —because, when she met Antony,
Bhe could not pretend to be the one thing which
men want. For men, so the sapient female
reasons, have but one desire, and that is to destroy
virtue wherever they find it.
But we are getting too far away from our lambs.
Revenons 1
We will not try to catalogue the company—
that always is tedious, and to one who has at-
tended a meeting of this kind unnecessary. Arch-
deacon Scribe was there, a few clergymen aspiring
to gaiters, and some of the laymen and laywomen
who are going to make the world a heaven indis-
tinguishable from a Presbyterian Sunday. Amongst
them Sir John Blake seemed to be somewhat out
of place. He was a poor thing, after all—merely
one of the leading scientific lights of the day,
knighted and likely to be made a baronet fpr
original work—but important in the drawing room
of Miss Welsh only as the husband of Lady Blake
—of Lady Blake, the great advocate of the female
suffrage movement in England, whom, as a visitor
to Australia, all these ladies and gentlemen were
met together in Miss Welsh’s drawing room to
honor.
It was in the midst of a pause in the general
conversation that Miss Allopecia Nunn made her
remark anent the knowledge of men displayed by
Miss Welsh. The remark was audible everywhere
in the room—perhaps to the satisfaction of the
speaker. The company was interested ; Miss
Welsh preened her social feathers and looked
round with the air of one who deprecates expected
general approval; Sir John gave a little cough
which might have indicated either approval or a
dissent from the proposition; and then a kind of
bomb fell into the assemblage.
“ I don’t think,” Lady Blake said in a quiet
voice, “ that we understand the nature of men—
any of us. I am not sure whether men under-
stand it themselves. We can observe certain
facts.” There was a humorous twinkle in her eye
as she fixed it upon her husband seldom seen in
the eyes of women, as she added : “ man is
by nature polygamous. I am not sure that we
know any other fact about him, with certainty.”
There was a sort of hesitating applause—of the
kind which is offered when people feel sure that
they ought to applaud the speaker, but are doubt-
ful about the sentiment. Sir John gazed at the
ceiling with a curious intentness which had
nothing to do with his interest in the ceiling.
“ What we have to do,” Lady Blake added,
addressing herself to a strained and interesting
silence, “ is to check this natural tendency in him
as far as we can.” There was a murmur of dis-
tinct approval. “That,” she said “is one of the
principal reasons why we mean to get the fran-
chise.”
The murmur grew louder, and Miss Welsh half
rose, as if she would say something ; but she sat
down and led a further chorus of subdued ap-
plause.
“Yes,” Lady Blake said, “we must get the
franchise. We want to be able to control our own
sex. Man must be protected.”
There was a silence.
“ It is time that he should be looked after,” she
added. “ He has been the victim of women by
far too long.”
Miss Welsh (who understood him) gave a little
gasp. Sir John Blake took the ceiling into his
confidence, as it were, and smiled at it.
“ Miss Welsh,” Lady Blake continued, “ has
asked me to say a few words to you, and I will
try to accede to her request. I want ypu to think
of man as he is. By nature, I am afraid that he
is what we call immoral (hear, hear !) and it is our
duty to help him to be better. (Hear, hear !)
Now, as a child, we women have charge of him ;
and I think that he is moral enough. (Hear,
hear.) But he grows up, and then —other women,
not we, have charge of him, and he becomes im-
moral. (Hear, hear.) Observe, he starts well,
and then falls away. Why ? ”
Nobody offered an answer to this question.
Lady Blake was left to deal with it. “I think,”
she said, “that human nature is very much the
same as any other nature.” Archdeacon Scribe
sighed deeply and audibly. Everybody looked at
him; and he felt that he had scored a point on
behalf of original sin and Mr. Scribe.
“ We see that,” Lady Blake continued, “ in the
Old Testament. Like a great many other animals,
man has a tendency towards polygamy. David
had it, and so had Solomon, not to mention several
of the patriarchs.”
“ That,” said the archdeacon, determined to
score again, “was the old dispensation.”
“Like the ten commandments,” the old lady
continued. “ I don’t see how you can throw away
the moral teaching of the Old Testament without
throwing away the ten commandments too. David
and Solomon came after Moses, and yet I don’t
know that their breaches of the seventh command-
ment came in for any very special condemnation,
except in particular cases.”
Her audience shivered; and the archdeacon
looked round in a dignified way—perhaps for his
hat. He met only the eye of Sir John Blake,
which had the expression which arises from total
want of expression. “ The conduct of Solomon,”
she continued, “ seems to have been regarded with
indifference, and, up to a certain point, even with
approval.”
There was a stir amongst the clerical and some
of the other members of Miss Welsh’s assembly.
It was something like the unquiet breeze which
seems to pick out certain particular trees in a
forest; but storm was averted because Lady Blake
altered the direction of her remarks —hardly, it is
to be feared, out of respect for her audience alone.
“ I said,” Lady Blake went on, “ that we don’t
understand the nature of man; but that we
observe certain facts. Also, I remarked that he
is good up to a certain point, and then appears to
fall away. Now in order to assist him towards
the good, we ought to consider for a moment why,
having started well, he runs towards evil —in other
words, we ought to glance at the history of the
ordinary male individual, and to see if the causes
tending towards deterioration can be minimised
or even averted.
“ Well now, plainly, what is that history ? ” The
question was oratorical perhaps—something like
those which so often are put from the pulpit or
the platform. Of course nobody tried to answer it.
“ I have given some attention to this matter,”
Lady Blake said, “ and, as I have been asked to
talk to you, I am going to talk plainly. Many of
you are mothers ” The phrase again was a
platform phrase ; as Lady Blake perhaps realised
in the face of a multitudinous flat-chested contra-
diction. She did her best to gloss over the slip.
“ God —or what with many of us stands for
God —has not seen fit to give me a child ; and
perhaps I speak ignorantly and even presumptu-
ously ; but if Providence had seen fit to allow me
to be a mother I believe that I should have
thought it my duty to enquire what my children
were likely to learn at the schools to which I
might choose to send them.”
A ruddy-faced curate —a sort of glorified public-
school-boy, suggestive of Harrow —said “ Heah,
heah ! ” and everybody stared at him until he
blushed dreadfully. He thought that he had
reached the pinnacle of human misery. He was
wrong ; as are all men who think that women can
separate principles from instances. Lady Blake
treated him tenderly enough ; Miss W elsh deter-
mined to deal with him later on.
“I don’t think,” Lady Blake continued, that
the average man has much chance of being really
moral. He goes to a school where he hears a
great many things which he ought not to hear at
the age at which he hears them. The prurient side
of his imagination is developed, before the moral
and reflective side has had time to grow ; his pas-
sions—unlike those of my own sex—in their
nature are initiative, and, as a consequence,
easily kindled; and, just at the point when
natural instinct and, it may be, curiosity
offer their own temptations, some woman,
years older than himself, gets hold of the
half-grown boy and leads him astray. I don’t
want to enlarge upon this topic too much.
Some of the reverend gentlemen present will
agree with me, no doubt, in thinking that this is
rather ancient history. Even in the far-away
times when the Proverbs were written the woman
is represented as being the tempter of the young
man, watching for him at the ”
“ That story,” Miss Welsh interjected, in dread-
ful tones, “ was written by a man.”
“Precisely,” Lady Blake replied, sweetly.
“ A man, I am afraid, would have the best
opportunity of knowing the truth of it; always
excepting those, like ourselves, who have studied
this difficulty—who have gone through the streets
of great cities and have noted how every step of
young men—innocent young men —is beset by
temptation, offered in the first instance by our own
sex—who also know how the polygamous in-
stinct, of which I have spoken, is systematically
nurtured and encouraged by women, until the
seduced becomes at last the seducer.”
“Miss Welsh,” Canon Buncombe was the
speaker, “ I regret that I am unable to remain
here any longer.”
“Pardon me, sir,” Lady Blake said. “By in-
vitation, I occupy the pulpit at the present
moment. In a church, if you do not agree with
the doctrine, you may leave, of course; but you
do not regard it as consonant with good manners
to address your protest to the congregation.”
“You are justifying immorality, madam.”
“I am defending nature, sir, against nonsense.
The days have come, I am sorry to say, when
nature needs defence. You know the old story of
the man who played with the tiger-cub. He used
to encourage the creature to lick his hand, until
one day it licked the skin off and tasted blood,
and ”
I don’t know what more Lady Blake was about
to say, or what moral she intended to draw. In a
moment, a buzz of conversation seemed to start
all over the room, which drowned the old lady’s
voice completely; and I saw Sir John, whose
opposition to his wife’s views upon the franchise
question was well-known, laughing in a quiet way.
I do not know what it was that amused him.
AYENAR.

Sick Headache.
Sick Headache is usually one of the symptoms
of biliousness, constipation, or other derangements
of the digestive organs. It is accompanied by
nausea, from which it derives its name, and is
E rally more violent in the front part of the
. The appetite is impaired, and there is a
bad taste in the mouth. There is a general want
of energy, and the sufferer is irritable and restless.
These headaches are caused by gases rising from
the digestive organs to the head, crowding the
nerves of the brain, and causing at times sharp
pains behind the eye-balls. Bilk Beans for
Biliousness will be found an excellent remedy, as
they put the digestive organs in good working
arder, enabling them to carry off the waste matter
uf the body.

THE WOE KI NEMAN'S ERIEND.
Round the Clock.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Like a fresh wind blown out of a lightning-
charged cloud his new life fell, blown out
from his old.
’Twas a song, ’twas a prayer, ’twas a sigh !
’Twas a speech, ’twas a drumbeat, a cry !
’Twas a flash of the soul that survives when the
turbulent kingdoms of flesh and blood die !
In the marginal east surged and eddied a tumult
of mountainous billows that rolled, .
Warring, out to the shining blue levels of sea with
faint fringes of white ;on the right
Huddled cities enwreathed with the smoke of
the work of the hands and the tongues and
the brains
Of the builders and killers of peoples; to left lay a
languorous land of delight
Where the senses swoon ; and to rear stood the
houses of Death in a mist of low rains.
Like a fresh wind he broke to the east for a space
and engaged with the eddying froth.
What was tumult of wave to him now,
With the new life alight on his brow,
But a summons to reap what the forces of Nature
to those who command them allow ?
The strong boisterous waves shook the marginal
rocks with their onsets of billowy wrath,
And he met them, now dipping beneath spiral
columns to rise in a spume-trough of mist,
Now clambering over a mountain of foam with
air swollen, pink-tinted with light,
And anon striking into a narrow low gorge where
the fringes of ridged billows kissed,
And at last floating out to the wide level sea,
with long ripples of blue and or white.
Like a fresh wind he turned to the right where
dense cities, smoke-wreathed and smoke-
garmented, lay ;
With a cry on his lips and a hum
In his heart, like the roll of a drum
Afar off, he passed through the thronged main-
ways of thunderous clamor and laborings
dumb;
And beheld the achievements of muscle and brain
at fierce work on the world night and day,
Building up, breaking down, giving life, taking
life ; and he said as he witnessed the strife,
While extending a hand to the lowly of spirit
and staying the violent strong,
“ If the millions of meek would but stand by the
weak they between them would blow a new
life
For the world from the midnight black cloud-
wrack above it of tyranny, madness, and
wrong.”
Like a fresh wind he turned to the left from the
cities that bootlessly struggled for ease;
And his eyes became dim in a light
That was neither of day nor of night,
But a blending of darkness more dark than the
night and of light than the noonday’s more
bright.
His blood pulsed in a slow sleepy rhythm, keeping
time with a slow sleepy pageant of trees,
All whose leaves were in slow sleepy motion ; of
birds with red breasts and blue wings and
white crests
In a slow sleepy motion in air and on bough ;
and a slow sleepy luminous flight
Of the tiny bright life which in infinite sparkles
the plains and the ridges infests ;
Till his senses grew drunk with the wine of the
indolent pageant of rhythmic delight.
With head swimming, eyes dim and feet feeble,he
stumblingly turned his white face to the
rear ;
There were mounds to the left and the right
Which his dizzy and palpitant sight
Recognised as the low-lying houses of Death in
the vaporous mists of wan light.
There, asleep, side by side, lay the friends whose
affection in life had been frozen to fear
By the wind of a vagrant suspicion ! and there,
fast asleep, foot near foot, head near head,
Lay the lovers whose lives were a song! and
asleep, all asleep, the wild spirits of strife
And the lowly of heart. And a cloud fell upon
him, and he, too, was one with the dead,
For the hand of the clock of his wandering
being had traversed the disc of his life !
N.S.W.
E. LOWE.
The Secret of the Crocodiles.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in Borneo. A man, pushing suddenly
through the rustling calamasks, stood in the door-
way of the hut, and Beckley hailed with feeble
gladness the only human being he had seen for six
weeks. “ Come in, my friend, come in ! You are
just in time —thank God ! ”
The stranger came in; a long, hatchet-faced
man, with a scanty red beard, and a squint in the
little grey eyes that peered from under his bat-
tered kika-grass hat. He leant a Winchester
rifle against the door-post. “ What’s the matter
with you?” he asked, up-ending a wicker fish-
trap and sitting down close to the naturalist’s
bunk. “ Goin’ to peg out, eh ? ”
“ Fever’s the matter —swamp-fever ! Been bad
for a week, and to-day I’m too feeble to get as far
as my medicine-chest. There it is in the corner.
Give it a lift this way, will you ? It’s a provi-
dence you happened in upon me like this ! ”
But the “ providence ” was in no hurry. With-
out so much as a glance at the medicine-chest, he
deliberately lighted his quacca-nut pipe and
smoked for about a minute in silence. “Know
me ? ” he asked then.
“ No, I don’t! But, whoever you are, I wish to
heaven you would ”
“Wait a minute; all in good time! You’ve
never seen me before, I know ; but p’r’aps you’ve
heard of a chap called Rixon—Red Rixon, eh ?
Ay, I see you have—and not much good either.
Well, now you know who you’re talkin’ to; what
d’you s’pose I’m goin’ to do? Doctor you up a
bit, eh ? Not much, sonny ! You’re pretty near
passin’ in your checks, by the look of you, and I’m
not goin’ to stir a finger to prevent it. That
knocks you a bit, don’t it ?”—and Mr. Red Rixon
grinned amiably at the astounded sick man.
“ What do you mean?” gasped Beckley. “Why,
» be nothing short of -order , ■
“Not a bit of it! Lettin’ a man die ain’t
murder ! You see it’s this way. I’ve been hearin’
a good deal about you lately, and, whatever other
people may think, I’m not such an almighty flat

as to believe you ve been so long wastin your
time and riskin’ your life in this God-forsaken
hole of a country all for the sake of a few measly
beasts and birds and insecks ! ” and here Mr.
Rixon, looking contemptuously round the speoi-
men-covered walls, spat with great accuracy into
the eye of a tame lemur.
The naturabst gazed at him in stupefaction.
What was it for, then?’’he asked.
“Gold, sonny—gold ! That’s what it’s for.
You see, you’re a tip-top jologist, or whatever they
call it, and so you know the likeliest places to find

Ed. Bulletin. —The suggested derivation
of “larrikin 5 from gypsy or Romany “leary
kinchin —cheeky or precocious youngster—is no
novelty. But the analogous cockney combination
of cheeky’’and “leary ”to make “chickaleary
has not, I think, been noticed. A cockney song
still current is called “ The Ghickaleary Bloke. ”-2
Bhilol. '•
The i 4 true inwardness ” of golf has re-
cently been expounded by one Doctor Louis
Robinson, re whose lucubrations thus San Fran-
cisco Wasp :
To most people golf looks like a bad excuse for killing
tune, and the people engaged in it are chiefly distin-
guished by. wearing “knee-pants” and loudly-checked
suits, in which habiliments they chase a little ball over a
large part of the county in which they reside. One may
see the devotees of the game any Saturday afternoon at
the Presidio. They in no wise strike you as being the
deeply scientific individuals who alone could master a
game such as Dr. Robinson describes. According to this
eminent writer, in football, cricket and almost all popular
athletic sports there is what he calls “ a continual inter-
action of wills and the constant excitement of opposed
endeavors, hence the mental and nervous processes in-
volved are extremely complex and no chance offered for
calm, introspectic analysis as in the great game of golf.”
When one begins to analyse “ the physiological pro-
cesses,” as Dr. Robinson calls them, involved in golf play-
ing, one soon becomes astonished at their complexity.
Not half as much, however, we should judge, as that any
one outside a lunatic asylum would sit down to write such
bosh. We thoroughly agree with Dr. Robinson that it is
remarkable how the game of golf has obtained the foot-
ing it has in the States. It is no mystery, however, why
it has done so. We have, in proportion to our popula-
tion, the finest assortment of snobs in the world, and as
golf is a game that has many pretensions to be ex-
clusive and aristocratic it readily endears itself to the
shining lights of snobdom. Outside of a very small pro-
portion of genuine enthusiasts who learned the game in
their youth in Scotland, it has no genuine admirers in
this country. Seven out of ten of the people who don
golf socks and swing a crooked stick all day over a wind-
swept moor have no further interest in the game than to
be known as belonging to the social set that supports the
golf club. It is a hollow pretence to hang upon the skirts
of the “ 400,” and the man who undertakes to write about
the game in a scientific way in a high-class magazine
should have his head examined by a lunacy commission.
